Bram was already sitting up. Leni could tell from the direction of his breathing — he breathed differently when he was upright, a teacher's posture reasserting itself even in the dark. He reached over Tomas and touched Leni's shoulder.

"Up," he said.

"Why."

"Because the bell rang."

"The bell's not for us. It's for people who want to pray."

"The bell's for everyone. Get up."

Leni opened her eyes. The sleeping quarters were dim — oil lamplight from the corridor, gray monsoon dawn barely reaching the high stone windows. Bodies lay in rows on mats, and the whole room had the intimate strangeness of sleeping near strangers. Across the partition, she could hear Deshi Mera telling Suna something in a voice that suggested Suna was doing it wrong, whatever it was. On the other side, one of the Kondo cousins was trying to fold his blanket while his mother corrected his corners.

She sat up, pulled her braids off her face, and bit the edge of her thumbnail before she caught herself doing it.

Morning prayers were in the courtyard, and by Day 4 Leni knew the cast well enough to count the absences before she was fully awake. Olet stood under the overhang. Fasa stood beside him with her hands folded and her face set in that workmanship expression, like she was checking the joints on a table nobody else could see wobbling. Peyam Mera was already there, small and precise, lips moving with words he somehow seemed to know. Jin Parra waited at the edge, not praying, just standing through the hour, shoulders locked and eyes on the paving stones as if the courtyard cost him less than lying awake beside Melie. Venn Kondo still didn't come. Tila did. Arash had shown up once, the morning before, and not returned. Bram had tried prayers on Day 3 and reported back to Nessa that they were "interesting from an anthropological perspective." He hadn't gone back. The Leos stayed away entirely.

After prayers the whole compound jammed itself onto the cistern steps. The stone was slick, the air smelled of wet moss and metal, and everybody needed water at once. Venn announced that the Kondos needed three jars before anybody else because twelve people drank more than four. Tila didn't argue with him. She just started moving bodies. She put the older children on the steepest stretch and the younger ones at the landing. Filled jars passed hand to hand instead of carried whole. When Suna Mera came up with two empty pots because breakfast was on her, Tila took the first full jar and put it straight into Suna's hands. Even Venn didn't object.

By the time Leni got back to the kitchen overhang with the onion basket, she'd identified the compound's real hierarchy, and it had almost nothing to do with who sounded important.

Day 4's cooking pair was the Meras and the Okoyes. Suna was already at the fire, rice soaking, flatbread dough under her palms, dal thickening in a pot she'd gotten started before the bell. Nessa arrived with Ada on her hip and a stack of bowls she nearly dropped when one of the Kondo boys cut behind her. Suna caught the top bowl without turning.

"Left side," she said. "Those stack easier."

Nessa shifted obediently. "Thanks."

Deshi took up position behind Suna like weather.

"Is that how much salt you're using?"

"Yes."

"For this many people."

"Yes, Deshi."

"Your mother, god rest her, always said salt is cheaper than complaint. But then she was cooking for four."

Suna added more salt. She did it without expression, which Leni respected. As far as Leni could tell, the trick with adults like Deshi wasn't winning. It was not giving them a fight to chew on.

"Your mother's helping," Suna said to Leni as Nessa tried again with the bowls. It wasn't a request so much as a practical redeployment.

Leni sat on an upturned crate near the edge of the overhang, where the rain came down in a silver curtain two feet from her knees, and started peeling onions. The stone under her feet still held yesterday's heat. Smoke from the fire crawled into her hair and eyes. Jin came up from the cistern with another jar braced against his hip, shoulders rounded forward as if he'd agreed long ago to carry whatever anybody handed him. Melie trailed him, leaned against his leg when he set the water down, then wandered back toward the courtyard before he could look at her for too long.

Across the yard, Miro and Enn Tesh were already headed for the latrines with rolled sleeves, not hurrying, not complaining, just going to scrub what needed scrubbing. Fen carried the sloshing bucket behind them, taller than Leni, dark hair in his eyes, dragging the handle low like he wanted the whole shrine to know he hadn't volunteered for character-building filth. He passed the kitchen, caught her looking, and she jerked her gaze down hard enough to nick her thumb with the peeling knife.

It bled fast. She shoved the thumb into her mouth and tasted iron under the onion. Nessa was beside her a second later, Ada still on her hip.

"Careful. Let me see."

"It's fine, Mom."

"Show me."

Leni held up her hand. Nessa peered at the cut as if the knife might've introduced a whole new plague to the shrine.

"Keep it clean," Nessa said. "We don't know what's on these surfaces."

"It's an onion, not a biohazard."

"Leni."

"I'm keeping it clean."

Nessa went back to the bowls. Leni knew the pattern by now: her mother's fear kept moving until it found an object. This morning it had chosen Leni's thumb.

Breakfast happened in a rush because forty-three people waiting for food turned even the quiet families into weather. Plates passed hand to hand. The Kondos took their usual corner and made a wall out of themselves. The Daos sat close enough to the kitchen that Hala could intercept anyone who approached Bett. The Leos made a small careful cluster around Pik. The shrine kept sorting itself into units, then leaking around the edges anyway.

When the last plate was scraped mostly clean, Olet stepped in and assigned labor with the same flat calm he brought to everything.

"The Parras and the Teshes will take the terraced garden this morning," he said. "The weeds have grown through the herb rows. The Daos will clean the main hall. The Okoyes and the Leos will gather firewood from the north slope — the fallen branches are dry enough."

"The Kondos?" Venn asked from his corner.

"The Kondos took the latrine yesterday, and the Meras cooked. They may rest this morning."

Venn accepted that with a single nod. Arash didn't. He was on his feet before Leni had swallowed her last bite.

"I'll help with the garden," Arash said.

Venn looked at him. Two bowls stopped halfway to mouths.

"I'd rather move than sit," Arash said, looking at the hall instead of at his father.

That hit harder than yelling would've.

Bram leaned toward Leni. "Ready for firewood?"

"Sure. Is there anything more thrilling than wet branches?"

"On a hilltop shrine in a monsoon? Probably not."

They gathered firewood on the north slope — Leni, Bram, Cade Leo, and Wynn Leo. The slope was steep and wet, and the fallen branches were heavy with rain. Cade moved carefully, testing each foothold before committing his weight, and Wynn walked ahead like the slope belonged to him. They worked in near-silence. Leni noticed that Cade and Wynn communicated in small touches — a hand on the shoulder to indicate a branch worth collecting, a tap on the forearm when the slope turned slippery. They had their own language, built from years of reading each other's bodies, and it was the opposite of how the naming wanted families to speak: no declarations, no formal categories, just a hand finding the right shoulder at the right time.

From the slope, Leni could see down to the terraced garden where Jin and Miro Tesh worked side by side, pulling weeds in the rain. Arash had joined them. Jin worked steadily, bending and pulling without pause, in the same punishing rhythm he'd had at the cistern, as if stopping would leave him alone with his own head. Miro matched his pace. Arash was faster but less systematic, yanking weeds in clumps and leaving roots behind. Enn Tesh knelt at the far end of the terrace, replanting herb seedlings the rain had displaced, thumb and forefinger pinching mud around each stem until the row looked even again.

By noon on Day 4, the shrine smelled of wet wood, cooking smoke, and forty-three bodies that hadn't had a proper bath since arriving. The rain had paused, which didn't mean it was over — the clouds sat low and fat over the valley like a held breath. From the main hall's open side, the flood was visible below: brown water covering the valley floor, the switchback road drowned past the third bend. The roads that had brought them here were still gone.

Leni looked at that water and thought: we're not going anywhere.

Looking at the flood, Leni found herself replaying the morning in pieces: Tila's flat hand redirecting the jar line on the cistern steps; Suna catching Nessa's bowl without turning; Jin stooping for another armful of weeds before Arash had finished straightening; Enn pinching mud around uprooted herbs at the terrace edge.

Somewhere behind her, Venn was still talking. Down on the terrace, Enn pressed the last loose seedling upright and waited a beat before taking her hand away. It stayed.

---

Asha sat down beside Pik Leo, and that was how it started.

Leni watched it happen from the courtyard steps, where she sat with her journal balanced on her knees, because the courtyard was the only place where she could be alone without being alone — people passed through, but nobody stopped to supervise her.

Suna's youngest was four, and four-year-olds don't have politics. Asha had spent the morning following Suna around the kitchen, getting underfoot, being redirected, and finally being deposited in the courtyard with a firmness that suggested Suna's patience had a floor. Asha sat on the warm stone for exactly thirty seconds before she walked over to where Pik Leo was playing with a pile of smooth river stones he'd found near the cistern.

Pik looked up, shoved the flattest stones toward her, and Asha sat down next to him. That was apparently enough.

Then Melie Parra drifted over. She stopped beside them, eyes flicking from Pik to Asha to the tower. Three seconds, maybe. Then she sat and started picking through the pile for the smoothest stones.

Nori Dao came next — Bett's daughter, seven, with the same economy of movement Leni had noticed in Bett, everything purposeful, nothing wasted. She didn't ask to join. She brought a stone and placed it on top of the tower and that was that.

Kael Mera, eight, arrived running from the direction of the sleeping quarters, chased by Kiri Tesh, also eight, both of them making a noise that suggested they had decided a stone courtyard was a racetrack. They nearly knocked over the tower. Asha said something sharp — Leni couldn't hear the words, but the tone was pure Deshi, three generations distilled into a four-year-old's voice — and Kael stopped, looked, and sat down.

By the middle of the afternoon, the courtyard held a cluster of children that made no sense by the shrine's family map. Asha Mera, Melie Parra, Pik Leo, Nori Dao, Kael Mera, Kiri Tesh, Ori Kondo, two of the younger Kondo cousins, and Tomas — Leni's own brother, who had abandoned her to join the group with a ten-year-old's confidence that playing was more important than family loyalty.

The stone tower had evolved into a game Leni didn't recognize — partly building, partly tag, partly an elaborate system of rules that the children were inventing in real time and revising by shouting. Pik was the most inventive — he kept proposing modifications that the others either adopted or ignored, and he seemed equally happy either way, his face lit up — a child who had discovered other children after being, Leni guessed, often the only child in whatever room he was in. Ori Kondo was the enforcer — she had inherited her uncle Venn's sense of order, if not his rigidity, and she settled disputes with a firmness that made the younger children comply without resentment. Tomas, who at home would never have voluntarily spent time with anyone under the age of nine, was teaching Kiri a clapping game that involved increasingly complicated hand patterns and both of them failing and laughing.

Rimi Mera, twelve, sat at the edge of the group, old enough to feel the pull of both sides. She braided Ori's hair while Ori argued with Tomas about whose turn it was, and the braid kept tightening under her hands without her ever looking down. Raya Dao, thirteen, leaned against the courtyard wall with her arms crossed, committed to looking above it all even as her eyes kept tracking the tower. Sem Dao, eleven, had no such dignity — he was in the middle of it, building and arguing and laughing, a lanky boy with a laugh that started in his stomach and didn't stop until someone pushed him over, which Kael did twice.

Leni watched. She was too old for this. Too old by exactly the right amount to make it painful, because part of her wanted to sit down and stack stones with Asha, and the rest of her knew that wanting that was itself the proof she was past it.

She bit her thumbnail and wrote in her journal: *Ori Kondo had Asha in her lap. Tomas ditched me for a stone tower and didn't even look sorry.*

Fen Tesh was on the far side of the courtyard, sitting on the low wall that separated the prayer space from the sleeping quarters. He had a stick and was scraping patterns into the moss between the stones — not watching the children, not ignoring them, occupying the same in-between space Leni was in. The other teenager. She thought about going over and saying something, but what would she say? Nice shrine. Great flood. Love what they've done with the mandatory prayers. She stayed where she was.

The parents cared. Suna came to the courtyard door and stood there for a long moment, her eyes tracking Asha, Kael, Rimi — counting her children, placing them in the geography of the group. Her face stayed calm, but her fingers worried at each other as if she were sorting invisible tasks. Asha was sitting in Ori Kondo's lap while Ori braided a piece of string into something. Leni waited for Suna to call Asha back. Suna didn't. She stood there one beat longer, mouth flattening, then turned back to the kitchen. Leni couldn't tell whether that was trust, exhaustion, or just one more fight Suna wasn't picking.

Nessa appeared later with Ada fussing on her hip. She found Tomas in the scrum and relaxed visibly — Leni could see the tension leave her mother's shoulders when she confirmed all three of her older children were accounted for. Baby Ada reached toward the group with both fists and made a sound of longing that no one over the age of one could have produced without embarrassment. Nessa bounced her and retreated.

Jin Parra came to the courtyard and leaned against the wall, watching Melie play. He stood exactly far enough away that she wouldn't notice him watching, and he watched like he was trying to store the sight of her against whatever came next. He rubbed the side of his jaw — that thing he did, the brief lock before his mouth opened, like speech was a door that stuck. After a few minutes, Melie saw him and waved, and he waved back, and she turned back to the game. He stayed.

Venn Kondo sent Tila to collect the Kondo children for afternoon rest, and Tila came to the courtyard with the authority of her voice alone. "Ori. The others. Come rest." The Kondo cousins peeled off from the group without argument, but Ori looked up from the string she was braiding and said, "Five more minutes."

Tila waited.

"Please."

Tila gave her five minutes. Leni timed it in her head. At four minutes and thirty seconds, Ori kissed the top of Asha's head — an odd gesture, casual and intimate, from one child to another child she'd known for barely four days — and followed Tila back inside.

The play group shrank but didn't dissolve. It kept reforming throughout the afternoon in different configurations, like water finding its level. The children moved between families without asking permission. They shared food, shared space, shared the game whose rules mutated hourly. Leni kept waiting for one of the adults to stop it outright, to call a child back hard enough to make the line stick. Nobody did.

By late afternoon, Melie was asleep with her head on Nori's stomach in the prayer courtyard, both of them curled on the warm stone like animals who'd chosen each other's warmth by instinct, and Kiri Tesh was teaching Tomas the same half-haunting song while he kept missing the rhythm and laughing.

---

By evening on Day 4, Leni knew two things about the custodians' quarters: the radio was in there, and the families weren't supposed to linger outside the door. Nobody had actually said they couldn't listen. That was different. She'd figured that out on Day 2.

Now heads were turning toward the custodians' door every time it opened. Whatever was coming over the radio, it wasn't good.

The evening gathering was the custodians' final scheduled event. After the dinner meal, the families collected in the main hall for what Fasa called "reflection" and what Leni called sitting around while adults talked about nothing useful. Oil lamps threw their warm unsteady light across the stone walls. Moths the size of Leni's thumb circled the flames, casting shadows that pulsed and shrank. The evening air was heavy, saturated with the smell of rain and wet earth and the woodsmoke that permeated everything — hair, clothes, skin. By the fourth day, everyone at the shrine smelled the same. Like the shrine.

Leni was bracing for another round of adults talking in circles when Peyam Mera produced a deck of cards.

Leni didn't know where the cards came from. They were old, soft-cornered, played with for decades until the faces were blurred and the suits were guesswork. Peyam had been one of the quietest people at the shrine since they arrived — quieter even than Jin, and Jin had turned silence into a vocation. Peyam was small and deliberate in his movements, with his carefully trimmed white beard and his thread bracelet and his genuine absorption in Olet's prayers. His lips moved at the edge of everything else he did, so steady Leni sometimes stopped noticing them.

But he was also, it turned out, a card player.

"You play?" he said to Mava Kondo. He threw it across the room at full courtyard volume, and Mava looked up from the corner where she'd been sitting with her knees under a blanket and her left hand rubbing her ear in that slow circle Leni had already come to associate with her.

"I play," Mava said. Seventy-three, white-haired, frail enough that the wind through the open side of the hall seemed to push her around. But her eyes, when she said it, were sharp as glass.

They sat cross-legged on Peyam's mat, the cards between them, and Peyam dealt. The game was one Leni didn't recognize — a regional variation, something with trumps and tricks and a complicated bidding system that involved tapping the mat with different knuckles.

The first hand went quickly. Mava won. Peyam studied his remaining cards and said nothing.

The second hand went slower. Peyam bid high. Mava matched him. When the tricks were played out, Peyam turned over his final card with a small flourish and smiled — the first smile Leni had seen from him that wasn't directed at god.

Then Mava turned over hers.

"That's not a legal play," Peyam said.

"It is if you're playing hill rules."

"There are no hill rules."

"There are hill rules. My mother taught me, and her mother taught her, and I've been playing hill rules since before your beard came in."

"You can't trump a trump with a lesser suit. It's not done."

"It's done in the hills. You're playing lowland rules, and lowland rules are for people who don't know how to play."

Peyam straightened. His beard bristled. It was, Leni realized, the most animated he'd been since arriving at the shrine — more alive in this argument about card rules than he'd been about the flood, the naming, or the sleeping arrangements. He pulled the disputed card from Mava's hand, examined it, held it to the lamplight as if checking for forgery, and set it down with a firmness that suggested he was also setting down a point of principle.

"This card," he said, "is a seven."

"I know what it is."

"A seven doesn't beat a twelve."

"It does if the seven's suited and the twelve is blind."

"There's no such thing as a blind twelve."

"Then what do you call a twelve played without a bid?"

"I call it a twelve."

"And that's why you lose."

A laugh escaped from somewhere — Sem Dao, who was watching from behind Hala's shoulder. Then Rimi Mera giggled, and Bram's low chuckle followed, and suddenly the room was paying attention, and the argument between two people with a combined age of a hundred and forty-five had become the first thing at the shrine nobody watched out of obligation.

Peyam reshuffled. "We play again," he said. "Properly."

"Properly means hill rules."

"Properly means rules. One set. Agreed upon before the first card is dealt."

Mava pulled the blanket tighter around her shoulders. "Fine. But I deal."

"Why?"

"Because you shuffle like a man who learned from a book."

The laughter was real this time — wide and sudden, out before anyone could remember to be polite. Venn snorted from his corner. Deshi covered her mouth but her shoulders shook. Hala's face split into a grin that transformed her entirely — for a moment she looked like someone who wasn't keeping watch over anything, who wasn't positioned between her sister and the rest of the world. Even Bett Dao's guarded face shifted, a small upward pull at the corner of her mouth, there and gone. Jin leaned against the wall and something in his shoulders loosened — not a laugh, not quite, but the nearest he'd come to one all day.

They played four more hands. Mava dealt. The rules were negotiated card by card, with arguments that kept circling back to fundamental disagreements about what constituted a legal play, and whether the hill rules were real rules or an elaborate justification for cheating, and whether Peyam's lowland rules were any better given that he was losing by three hands and his beard was doing the bristling thing again.

Gitta Tesh watched from her place near the Tesh family's mat, small and still, her hands folded in her lap. When Mava won the seventh hand with a play Peyam couldn't even identify, Gitta's mouth curved into something private and almost fond, and Leni found herself wondering who used to sit across from her.

Leni found herself smiling. She was still smiling when she noticed Fen Tesh watching the game from across the hall, his dark hair in his eyes, his mouth pulled crooked as if laughter had caught him by surprise and he didn't trust it yet. Their eyes met for a half-second. They both looked away.

For ten minutes the flood dropped out of the room.

Then Olet spoke.

"The news today," he said, and the leftover laughter snapped off. People straightened. Olet stood by the main hall's open side, the valley behind him invisible in the dark but audible — that steady rush of water that never stopped. The oil lamp nearest him lit the lower half of his face and left his eyes in shadow.

"The radio received a broadcast from the eastern station this afternoon. Checkpoints have been established on the main highway east of the pass. The lowland curfew has been extended from dusk to dawn. There are reports of fires and violence in the lowlands, including the capital district."

Bram leaned forward. "What kind of violence?"

"The broadcast did not specify."

"Are they sending road crews? Is anyone clearing the route?"

"There was no mention of road crews."

The silence that followed had weight. Leni could feel it pressing against the walls, against the high stone ceiling, against her own ribs. She looked at her father. Bram's jaw was tight, and his hands, which were usually in motion, had gone still on his knees. Nessa had baby Ada pressed against her shoulder, and her other hand was spread across Tomas's back, fingers splayed as if she were checking he was still there.

Venn spoke from his corner. "How long until the road is passable?"

"The water has not receded," Olet said.

"It's been four days."

"The water has not receded."

"Seasonal flooding on this road takes four to five days to drain. I've traveled this route before."

"Under normal conditions, yes. The conditions are not normal. The upstream infrastructure has been damaged. The drainage systems are not functioning."

Venn's voice hardened. "So what are you telling us?"

"I am telling you what the radio told us. The checkpoints, the curfew, the reports of violence. The water has not receded. That is what we know."

Leni looked around the room. The laughter from the card game had burned off like mist, as if it had never happened. Suna sat with Asha in her lap, one hand resting flat on the child's shin, her whole body held too still. Leni had seen Suna go that still only when something was wrong and there was nothing useful to say about it. Hala had pulled Raya and Sem closer to her, a physical gathering that looked almost reflexive. Cade Leo put his arm around Pik's shoulder. Wynn didn't move, but his hand found Cade's knee and stayed there.

Bram spoke again, and his voice was the careful voice he used when he was constructing an argument and needed every piece to land. "The political situation. The identity checks, the movement permits. That's what's keeping the road crews away. Not just the water."

"That is a reasonable interpretation," Olet said.

"So even if the water dropped tomorrow —"

"Even if the water dropped tomorrow, the road leads to a checkpoint. Whether you would be permitted to pass is not something we can know from here."

Bram sat back. He looked at Nessa. Their eyes met and held, and Leni saw something pass between her parents that she was not supposed to see. Nessa's mouth tightened. Bram gave the smallest shake of his head. It was a whole conversation anyway.

The gathering broke up slowly after that. Families retreated to their sleeping areas under the kind of quiet bad news leaves behind, when nobody knows yet what to do with their hands. Peyam folded his cards, squared the deck against his knee, and tucked it inside his vest. Mava pulled her blanket around her shoulders and let Tila guide her back to the Kondo corner, her frail body listing slightly, the sharpness from the card game already draining from her face. The moth shadows danced on the walls as lamps were carried to sleeping quarters, each flame pulling its bubble of warm light along the stone corridors.

Leni lay on her mat in the dark. Tomas was already asleep beside her — he was ten, he could sleep through anything, his body still trusted the adults to handle whatever was happening. Ada was asleep on Nessa's chest, milk-drunk and limp. Bram and Nessa were lying down but not sleeping. Leni could hear them whispering.

She couldn't hear the words. She could hear the register — low, tight, the kind of whisper Leni had only heard from her parents when they were scared and trying not to wake anyone. She caught fragments. "Checkpoints." "Papers." Her mother said something that ended on a rising sound — a question, or the word *how*, or a name Leni couldn't place. Bram whispered back. More fragments. "...morning..." and "...doesn't matter what we..." and then Nessa's hand found Bram's in the lamplight from the corridor — Leni saw it, her father's long fingers closing around her mother's smaller hand, and the grip was not comfort. It was ballast. Two people holding each other because the ground was moving and there was nothing else to hold.

Leni turned away from them. She reached into her bag and pulled out her journal and the pen she'd brought from home, from the school desk in her bedroom, a place that already felt stupidly far away. The pen was a cheap ballpoint, blue ink, almost out.

She wrote by the corridor's faint lamplight, her handwriting cramped and leaning hard to the right, the same ugly slant it got whenever she wrote in the dark or in a hurry or both. She wrote about the day. The cooking rotation and Deshi's salt commentary. The children's game that made no sense and didn't need to. Ori kissing Asha's head. Fen scraping moss off the wall with a stick. Her mother's freckles darkening in the humidity. Peyam and Mava arguing about hill rules while the flood erased the road below them and they played like the game was the only thing in the world.

She wrote: *Nobody's coming to get us. Dad knows. Mom knows. They're just not saying it out loud because once you say it out loud you have to do something about it and there's nothing to do.*

She wrote: *Tomorrow the bell's going to ring again and we're going to get up and carry water and peel onions and the kids are going to play like nothing's wrong because nothing is wrong for them. They don't know the road goes to a checkpoint. They don't know what checkpoints mean.*

She didn't write: *I'm scared.* She didn't need to. The handwriting said it — the hard lean of the letters, the pressure of the pen against the page, the words driving hard and then cutting off.

She closed the journal and put it under her pillow and lay there listening. The flood. The rain starting up again, soft at first and then heavier, drumming on the stone overhead. Ada's small sleeping breaths. A man coughing in the Kondo section. The insects, that constant monsoon hum from the wet vegetation around the shrine, a sound so persistent you stopped hearing it until the night got quiet enough to bring it back.

There were forty-three of them on the hilltop, four days in, with no road, no phone, and nobody coming for them. Morning would come the way it did here now: people stirring before dawn, damp mats hauled out to air, somebody getting the fire going beyond the partition, the bell sooner or later, everyone getting up to carry water, peel onions, clean, eat. By noon the courtyard would be full again — dirty knees, wet cuffs, Tomas shoulder to shoulder with Kiri, Melie drifting wherever Nori sat, Asha ending up in whatever lap was nearest. Somebody's mother would call for food or rest or prayers, and half the kids would pretend not to hear the first time.

Leni bit her thumbnail in the dark. She could taste stone dust and onion and the faint metallic residue of the pen she'd been chewing without noticing.

Day 5 would look like that. Then Day 6. Then however many days until the water went down or the checkpoints opened or someone, somewhere, figured out they were up here.

She didn't think anyone was figuring out they were up here.


# Chapter 4

The mat under Suna's hip was damp with sweat when she woke. Beyond the partition the cooking fire was already lit, and the hush before the custodians' bell still held. She lay still for a moment and took stock. Asha breathing against her side, the small furnace heat of a child in deep sleep. Kael in the corner of the mat, curled tight, one ankle hooked over the other. Rimi asleep with her forearm across her eyes — twelve and already perfecting the art of blocking things out. Deshi's mat empty, which meant she was up, which meant criticism of the morning meal was already being prepared alongside the rice.

Peyam was on his mat by the far wall, still asleep or pretending, his thin chest rising under the worn cotton of his shirt. His devotional beads were wound around his left wrist even in sleep. He was seventy-two and had not missed morning prayers since arriving at the shrine — up before the bell every day, crossing the courtyard to his corner of the prayer yard while the stones were still damp with the night's rain. He didn't ask Suna's permission or tell her his schedule. He simply went. Of all the Mera family members she was managing alone, Peyam was the only one who required nothing from her.

She eased Asha off her shoulder and stood. The mat needed hanging again. She rolled it, tucked it under her arm, and crossed to the courtyard. The air outside the sleeping quarters was cooler but not cold — monsoon humidity, the stones still holding the night's damp. The sound of the flood was there as always, a low massed rush from the valley below that she had stopped noticing by the second day and now only heard again when she listened for it. Like living next to a river, except the river was the road, and the road was gone. She hung the mat over the low courtyard wall and pressed it flat. The stone beneath her hands was already warming.

Breakfast was communal now, the families rotating through kitchen duty on the custodians' schedule. This morning was the Kondos' turn, and when Suna reached the cooking area under the stone overhang, Tila Kondo was already portioning rice into bowls while two of the younger Kondo cousins carried them to the main hall. The smoke from the cooking fire drifted low under the overhang, thick and sweet from monsoon-damp wood, the kind that got into clothes and hair and stayed. By the fifth day everyone at the shrine smelled of it — smoke and damp cotton and the mineral flatness of cistern water.

Deshi was there too, seated on the bench near the wall with the blue shawl she'd brought from home pulled around her shoulders, watching the Kondos work with the alert stillness of a woman conducting an inspection she hadn't been asked to perform. The shawl was frayed at both edges, pilling along the fold where she clutched it closed, and she had refused Suna's offer to wash it because it would lose what was left of its shape. It was the only thing Deshi had kept close through the whole journey — not sentiment, Suna thought, only the stubbornness of a woman who would not be separated from what was hers.

"The lentils are thin," Deshi said as Suna passed.

"They're fine."

"They're thin. More water than dal. You could read a letter through them."

"Then don't eat them."

Deshi made a sound balanced between agreement and objection — the sound of a woman who had won the exchange by provoking a response. Suna let it go and went to collect her children for breakfast.

The main hall was already filling. Forty-three people eating in shifts of twenty and twenty-three, the arrangement worked out by the custodians on the second day and adhered to since without further argument. The Mera family ate in the first shift with the Kondos and the Leos. Suna gathered Kael and Rimi — Asha was still half-asleep on Rimi's hip, her head tipped against her sister's shoulder, mouth slack — and found their spot on the long bench. The stone floor of the main hall was cool underfoot, not yet reached by the morning sun. The open side of the hall faced the valley, and the light that came through it was flat and gray, filtered through the cloud cover that hadn't fully broken in five days.

Suna had told the children their father had gone ahead. He was waiting for them at the end of the road, at their destination, and they would meet him when the water went down and the route opened again. Rimi had stopped asking about it on the third day. Kael had never asked. Asha was too young for the question. The lie turned breakfast mechanical. Her mouth kept drying out, so she had to wash every few bites down with water, and every time one of the children looked up she fixed her attention on the rice until they looked away.

Across the hall, the Kondo family occupied their usual half of the second bench, their twelve members arranged with the unconscious precision of a family that had been traveling together and knew its own geometry. Venn sat at the end, upright, eating with the deliberate pace of a man who considered meals a public act. Beside him, at the far end of the bench where the draft from the valley came up cool and steady, Mava Kondo sat with a bowl of dal in her lap, her left hand rising periodically to work at her ear, palm pressed to the cartilage in a slow looping motion as habitual and unconscious as breathing. Suna had noticed the habit on the first day and marked it the way she marked most things about other families: noted, sorted, set aside.

Asha ate slowly, picking rice grains out of her bowl with her fingers and dropping half of them on Rimi's thigh. She was four and had no interest in efficiency. Her attention moved across the hall, watching faces, watching hands, her gaze traveling from person to person with a four-year-old's undiscriminating appetite for the visible world. She touched everything — the bench, the bowl, Rimi's ear, the stone wall behind them — her hands in constant exploratory motion, absorbing textures like a second kind of sight.

She reached up and rubbed her left ear.

It was the precision that caught Suna, not the fact of it. Children touched their ears constantly — Asha especially, who touched everything. But this was not a casual scratch. Her palm settled over the cartilage and turned in slow, deliberate circles, fingers loose and slightly spread. The motion belonged to Mava. Suna looked across the hall at the old woman, still rubbing her own ear on the far bench, and then back at Asha, who had already stopped and gone to picking at her rice again, her attention elsewhere.

Suna took another bite and kept her face still. Across the hall Mava's hand made another slow circle. Asha had already gone back to her rice. Kael kicked the bench leg again, and Suna told him to stop.

The play group formed after breakfast, as it had for the past two days — the shrine's younger children sorting themselves by affinity rather than family. Kael went immediately to the Kondo boys, who had invented a game involving pebbles and a crack in the courtyard stone that seemed to function as both goal and boundary. Rimi drifted toward the older girls — Raya Dao, a few of the Kondo cousins — hovering at the edge of their circle, careful not to press in and not to stand alone either. Asha was with the younger ones: Nori Dao, Pik Leo, Ori Kondo. They had claimed the corner of the main hall near the open side where the light came in and the stone floor was warm with reflected sun, and they moved among each other with the uncomplicated physicality of children who had not yet learned to maintain the distances their parents kept.

Suna was sorting laundry — hers and Deshi's and the children's, the limited clothes they'd arrived with, washed and rewashed in the cistern until the fabric was thinning at the seams. She could see Asha from where she sat against the wall, the bright head moving among the other children, and she registered each position the way she registered everything about her children: automatically, continuously, the way you track weather or the level of water in a pot. Asha by the wall. Asha near the open side, holding Nori's hand. Asha standing next to Ori, reaching for something in Ori's pocket. Asha near Mava.

Mava was sitting in her usual spot — the section of bench near the open side where the draft came up from the valley. She had a small cloth bag in her lap, and she was sorting dried lentils with her fingers, picking out the small pale stones that hid among the grains and setting them aside in a neat row on the bench. She did this every morning after breakfast. It was her contribution to the kitchen — a task that required no standing, no lifting, no argument with Venn about whether she was overexerting herself — and she performed it with the patient sureness of someone who had been sorting lentils for longer than Suna had been alive, her fingers reading the difference between grain and stone by weight and texture alone. The stones she removed were small and smooth, washed to the same color as the lentils by decades of monsoon water, and she found them without looking.

Asha was standing near her, watching the old woman's fingers move through the grain. Mava gave her the mild automatic smile of a woman who had been around children her whole life and neither resented nor sought out their attention. Asha pointed at her and said something Suna couldn't hear across the hall.

Then she said it again, louder, turning toward Suna. "Nushi."

"What?" Suna looked up from the damp shirt in her hands.

"Nushi." Asha pointed at Mava again, then turned back to the old woman and the lentils. She said it with the flat certainty she used for bird or cup or Mama, as if she'd named exactly what was in front of her.

The word landed oddly. Suna repeated it once under her breath, testing its shape, and it stayed strange in her mouth. Across the hall Mava kept sorting lentils, head bent, fingers moving through grain and stone. Suna twisted water from the shirt until it ran down her wrist and went back to the laundry.

The afternoon was the dead time at the shrine. Rain started again after the midday meal — heavy, steady, the kind that lasted hours and drove everyone inside, turning the courtyard into a shallow pool of runoff that crept slowly toward the terraced garden. The main hall settled into the cramped, restless quiet of forty-three people with nowhere to go. The space was large enough for all of them, but only just — bodies shifting on benches and mats, conversations murmured in corners, the ambient hum of enforced proximity. Baby Ada Okoye was fussing. Someone was snoring. Moths had found the first lit oil lamp and were circling it in the gray afternoon light, their bodies tapping against the glass with a sound like small fingernails.

Kael was playing quietly with the pebble game he'd learned from the Kondo boys. Rimi had borrowed a book from Leni Okoye and was reading against the far wall, knees drawn up. Deshi was asleep on her mat, her blue shawl tucked under her chin, mouth open, snoring with the easy disregard of a woman who had stopped caring what she sounded like in her sleep decades ago.

Asha had worn herself out in the play group and climbed into Suna's lap after the meal, her eyelids dropping, her body performing that heavy, gradual collapse that small children did when sleep took them between one word and the next. She was asleep in minutes, her weight tipped against Suna's chest, one hand curled around a fold of Suna's shirt. Her breathing deepened and steadied.

Suna held her and watched the rain. The light in the main hall was gray and even. Across the hall, other families moved in their own slow orbits — Hala Dao wiping down the cooking pots with a rag, Jin Parra sitting near the wall with Melie asleep across his knees, one hand resting on her back in the absent protective gesture of a man who slept lightly. Cade Leo and his partner Wynn sat together with Pik between them, Wynn's head on Cade's shoulder, the three of them arranged in a quiet private triangle that excluded the room. Mava was on her bench, eyes closed, hands in her lap. Not rubbing. Still.

Asha shifted against Suna's chest. Her breathing stayed deep and even, the breath of real sleep, not the light doze that could be broken by a voice or a closing door. Her left hand, the one that had been gripping Suna's shirt, loosened and rose.

She brought it to her left ear and began to rub, palm laid over the cartilage, the movement slow and even.

Suna watched the hand move. The motion was precise and unhurried, the same tempo and pressure she had seen at breakfast, the same habit she had watched Mava Kondo repeat for five days. But Asha was asleep. Her eyes were closed, her body slack, her breathing unchanged. There was no one across the hall to watch, no gesture to mirror. Her hand moved against her ear with the certainty of a thing the body knew how to do on its own, without instruction or audience.

Suna sat very still. The muscles in her arms locked around Asha's warm weight. Rain hit the courtyard stones outside. The moths tapped the lamp glass. Asha's hand kept its slow circuit. Only when the hand stopped did Suna let herself move, and by then the thought was already there: I need to talk to the old woman.

---

She found Mava after the rain eased, sitting alone on the courtyard steps. The stones were wet and dark, and the sky had gone to that thick white pause between rain spells, clouds rearranging themselves before the next fall. The air was cooler and smelled of stone and wet earth and the green bite of monsoon growth pushing up faster than anything could eat it. The flood sound was louder after rain, swollen with fresh runoff, a continuous low roar from the valley that made ordinary voices feel temporary.

Mava was watching the view. From the courtyard steps you could see down the slope to where the switchback road disappeared into brown water — the point where the seven roads converged, all of them drowned. The water was the color of milked tea, opaque, slow-moving at this distance, its surface pocked with rain. A bird Suna couldn't name was working the updraft above the waterline, banking and tilting with the concentrated effort of something hunting.

Suna sat down next to her. She didn't know Mava. Five days of shared confinement had made them neighbors, nothing more. They had eaten in the same hall, slept behind nearby partitions, crossed the same courtyard with damp mats over their arms. Mava was a Kondo, one of Venn's twelve, and the Kondo family kept its own close. Suna had heard her laugh at a card game two nights ago, a dry low sound that carried farther than her small frame suggested. She had seen her sort lentils, rub her ear, walk slowly to the latrine and back. That was what she knew.

"Can I ask you something?"

Mava looked at her. Up close, her face was deeply lined, the skin around her eyes creased in a way that suggested decades of squinting against bright light — a life lived outdoors, or at altitude, or both. Her eyes themselves were sharp and dark and unhurried, the eyes of someone who had long ago stopped feeling obligated to respond quickly.

"You can ask."

"Your ear." Suna touched her own left ear. "The way you rub it. You do it all the time."

Mava's hand rose, almost involuntarily, to her ear. She caught herself and lowered it. A small embarrassed sound, almost a laugh. "Old habit. My sister used to say I'd wear a hole through it."

"Asha does it too."

The faint amusement faded. Mava's face stayed open but the quality of her attention shifted — she was listening now, not just hearing.

"My daughter. She's four. She started doing it this morning — your exact habit, left hand and all. I thought she was copying you. Kids do that. They steal gestures." Suna paused. She was aware that what she was about to say would sound unreasonable, and she could not make it sound less so, so she said it directly. "She did it in her sleep. Just now, in the main hall. She was completely asleep, and her hand came up and did it anyway."

Mava did not speak. She looked at Suna for several seconds with an expression Suna could not categorize. There was no disbelief in it, no alarm either, only the look of a person hearing a sound she had been straining to hear for a very long time.

"There's something else," Suna said. "She used a word I don't know. She pointed at you and said it. Nushi."

Mava made a small involuntary sound, the breath knocked out of her chest in the one place it couldn't brace. She put her hand flat on the stone step and pressed down, as if she needed to confirm that the stone was solid, that she was sitting on something that would hold.

"Say it again."

"Nushi."

Mava closed her eyes. She sat like that for several seconds, her face turned slightly toward the valley, the wet air moving past them both. When she opened her eyes they had a bright, hard shine that was not tears — the gloss of pressure held at the surface.

"My sister called me that." Her voice was low and steady, each word chosen from a very small and very precise set. "Nushi. It's from our home dialect — an old valley word from the village we grew up in, past the Anke pass. Iru used it only for me. Nobody speaks that dialect anymore. I don't think there's anyone left alive who does, except me. And I barely speak it. I just remember it."

"Your sister."

"Iru." Mava said the name the way she might set down something fragile — with both hands, with her full attention. "My younger sister. She started calling me that when we were small, and then it stuck. Even when we were grown women. Even when I told her it made me sound like a cat." The corner of her mouth lifted for a second, more memory than smile — the ghost of an old joke that had outlived the person who'd made it. "She died on this road. Forty years ago. She was coming down from the pass in a cart with a traveling group — porters, another family, I don't remember who they were — and the road gave way in the monsoon. Three people went down with it. Iru was one of them. She was twenty-nine."

She said it simply. The words came out smooth, rubbed down by use, and Suna had the sense Mava had said them many times where no one could hear.

"I haven't heard anyone say that word since she died," Mava said. "It isn't a word you'd find in a book. It was just what she called me, in a language that was already dying when we were girls."

Suna did not speak for a moment. She was aware of the stone step under her, the damp seeping through the fabric of her skirt where she sat, the flood sound filling the valley below with its steady undifferentiated roar. She was aware that she had come here hoping to be told something ordinary — that "nushi" was a Kondo family nickname, a grandmother's word, something the children shared among themselves — and that she was not going to be told anything ordinary.

"Your daughter's never heard me use that word," Mava said, looking straight at Suna. "Not here. If it comes out at all now, it comes out with Ori when she's sick or grieving, and I don't even hear myself do it."

"No," Suna said. "She hasn't."

They sat together on the wet steps. The bird was still working the updraft above the waterline. From inside the main hall came the muffled sound of children's voices — Kael's shout, the babbling run of the younger play group, a baby crying and being hushed — the ordinary noise of forty-three people crammed into a space that had been built for travelers passing through, not for travelers stuck.

Mava rubbed her ear. She caught herself doing it and this time did not stop. Her palm worked the ear in its old slow circle while she watched the valley as if the landscape had another shape underneath it.

"The road," she said. "Iru died right below us. Somewhere down there, under the water. I didn't know that when we arrived — Venn planned the route, but he never knew what stretch of road this was to me. Habel did. She was fifteen when it happened, old enough to remember the search and the way my mother sounded when they brought the body up." She paused. "I recognized the road on the second day. I've been watching the water cover the place where she fell, and I haven't said anything about it to anyone, because what would I say? It was forty years ago. The cart went off the road and she went down with it, and they found her body two days later in the river below. She was twenty-nine years old and she called me Nushi and nobody has said that word to me since."

Suna thought: twenty-nine, the same age as Jin Parra. The fact arrived useless and exact. She had nowhere to put it, but it stayed.

"I don't understand how Asha could know that word," Suna said.

"No." Mava's voice was quiet. "I don't either."

Suna felt the skin tighten along her arms. If Mava had laughed, or shrugged, or named some ordinary source for the word, Suna could have filed it and gone back inside. Instead there was only the old woman beside her, the water below, and the fact itself sitting between them with nowhere to be put.

"Can I —" Suna started, and stopped. She had been about to ask if she could bring Asha to Mava, to sit them together, and she realized she did not know what she expected to happen if she did. What she expected the old woman and the child to do in each other's presence that would make this resolve into sense.

"I'd like to see her do it," Mava said quietly. "The ear. I've done that my whole life. My mother did it too. Iru did too."

"Iru did it?"

"All of us. On my mother's side. Left ear. I never noticed until Iru pointed it out — she thought it was funny. She'd do it and then point at me doing it and laugh." Mava's hand was on her ear again, and she let it stay this time. "It's just something our bodies did."

Our bodies. The phrase snagged. Mava's hand was still on her ear, and Suna saw Asha's sleeping hand there too, left palm over cartilage, the same slow turn.

Suna sat with her a while longer. They did not talk much more. Mava asked after Asha — how she was sleeping, whether she was eating well, whether the damp in the sleeping quarters bothered her — the practical questions of a woman who had raised children and grandchildren and great-nieces and understood that the body was the first thing you checked, regardless of what else was happening. Suna answered. The rain started again, softly, more mist than fall, and they watched it settle on the courtyard stones and on the brown surface of the flood below.

When Suna went back inside, the main hall was dim and warm with breath and lamp-oil, the evening meal being prepared in the kitchen outside, its smoke drifting in through the open side. She found her children: Kael at his pebble game, Rimi reading, Asha on their shared mat, awake now, arranging a strip of cloth she'd found somewhere into shapes that followed a logic only she understood.

"Asha."

"Mama."

"Who's Nushi?"

Asha looked at her with the transparent certainty of a child who did not understand why an adult would ask something so obvious. She pointed through the doorway, toward the courtyard, in the direction where Mava had been sitting and might still be sitting.

"Nushi," she said.

Suna picked her up and held her, pressing her face into Asha's hair, which smelled of smoke and sweat and the warm-grain scent of a child who had been sleeping on a cotton mat all afternoon. Asha allowed it for a moment, then squirmed, and Suna let her go. She watched her daughter return to the mat and the strip of cloth, small fingers working it into a new arrangement.

After supper she saw Mava leaning toward Venn at the Kondo bench, speaking low while the others carried their bowls to the wash buckets. Suna couldn't hear the words, but Venn's face had gone still by the time Mava finished.

That night Suna slept badly. Deshi snored on her right side, one hand tucked under her cheek. Peyam murmured through the tail end of his prayers, the beads clicking softly against each other in the dark. Rain fell and stopped and fell again, and the flood below the shrine filled the night with its low, constant sound — water over stone, water over earth, water over the place where the roads converged and drowned.

Asha slept deeply, curled against Suna's side, her face pressed into Suna's ribs. At some point in the middle of the night — the rain had paused and the only sound was the flood and the breathing of her family and the forty other lives separated from them by nothing but thin partitions of stone — Asha's left hand rose to her ear.

"Nushi," she said, not waking.

In the dark her hand found the ear again and moved there with the same patient turn.

Suna lay still and watched the hand move in the dark. The gesture no longer looked borrowed. It looked settled, at home in the body making it. By morning she would have to let somebody else see it — Mava again, maybe more than Mava. She could not keep this one inside the narrow partition of her own watching and still call it ordinary. The water below them sounded like the road giving way under Iru's feet. She held her daughter against her ribs and did not sleep for a long time.


# Chapter 5

Venn counted before the bell. Not because the number changed, but because order lived in the saying of it and the saying had to happen somewhere before the custodians began theirs. Twelve Kondos on the hill. Twelve sleeping mats. Twelve bowls at breakfast. Twelve bodies he meant to take down the switchback the moment the road showed stone again. Arash sat beside him on the wall, long legs stretched out, jaw set toward the valley as if he could stare the flood backward. There were nineteen days until the wedding. The gauging stone at the third bend was still half lost under brown water. Below it the seven-road convergence had gone flat and wrong, nothing but current where routes should have split cleanly. Every morning Venn made the same calculation: if the rain stopped today, if the lower mud held, if the crews in the lowlands had not given the road up for some other crisis. Every morning it came out the same. Not yet.

He had slept badly because Mava had given him Iru in pieces after supper. Not the whole story. Just the parts sharp enough to cut on contact. The word. The old valley dialect from Ishe. The child saying it in sleep with her hand at her ear. Venn had been twelve when Iru came back up this hill on a door ripped off somebody's shed. He remembered the wet pine boards flexing under her weight, remembered a strip of yellow cloth stuck to her shin with blood and rainwater, remembered one broken wheel from the cart still turning uselessly in the ditch when the men climbed down to fetch her. He did not need Suna Mera's little girl to make that death uncanny. Iru had been uncanny enough dead.

Arash rubbed his palms on his knees and kept looking at the valley.

"You think Fasa's going to make a room of it?" he asked.

Venn did not look at him. "She rang the bell before breakfast. She didn't do that for weather."

Arash's mouth tightened. He was Venn's son in the jaw and the shoulders and nowhere else clear enough to trust.

"Mava shouldn't have to speak if she doesn't want to," Arash said.

"If Fasa wanted quiet, she'd have come to our doorway. She wants witnesses."

Arash gave a short breath through his nose. Not agreement. Not refusal either. That in-between had become his habit over the past few days, and Venn disliked it more than open argument. Open argument could be answered. The half-space between one thing and another just spread.

The bell rang from the custodians' walkway. It sent a single iron note through stone.

"Everyone," Venn said.

They came at once. Tila came with the cloth wrap of needles and thread still in her hand. Habel came with Ori tucked close against her hip, the girl watching the Mera side even now, as if Asha might step out from behind a doorway and solve this by being ordinary again. Mava crossed more slowly than the others, with Arash steady at her elbow. Ger arrived behind Habel, then Rem and Osh, then the younger ones, subdued for once by the bell and the room it promised. There were twelve. All of them were here.

The naming hall was already full. Rain light washed in from the open side, gray and cold. Lamps burned along the inner wall. Bram Okoye leaned so far forward both palms were planted on his knees, ready for the room to give him an excuse. Jin Parra sat against the far wall with Melie in his lap, his face turned inward. Gitta Tesh's thumbs moved in their private circle. Hala Dao's shoulder was angled in front of Bett without seeming to be. The Leo family held themselves close together at the end of a bench. Suna Mera stood near Deshi with Asha on her hip and Kael pressed against her side, her body already arranged around refusal. Fasa stood at the front with the valley at her back. Olet sat to one side, hands on his knees, like stone that had chosen the shape of a man.

Venn put the Kondos on the right, near the inner wall, in the arrangement that said what he needed it to say. Tila stood beside him and Arash held the place at his shoulder. Mava sat because standing too long made her knees buckle, with Habel and Ori close behind her. Ger took the far end. Rem and Osh drew the younger ones into the line. No gaps. No openings. If the hall wanted to look at the Kondos, it would look at them as a unit.

Fasa began before he had fully settled.

"The naming continues," she said. "Four days ago each family spoke itself aloud. Since then this house has watched you eat, sleep, quarrel, and wake. Some things do not stay where you first set them."

Venn felt the back of his neck tighten.

"The Mera family and the Kondo family," Fasa said. "Come forward. Stand where the room can see you together."

The silence hit hard enough that even the Okoye baby stopped fussing.

Suna answered first. "No."

She held Asha against her chest and did not look at Venn. "We're not doing that. What happened between Asha and Mava stays with us. I'm not standing my daughter up for the hall."

Venn should have welcomed it. The refusal came from the other side. The Mera woman was doing his work for him.

But she had refused without even turning to him. She kept her eyes on Fasa, cutting the Kondos out of it entirely, and that slight stung worse than a direct quarrel. It made his family sound incidental, a thing to be managed between shrine women and a mother with a frightened child.

"My son marries in nineteen days," Venn said. He gave the words to Fasa, not to Suna. "The road is still under floodwater, and my family is waiting on it to clear. I won't have our name tied to another family's because a child made a sound in her sleep. We came here to pass through, not to be bound to strangers."

Fasa let the sentence die on the stone.

"Mava," she said.

Mava's hand rose to her left ear and began its slow circles. Ori saw it and caught Habel's wrist with both hands.

Venn shook his head once.

Fasa did not look at him. "Speak for yourself, Mava Kondo. Tell the room what Suna Mera asked you yesterday."

Mava lowered her hand to her lap. "The child used a word."

"Say it."

Mava swallowed. "Nushi."

The word sat in the middle of the hall. Venn heard rain against the open stone, the soft gutter of lamp oil, somebody dragging a breath through a narrow throat. He had not heard that word aloud in decades. Hearing it now cracked open the old picture against his will: the lifted door, the muddy hem, Mava making a sound Venn had never heard from a grown woman and never forgot.

"Whose word is it?" Fasa asked.

"My sister's." Mava's voice was thin but steady. "Iru's. Old valley speech from Ishe, where she married. It means something like the other half of my face. No one here speaks it. No one here should know it. Iru has been dead forty years."

Fasa turned to Suna. "Do you know the valley speech of Ishe?"

"No."

"Has anyone in your family traveled there?"

"No."

Fasa looked past Suna. "Deshi. Peyam."

Old Peyam opened his eyes, beads cupped in both hands, and gave one deliberate shake of his head.

Deshi said nothing. She fixed on Mava so completely the rest of the hall might as well have emptied.

"Then say what is in front of you," Fasa said. "A child has spoken a dead woman's word with no road to it that anyone in this room can name. Stand together and answer to that."

Suna's arms tightened around Asha. "I already answered."

Fasa kept her eyes on her. "I heard the refusal. The thing itself is still here."

Venn rose before he knew he had decided to.

"This is grief," he said. The hall gave his voice back to him. "My aunt's grief. A word she's carried in her mouth for forty years. A four-year-old saying something strange does not make a bond between families. It does not rewrite who we are. I won't have my family hauled into a story because a ritual wants more than the facts can bear."

His words died in the benches. Bram leaned farther forward, as if this might finally become an argument he could win. Jin stared at the floor between his feet. Cade Leo set a hand on Pik's shoulder and kept it there. Even the children held still.

Then Deshi stood.

Venn had filed her days ago: the sharp old woman from the Mera side, measuring rice by bite and children by noise. He knew the type because his own family had made women like that and depended on them. She should have stayed behind Suna, hard and traditional and on her own side of the room. If anyone in the Mera line should have distrusted this, it should have been her.

She crossed the hall instead.

Her sandals made almost no sound. She stopped in front of Mava's bench and looked down at her.

"Your sister," Deshi said. "What was her name?"

"Iru."

"And Nushi was hers for you?"

"Yes."

Deshi turned her head toward Asha for one beat, then back to Mava. "My granddaughter didn't get that from me."

She sat beside Mava on the Kondo bench.

The room changed shape with that one movement. Venn felt it happen the way he felt a roof beam shift in bad weather, not visible at first, only wrong in the body. If Deshi Mera could plant herself among the Kondos before witnesses, then the line between one family and another had already gone soft in everyone's mind.

Suna made a small shocked sound. "Deshi."

"Be quiet," Deshi said.

She took Mava's hands in both of hers and settled around them with an old, practiced certainty.

"Tell me about Iru."

Mava looked down at the hands around hers. Her throat worked once, twice. Then the sound came out of her ragged and low, and Ori buried her face in Habel's skirt.

"She was four years younger," Mava said. "Pretty. Spoiled. Always laughing at me for worrying. She married into Ishe and kept talking about the valley as if she'd invented it. She was coming home to see me when the cart went over right below the shrine turn. They brought her back on a door because the wheels had shattered."

Venn could see the door again. He had helped wash the mud off Iru's heel because no one had thought to move the child from the room. The skin there had been cold and waxy and torn at the ankle where the wood had bitten. He had thrown up afterward behind the cistern and told no one. Mava did not know he remembered that much. Nobody did.

Deshi did not release her hands.

"Twenty-nine," Mava said. "That's how old she was. Twenty-nine."

Venn stayed on his feet because sitting would have looked like surrender and stepping forward would have put his hands on a woman who did not belong to him. Tila's fingers touched the inside of his wrist, light as thread tension. He did not know whether she was steadying him or warning him. He did not move.

***

Fasa did not call the room back to order. She stayed where she was and let the silence stretch around the bench where the two old women sat. Bench wood creaked under shifting weight, but every face stayed on Mava's open mouth. Olet sat at the edge of the hall with his hands on his knees, outside the pull of it. The hall had tilted and would not tilt back.

She wept without trying to stop. She did not weep loudly. The tears kept dropping off her jaw onto the front of her blouse while Deshi sat close and held on. Habel moved to Mava's other side and pressed a palm between her shoulder blades. Arash stayed behind the bench with his hands in his pockets and did not once glance at Venn. The arrangement Venn had built at the start of the hour came apart around them and stayed apart.

He sat down because his knees did it first.

Across the hall Suna spoke into the quiet. "She said it asleep. I'd never heard it from her awake. She was dreaming, and her hand was on her ear, and she said Nushi like she knew who she was calling."

Nessa pulled the baby tighter. Bram's mouth stayed open a moment before he shut it. Venn felt damp air on the sweat cooling under his shirt.

Gitta's thumbs were still circling each other. Fen Tesh watched the bench without blinking, shoulders drawn tight enough that even his next breath seemed delayed.

Bram cleared his throat. "Then let's be exact about what we're saying. Either the child made a sound that happened to resemble a word, or we're being asked to admit that something passed into her that no one can account for. Those are not the same claim."

For one breath Venn was grateful to him. A claim could be argued. A premise could be broken apart. Bram liked order almost as much as Venn did, only his kind lived in language instead of people.

"Belief is your own work," Fasa said. "This room is for naming what it has seen."

"What I've seen is an old woman crying and a child touching her ear."

Venn seized on the sentence even before Bram finished it. Yes. Keep it there. Keep it on what any pair of eyes could swear to. Tears, gesture, accident. Nothing that traveled. Nothing that joined.

Nessa's hand landed on Bram's forearm. He nodded once and shut his mouth on the rest, corrected by the touch before the room could do it for him.

"I'm not dismissing it," he said. "I'm asking what you expect us to do with it."

"Not turn away," Fasa said.

Venn stood again. The hall felt narrower than it had an hour earlier. Wet wool, lamp smoke, human breath, the smell of old people crying. He could feel the pressure of every face turning not toward Mava now but toward him, waiting to see whether the head of the Kondos would call the thing by a name he could never pull back.

"Turn away from what?" he said. "From my aunt grieving her sister? From a child not knowing what comes out of her own mouth? Iru is ours. Her death is ours. The road took her from our family, not from this room."

Deshi lifted her face to him. Her eyes were dry and steady.

"A dead woman's word is in my granddaughter's mouth," she said. "What should I do with that? Pretend I did not hear it? Pretend your aunt is not crying into my hands because no one living has said that word to her in forty years?" She looked at him the way she might have looked at a pot she knew had been left too long on the fire. "You are the man who likes order. Tell me the orderly response."

Heat ran under his collar. He bit the inside of his cheek hard enough to taste iron. The old machinery in him kicked for purchase anyway: routes, dowries, sleeping assignments, debt owed at a wedding, who carried which parcel, who answered at checkpoints if armed boys decided to make an afternoon of it. He knew how to solve those things. He knew how to make a family move through the world in one shape. This was not one of those things, and the uselessness of that fact landed in him like a slap.

"The Kondo family will speak on this privately," he said at last.

Arash looked away. The movement was small, but Venn saw it. Bram shifted and did not pick up the offer. Fasa remained still.

"Private speech made this room necessary," she said. "What hides in families comes here to be spoken where others can hear it."

Tila broke in before he could answer. "Enough. He's heard it. Mava's shaking. Let the old woman breathe."

Fasa's gaze moved to Olet. He sat as he had sat all morning, hands on knees, unreadable. After a moment she nodded.

"We stop here for today," she said.

Still no one scattered. Peyam rose from the Mera side with his beads looped over one wrist and crossed the hall to the bench. Habel made room. He sat beside Mava without a word, another old body bracing the space around her. Bett's eyes never left Mava. Hala leaned in and whispered something to her sister that Venn could not catch. Cade Leo turned fully toward Wynn for the first time all morning and said something low into his shoulder. Jin was the last of the untouched families to go; he paused in the doorway with Melie on his hip and looked back once before the rain took him out of sight.

When the hall had thinned, Mava spoke again.

"She was coming to visit me," she said. Her voice was rubbed raw. "Iru had jars of quince preserve wrapped in cloth under the seat because she knew I liked it and never made it right. She rubbed my ear when I was a child and when I was old enough to be ashamed of crying. She'd say Nushi and laugh at my face and I would stop. I still do it because of her." She pressed her fingertips to her ear and let them fall. "I just stopped doing it where people could see."

Deshi nodded once. "Then the word did not stay buried with her."

Habel kept her hand on Mava's back. Peyam bowed his head over his beads. Fasa let the sentence stand where it had fallen. Across the hall Bram pressed his lips together, argument trapped there. The sentence went into the stone with everything else that mattered in the room and stayed there.

Tila touched Venn's sleeve. "Come outside."

They stood under the kitchen overhang with rain dripping from the edge of the roof hard enough to splash their sandals. The banked fire smelled of wet ash. Beyond the courtyard wall the flood kept up its blunt roar.

For a while Tila said nothing. She let the rain fill the space and waited, knowing a question too soon would only lock him harder.

"The wedding is in nineteen days," Venn said.

"I know."

"The road is still gone."

"I know."

"And now this."

He lifted a hand toward the hall and let it drop. The word, the tears, Deshi crossing the room, Arash standing with his hands in his pockets while the Kondos came apart in public. None of it fit cleanly inside one sentence.

Inside the hall Mava's voice carried out in scraps, low and steady now, telling Deshi things he had never heard her tell him. He could feel the story leaving the family already, passing through the open doorway into common use.

Tila looked toward the sound. "Mava hasn't cried for Iru since the funeral."

Rain struck stone in quick hard beats. Venn could still see Deshi taking the floor from everyone else, the whole room holding still when he tried to drag it back under him.

"She's crying now," he said.

"Because somebody finally asked for the whole thing," Tila said. "Not the custodian. Deshi."

Venn turned to her. "And what do you think happens next? We stand them together once and then what? Fasa asks again. Olet listens. The room decides it has learned something about us that belongs in every mouth on the hill. By the time the road opens, the Kondos are not twelve people traveling to a wedding. We're a question."

Tila did not flinch. "No one said anything about breaking the family apart."

"They don't have to say it yet." He kept his voice low only because the walls were close. "On this hill they call it naming. Down there it's checkpoints and men with ledgers and the wrong answer at the wrong table. I need twelve bodies walking one road when the flood drops. I need one story, not seven families chewing on ours."

She watched him for a long moment. Rainwater dripped off the roof between them in a steady line.

"You're talking like Deshi came to steal Mava," she said.

"She crossed the hall and took my aunt's hands in front of everybody."

"She held them."

"In front of everybody."

Tila's mouth tightened, not with anger but with the weariness that came over her when he insisted on a distinction after the room had already moved past it.

"If you make a public fight out of a private comfort," she said, "you will lose both."

He looked away to the flood because looking at Tila meant admitting she had seen the room more clearly than he had. Beyond the courtyard, water shoved itself through the trees in one broad brown body. Somewhere under it were the switchbacks, the third bend, the convergence, the road that should have been carrying his family toward the wedding instead of trapping them in shrine law and other people's dead.

The noon meal bell sounded from the kitchen. Nobody hurried to it. That told him more than any argument could have.

Families came out of the hall in slow knots, speaking low. Bram and Nessa moved together for once, his hand at the baby's back while she talked into his shoulder. Hala Dao crossed to the cooking fire with Bett just behind her, both women already reaching for work because work kept the body from showing too much. The Leos kept close enough to touch. Suna emerged last among the Meras with Asha on her hip and Deshi not with her, which meant the old woman had chosen to remain behind with Mava after her own family rose to go. That detail lodged under Venn's ribs.

He went to the water jars and washed his hands longer than necessary. When he straightened, Deshi had appeared at the kitchen fire with one of the shrine bowls in her hands. She said something to the assistant at the pot, took a ladle of broth without waiting to be served, and crossed the courtyard as if the path were hers. Suna saw it and stopped dead. Deshi did not look at her.

Venn stepped into her line.

"The Kondo family will see to our own," he said.

Deshi stopped because his body forced the stop, not because his words had weight with her. The bowl steamed between her hands. Pepper and onion cut through the smell of rain.

"Then see to her after she drinks this," Deshi said. "Crying empties a body."

"You have said enough for one day."

At that she looked at him directly. There was no fear in it, and no ritual solemnity either. Just the flat attention of an old woman refusing a foolish obstacle in her kitchen.

"I did not cross that hall to claim your aunt," she said. "I crossed because no one else moved fast enough. Step aside."

Before he could answer, Tila took the bowl from Deshi's hands.

"Thank you," Tila said.

It was done so quickly he had nothing to oppose without making a spectacle of himself in the yard. Deshi gave one curt nod, as if a task had been passed from one competent pair of hands to another, and went back toward the Mera side. Suna was still standing where she had stopped. Deshi brushed past her without a word. Asha, riding on Suna's hip, twisted to watch Tila carry the bowl into the hall.

That small turning of the child's body felt worse than the bowl.

Venn called the Kondos to eat on the right side of the courtyard, away from the Meras, and they obeyed because habit still held where conviction had frayed. Ger brought the younger ones. Rem and Osh took the bowls. Arash fetched water without being asked. Habel stayed near Mava while she drank. Ori did not sit. She stood against Habel's leg and watched the doorway where Asha had vanished behind Suna's shoulder.

No one spoke much over the meal. The rain kept up. Spoons struck bowls. Somewhere on the far side of the overhang Bram started an argument too quietly to travel, and Nessa cut it off before it could rise. Bett and Hala worked through their food in practical silence. Fasa moved from family to family with the face she wore when she believed waiting was a kind of labor. Olet did not come out at all.

When Mava finished the broth, she set the bowl down and said, almost to herself, "She used to put too much pepper in everything. Iru. Said food should make you notice your own mouth."

Habel's head turned sharply toward her. Ori pressed closer.

Tila said, "Drink the rest while it's hot."

Venn looked across the courtyard. Deshi was not watching them. That made it worse. If she had been looking, he could have answered the look. Instead she was peeling a boiled yam for Kael with the same dry concentration she might have used any other day, as if carrying broth to Mava were already folded into the ordinary business of noon.

After the meal he gathered the Kondos into the sleeping quarters before the room could thin and reform around separate errands. The partition cloth dropped behind the last of them with a damp slap. Outside, the courtyard voices blurred together. Inside, the air smelled of wool, stored clothes, wet sandals, and the medicinal oil Habel rubbed on Mava's knees at night. Mats were stacked against the wall to make space. The younger ones hovered near Rem and Osh, close enough to listen and old enough to know listening was dangerous.

Venn stood where the entrance let him see all of them.
