"What happened in that hall does not alter who we are," he said. "When anyone here is asked about today, you keep to what can be said cleanly. Asha Mera used a word. Mava knew it. The rest is grief and a room making too much of it. We do not help the shrine build more from that than it has."

No one answered at first. Rain ticked against the roof tiles. Through the partition somebody laughed once in the Leo room and then stopped, as if they too had remembered where they were.

Habel was the first to speak. "Ori heard it too."

"Heard what?"

"Mava in the hall. Asha last night in the courtyard when Suna brought her over." Habel's hand stayed on Ori's shoulder. "She asked me if Aunt Iru found the wrong house."

The words landed in the cramped room and stayed there. One of the younger boys sucked in a breath. Tila shut her eyes for a beat.

"What did you tell her?" Venn asked.

"Nothing yet. I was waiting to know what to say."

"You tell her dead people stay dead," he said.

Ori looked at him with the naked concentration children had when they were trying to decide whether an adult had answered the right question. She did not cry. That steadiness unsettled him more than tears would have.

Mava spoke from her mat. Her voice had scraped itself raw in the hall and not recovered. "Dead people stay dead, yes. Their words don't always."

He turned to her. "A word does not make kin."

"No." She looked back at him without heat. "But hearing it in that child's mouth did not feel like nothing either."

"Feeling is not the measure."

"It is when the feeling is grief," Tila said quietly.

Venn ignored her because if he answered Tila first the room would hear where the stronger resistance sat.

"We are not here to be measured by grief," he said to Mava. "We are here to get off this hill in one piece and continue to the wedding."

Arash, who had been leaning against the rear wall with his arms folded, said, "Nobody's stopping us from leaving when the road opens."

Venn turned too fast. "That is exactly what I am trying to prevent."

"By telling Mava what she can hear?"

"By keeping forty-three people from deciding our family can be talked into pieces."

Arash pushed off the wall. "Standing next to the Meras in one hall isn't the same as breaking the family apart."

"Not in one hall," Venn said. "In seven more conversations after. In the road crew's questions. In checkpoint lines. In whatever story the custodians think they have earned the right to tell about us before we are gone."

Arash's face sharpened, not with open defiance but with the effort not to show it. "You think some soldier in the lowlands is going to ask whether a four-year-old said an old woman's word in her sleep?"

"I think men with guns make trouble out of whatever lies nearest to hand," Venn said. "And I think a family travels better when it does not hand strangers extra material."

Rem shifted with one of the younger girls pressed to his side. Osh stared at the floor. Ger looked from Venn to Arash and back again, already measuring where the force in the room would land. Venn hated that look. He knew it because he had worn it in his own father's house.

Mava pulled the blanket higher over her knees.

"You keep saying ours as if that settles it," she said.

"It does settle who Iru belonged to."

"Iru did not belong." She coughed once into the heel of her hand, then went on. "She was my sister. She married where she pleased. She died on the road. Now a child says her word, and you want to cork it because you are afraid of what other people might do with it."

The nakedness of the sentence made the younger ones look down.

"Yes," Venn said. There was no point pretending otherwise. "I am afraid of what other people might do with it. That is my job."

Silence after that. He heard the truth of the admission even as he made it and disliked the sound of it in the room. Not because it was false, but because it gave away more than he intended.

Tila sat on the edge of the rolled bedding and folded the empty broth cloth in her lap.

"Protection isn't the same thing as shutting every door," she said.

"Sometimes it is."

"Not this one."

He looked at her. Tila almost never crossed him in front of the younger ones. When she did, it was because she had already decided the cost of silence was worse.

"You saw the room," he said. "You think they will leave this alone?"

"No," she said. "I think they saw an old woman say her dead sister's word and another old woman go sit with her. I think they will keep talking because that is what people do when they are trapped together. You cannot stop that by telling Mava to call it coincidence."

At the edge of the room Ori finally spoke, so softly he almost missed it.

"Was Aunt Iru scared?"

Habel bent toward her, but Mava answered first.

"Yes," she said. "I think she was."

"Did it hurt?"

Mava closed her eyes. Venn felt the whole room waiting on the answer, even the youngest ones with no clean grasp of what had been asked.

"Yes," Mava said again. "The cart broke under her. The hill cut her up. It hurt."

There was no point in softening it. Venn was almost grateful for that. At least pain, once named, stayed in the body where it belonged.

Ori pressed her face into Habel's hip.

Venn said, more harshly than he intended, "Enough."

The partition cloth lifted before anyone could obey. Deshi stood in the doorway with Peyam just behind her, beads looped over one wrist, both of them framed by the gray light from the hall.

For a second the room held perfectly still.

Deshi looked from Venn to Mava to the children crowded around Habel and did not bother pretending she had arrived at a convenient moment.

"I brought the shawl she left on the bench," she said.

The blue wool was folded over her arm. Mava's, not hers. Venn had not even noticed it was missing.

"Leave it there," he said.

Deshi ignored him and crossed to Mava's mat. Peyam stayed by the doorway like a witness who had no intention of speaking unless asked.

"You should have it before the damp gets in your shoulders," Deshi said, laying the shawl over Mava's knees.

Mava touched the edge of it. "Thank you."

Deshi straightened with a hand at her own back. Age showed in the movement for the first time all day. Then she looked at Venn.

"Peyam wants to sit with her after evening prayer if she allows it," she said. "If she wants quiet, we'll leave her quiet. If she wants to talk, she'll talk."

"We've had enough visitors."

"That is not your choice alone."

It was the calmness of the sentence that struck hardest. No shout. No sanctimony. Just flat refusal to let him make the room smaller than it now was.

Mava said, before he could answer, "He can come."

Peyam bowed his head once in acceptance, as if this had been the entire matter.

Deshi looked at Venn again. "I did not cross that hall to steal anybody. I crossed because she broke open in front of me. There is no law worth much that asks a person to step back from that."

Then she turned and went out. Peyam followed her. The partition fell closed behind them.

No one spoke for several seconds. Rain thudded on the roof. Outside, somebody called for a missing cup.

Venn had the absurd urge to go after Deshi and drag the argument into the courtyard where it could at least belong to one afternoon instead of seeping into the whole hill. But the children had heard enough, and rage performed in front of children was its own kind of surrender. He knew that from his father too.

"Rest," he said at last to Mava. "All of you who can, rest."

It was not an ending. It was only the least broken order he could still give.

The room loosened without returning to normal. Rem took the younger ones to the far mat with a game of knucklebones and too-bright gentleness. Osh ducked out to fetch dry wood for the evening fire. Ger sat near the doorway and stared through the partition cloth as if trying to see the shape of the conversations happening beyond it. Habel eased Ori down beside Mava and stroked the girl's hair until her breathing slowed. Arash remained standing.

Venn waited until the younger ones had drifted far enough into their own noises that the room would not remember every word.

"You undermined me in there," he said.

Arash did not pretend to misunderstand. "I stood with Mava."

"You stood with your hands in your pockets while the room looked at our family as if it had come half unstitched."

"Because it had."

The answer landed so cleanly Venn almost missed its force.

"Watch yourself," he said.

Arash's jaw flexed. "I am."

"Then act like a man with a road ahead of him."

"I do."

"Do you?" Venn stepped closer, keeping his voice low enough that it would not spill to the children. "There is a house waiting on the wedding road. There are people there taking this delay as misfortune, not insult. Every day the road stays closed, we spend more of that good will. Every hour this hill teaches people to talk about us as if we belong to their stories, we spend more. You think that does not matter because you do not carry it."

Arash did not step back. "I carry plenty."

"Not enough."

For a second Venn thought the boy would finally raise his voice. Instead Arash spoke even more quietly.

"You keep saying wedding as if the word itself clears mud," he said. "It doesn't. And shouting over Mava won't either."

"I was not shouting at Mava."

"The room couldn't tell the difference."

That one got through. Venn felt it in his chest, a physical blow from the child he had taught to stand straight, to keep count, to answer for the family when called.

"What do you believe happened?" he asked.

Arash rubbed a hand over his mouth. "I believe Asha said a word she shouldn't know. I believe Mava heard it. I believe Deshi heard enough to move. That's already more trouble than I know what to do with."

"That is not an answer."

"It's the only honest one I've got."

Honest. The word made Venn want to strike something. As if honesty were simple. As if keeping twelve people moving toward a future required less truth than standing in a hall and naming whatever broke your heart.

He looked away first. That, too, was a small defeat, but the room was full of them now and one more scarcely changed the shape of the day.

By late afternoon the rain eased to a fine steady mist. The courtyard darkened early under the weather. Supper was thinner than noon, the grain stretched with more water than anyone admitted. Venn ate it sitting beside Tila while the children leaned heavy with exhaustion and the adults kept their voices down out of habit, not peace. Deshi and Peyam did come after evening prayer. They sat with Mava near the back wall of the hall, and nobody stopped them. Suna kept Asha to the far side and did not once bring the child near, but once, when the little girl twisted to look toward Mava, Deshi's face changed in a way Venn could not read. Not tenderness exactly. Recognition, maybe, and alarm riding under it.

The hour after supper should have belonged to the winding down of a normal day: washing bowls, settling mats, the final checking of latches though there was nothing to lock. Instead the whole compound carried the tautness of a house after somebody had slapped somebody else at a feast. No one wanted another scene. No one believed the scene was over.

Venn went back to the courtyard wall when the lamps were lit. The mist silvered in the dark beyond the eaves. Down below, the flood sounded wider at night. He could no longer see the gauging stone, only the broad, wrong sheen where the road ought to be.

After a while Arash came and stood beside him. Not close enough to touch. Not far enough to call it avoidance.

For several breaths they listened to the water.

"Nineteen days," Venn said.

Arash did not pretend not to know what he meant. "I heard you the first time."

"Did you."

"Yes."

Venn kept his eyes on the valley. "When the road opens, we move that morning. No delays. No extra visits. No more of this room making a lesson out of us."

Arash was quiet long enough that Venn turned to look at him.

"If the road opens, we move," Arash said.

"When."

Arash did not correct him. That small mercy irritated Venn almost as much as contradiction would have.

"When," Arash said.

From inside the hall came the low braid of voices that had haunted the whole day: Mava rough and thinning with fatigue, Deshi sharp and dry, Peyam's deeper murmur only now and then. They were not arguing. They were doing something worse in Venn's mind. They were getting familiar.

"You hear that?" Venn said.

"Yes."

"That is exactly what I mean."

Arash leaned his forearms on the wall. "You mean people talking."

"I mean my aunt's grief becoming common property."

"Maybe grief doesn't stay private just because you order it to."

Venn let the sentence sit between them. There were answers to it, dozens of them. None felt solid enough to use. He had spent the day learning how quickly a room could make his best language sound like stubbornness dressed in family duty.

"When I was twelve," he said, surprising himself, "I helped wash Iru's foot."

Arash turned. In the lamp spill his face lost some of its hardness and showed its age again, which was to say not enough.

Venn went on because once he had started, stopping would have made the admission theatrical, and he hated theater.

"No one told me to leave the room. They were too busy. Mud was caked up under her heel. I remember thinking if I got the mud off, she'd look less dead." He swallowed. "That was forty years ago and I can still feel the skin."

Arash said nothing. Venn was grateful for that too. Sympathy from his son would have been harder to bear than silence.

"So do not tell me I am speaking from distance," Venn said. "I know exactly what road that was. I know what it took from Mava. I know what it took from the family. I will not stand here and hand it over because a child repeated a word."

Arash looked back toward the hall. "Nobody can take Iru from us."

"You say that because you have not spent your life learning how easily people take what is not theirs and call it a new order."

The political trouble in the lowlands had made that lesson plain in a hundred ways: men reassigned to roads they never chose, shops shut for lacking the right paper, boys held overnight because a name was misspelled somewhere upstream of a checkpoint. Venn did not have to explain the rest. Arash had seen enough of the same world to follow the line.

Still, his son said, "This isn't a checkpoint."

"No. It is worse. A checkpoint only wants an answer. This place wants confession."

Arash gave the smallest shake of his head. "Maybe it wants witness."

Venn almost laughed at that, but there was no humor in him.

"Witness is what people call it when they approve of what is being done," he said.

Neither of them spoke after that. The flood kept roaring. In the hall, Mava's voice dropped low enough to lose the words, but not the fact of being heard. Somewhere a child coughed in sleep. Tila crossed the courtyard once with folded blankets in her arms and did not interrupt them.

At last Arash said, "I'm going in."

Venn nodded without turning. Arash waited half a second, maybe for something more, then went.

Venn stayed at the wall until the lamps in the hall burned lower. He counted without looking, the old habit moving in him on its own: Tila in the sleeping room, Mava under her blanket, Habel and Ori beside her, Ger, Arash, Rem, Osh, the younger ones settled in a row. There were still twelve Kondos under one roof. He could reach the number cleanly every time.

From inside, Mava's voice carried once more through the thin partition, and another family's old woman answered as if she had every right. By tomorrow the whole hill would still be turning it over in low voices, the same way they worried floodwater and road crews and any other trouble that looked built to last.


# Chapter 6

The next afternoon, the children had taken over the lower terrace.

Jin watched from the kitchen overhang where he'd been scrubbing pots for the past hour — volunteer work nobody argued about, because it kept him useful and silent and away from the naming hall where the adults were still sorting through whatever had happened yesterday between Mava Kondo and Suna Mera's mother-in-law. He didn't want to know. He didn't want to have an opinion about it. He wanted to scrub the black crust off the rice pot and watch his daughter.

Melie was in the middle of the group, which was new. For the first four days she'd circled the edges, watching the Kondo children and the Mera boy with the cautious hunger Jin recognized because he'd felt it at her age — wanting to be included but not knowing how to step in. She'd stood near Rimi Mera once, close enough that the twelve-year-old had to notice her, and Rimi had handed her a smooth pebble without looking down, an offhand kindness that Melie had carried in her pocket for two days afterward, turning it over and over until the warmth of her hand was indistinguishable from the warmth of the stone.

Now she sat cross-legged next to Nori Dao, the two of them arranging sticks in a pattern that seemed to involve rules Jin couldn't follow. Nori was a year older and a head taller and kept rearranging Melie's sticks without asking, and Melie — his Melie, who had spent three months going quiet at the table, who had stopped singing in the bath, who had developed the habit of checking the front door whenever she heard footsteps — didn't object. She just waited for Nori to finish correcting her placement and then added her next stick with careful concentration. The patience was new. Or not new. Recovered. She'd been a patient child before Reva left.

Jin's hands stopped in the water. He made himself keep scrubbing. The pot was copper, dented at the rim, and the burnt rice came away in black flakes that dissolved between his fingers. He pressed his thumb against the metal hard enough to whiten the nail. The water was cold from the cistern, cold enough that his knuckles ached, and he pressed harder.

Reva had walked out on a Tuesday. He remembered the day because she'd left her lunch in the fridge — the container she packed every morning, the one with the cracked blue lid — and he'd come home from the site and seen it sitting there on the shelf and known before he checked the closet, before he found the note, before he counted what was missing. She'd left her lunch. She hadn't planned on going that morning. Or she had planned it and then still packed her lunch out of habit and then left it there and walked out anyway, and he didn't know which version was worse — the impulsive abandonment or the deliberate one that was so rehearsed she could go through the motions of staying even while she was leaving.

He'd told Melie that her mother had died. He'd told her that evening, quickly, before anyone else could tell her something different, because Reva's sister knew and her sister's husband knew and Jin could already see the machinery of explanation gearing up around him, the concerned faces and the careful words and the particular head-tilt of pity that precedes unsolicited advice. He'd needed to control the story. A dead mother was clean. A dead mother couldn't come back. A dead mother wasn't a woman who looked at her daughter and her husband and their kitchen with the cracked blue container still in the fridge and decided she wanted something else.

The shame of it lived in where other people's eyes went first: the sink, the unmade bed, Melie's shoes by the door. Men whose wives died got casseroles, soft voices, neighbors who checked in and brought extra food on weekends. Men whose wives left got the quick sideways look, the change of subject, the pause before sympathy turned into appraisal. He'd watched it happen in the first week after Reva walked out, before he'd settled on the lie, when his brother-in-law stood in the kitchen doorway and let his gaze move over the room before it landed on Jin. Maybe they were right to look. A woman had looked at this life and gone.

He pulled the pot from the basin and set it upside down on the stone ledge to dry. His hands were red and his knuckles were swelling slightly from the cold. Below him, on the terrace, Melie laughed.

The sound went through him like a needle. She was laughing at something Nori had done — knocked over the entire stick arrangement, maybe, or said something in the blunt way that Nori had, a seven-year-old's declarative certainty about the world that seemed to delight Melie in a way that adult gentleness never did. Jin watched his daughter's face: open, unguarded, the gap where her front teeth used to be visible because she was laughing with her whole mouth. She hadn't laughed like that since before Reva left. Since before the note, the empty closet, the lie.

She was thriving here. In this stone compound surrounded by strangers who asked too many questions and custodians who rang a bell for every meal and prayer and an old woman who wanted to know the maiden name of his dead wife. She was thriving because there were other children and because no one here knew the hole in her life well enough to poke at it, and the story Jin had told at the naming — my wife passed, we're traveling to her family — sat over the truth like a lid, letting Melie move through this temporary world without the particular cruelty of being the girl whose mother chose to leave.

She was the girl whose mother chose to leave. He couldn't even think the words without his mouth bracing for the catch before the consonant. He pressed his teeth together and waited for it to pass. It always passed. It had been doing this since he was twelve, long before Reva, long before any of this, and it always passed.

He stacked the clean pots on the ledge, dried his hands on the cloth hanging from the overhang post, and stood watching his daughter play until the light shifted and the shadows of the compound walls reached the lower terrace and the children began to scatter toward their families.

---

Bett Dao was at the cistern when Jin brought Melie down to wash her hair before the evening meal.

She was filling a clay jug, one hand braced on the stone basin's lip, and she straightened when she heard them on the steps. Her sleeves were rolled to her elbows in precise folds, each cuff flattened to the same width with a thumbnail somewhere on the way up. It was tidiness as reflex, not vanity. She wore a thin brass ring on her right hand, tarnished nearly black, that she turned with her thumb while she waited for the jug to fill. She nodded at him — the same flat acknowledgment she gave everyone, not unfriendly but not an opening — and went back to her water.

"Papa, it's cold," Melie said, touching the surface with one finger and pulling it back.

"I know. It's always cold." He'd washed her hair here twice since they'd arrived, both times in the late afternoon when the stone basin caught the last warmth from the sun. The water came from a spring somewhere beneath the shrine, cold even in monsoon heat, and Melie complained about it every time, affronted all over again when her finger hit the surface and found the warning had told only half the truth.

"Nori says her aunt washes her hair with soap flowers," Melie said, looking at Bett's back. "She says they make your hair smell like the garden."

"We don't have soap flowers."

"Maybe Bett has some."

"We're not going to bother her. Come here."

Jin cupped water in both hands and brought it to Melie's head. She ducked and squealed and he caught her by the shoulder, firm but careful, the grip of a parent whose child was always about to dart away from the one thing that needed doing. "Hold still. I'll be fast."

The water ran through her dark hair and down the back of her neck. She shivered and he felt the shiver travel up through his hand into his wrist. Her hair was getting long — past her shoulders now, the longest it had ever been. Reva had always kept it trimmed, sitting Melie on a kitchen stool with a towel around her shoulders and cutting with the good scissors, the ones with the orange handles that were still in the drawer at home. Jin didn't know how to cut hair evenly. Melie didn't seem to mind the length, or if she minded she hadn't said, and he hadn't asked because asking might surface the question of why her mother wasn't here to do it.

He worked the soap through her hair — a brown block the custodians provided, harsh and unscented, dragging the oil out until the strands squeaked between his fingers — his thumbs moving along her scalp in the pattern he'd learned from watching Reva do this. His hands remembered the small circles. The gentleness required at the temples where the skin was thin. His hands had learned this from a woman who was alive and somewhere else, living a life that didn't include the small warm skull under his fingers, and his hands did it anyway because muscle memory doesn't care about betrayal.

"Papa."

"Hmm."

"Is Nori's mum here?"

His fingers went still in her soapy hair. "I'm not sure about their family," he said. He didn't know the Daos' arrangement beyond the obvious — Bett and her older sister Hala, Sem and Raya usually with Hala, Nori orbiting Bett so naturally that Jin had stopped trying to sort out how the household had been arranged before they all got stranded here. He hadn't asked, because asking invited questions in return, and questions were the one thing he couldn't afford.

Bett was still at the basin, her jug full now, but she hadn't moved to leave. She was listening — not obviously, her body angled away, her free hand worrying a thread loose from her sleeve cuff — but listening in the way Jin recognized because he did it himself: face turned from the conversation, attention fixed on every word.

"That's me," Bett said, without turning around. Her voice was even, unperformative. "Nori's mine. Hala's my sister."

"Oh." Melie's face was upside down, looking up at Jin through the fall of her wet hair. "Then Hala's the aunt with the soap flowers?"

"Yeah," Bett said. "That's Hala."

Jin reached for the tin cup by the basin and rinsed Melie's hair, pouring with one hand and shielding her eyes with the other, and Melie endured it with the stoic misery of a child who has learned that protest doesn't shorten the process.

Bett had turned and was watching them. Not watching — something less intrusive. She held the jug on her hip, elbow locked beneath the weight, and her face, which Jin had only ever seen from across the hall as a guarded surface, was doing something he hadn't expected. Her free hand lifted an inch before she stopped it, two fingers already shaped to rake soap away from a child's eyes. Then she pinned that hand back against the jug hard enough that water lipped over the rim and ran across her wrist. She kept looking at Melie's wet hair and Jin's careful hands until the spill reached her cuff.

Then she turned and climbed the steps with the jug on her hip, and the moment closed behind her.

Jin wrung Melie's hair between his palms and twisted it into the rough shape of a braid. He didn't know how to braid properly. Another thing Reva had done. Another skill he'd never learned because he'd assumed she would always be there to do it, and the assumption had felt so solid, so much like ground, that he'd never noticed he was standing on something that could move.

"Come on," he said. "Dinner soon."

Melie took his hand. Her fingers were cold and small and damp, and she swung his arm as they climbed the steps, and Jin held on.

---

The evening meal was set out on the stone ledge that served as the communal table — flatbreads stacked under a cloth, a lentil pot still bubbling over the last of the coals, rice with fried shallots, and three small bowls of condiments the Dao women had prepared from their own stores. Jin knew the Daos had been on kitchen rotation because Hala had been there all afternoon, her broad back bent over the cutting board, humming something under her breath while she worked, filling the kitchen with sound the way other people filled it with talk. Bett had been grinding spices in the stone mortar when Jin had passed through with Melie on the way to the cistern, her wrist turning in steady circles while the stone gave back a wet rasp under each pass, her face absent and focused at the same time, as if the mortar were holding a thought steady for her.

The children ate together, as they had since the third or fourth day — a cluster of bodies on the lower step, plates balanced on knees, the older ones helping the younger ones without being told. Rimi Mera tore flatbread for Asha, who was sitting in Rimi's lap, four years old and unbothered by the fact that she was using a stranger's twelve-year-old as furniture. Kael held his own plate with the defensive grip of an eight-year-old who has been told he is old enough to manage. The Kondo children ate in their own tight group — Venn's influence, visible even at mealtimes in the way they kept their elbows close and their voices down — but Ori, the youngest at nine, kept glancing over at the other children, then back to her own plate, then over again, as if the space between the groups might bite.

Melie sat between Nori and Sem Dao. Sem was eleven, quiet, with the same compact build as his aunt Bett and the same economy of movement — he did things once and correctly, without wasted effort, as though he'd been taught that resources were finite and motion was a resource. He'd started saving a spot for Melie at meals without being asked, a small territorial gesture that Melie accepted without comment, the way children accept kindness they haven't been taught to question.

Jin ate standing, leaning against the kitchen overhang's wall, tearing bread and scooping lentils without tasting either. He'd taken some of the fried shallot rice and a smear of the brown-gold paste from one of the small bowls — rich, sweet, with a depth he vaguely identified as nut-based. He ate mechanically. The food was good. The Daos could cook. He chewed and swallowed and watched his daughter.

Melie was eating with both hands on her bread, tearing and dipping with the focused intensity of a child who takes food seriously. She dipped her torn bread into the brown-gold paste, took a large bite, chewed, dipped again. Nori was telling her something about a game they would play tomorrow, and Melie nodded while she chewed, her attention split between the conversation and the food with the particular greed of a six-year-old who hasn't fully learned to pretend she isn't hungry.

She paused. Her hand stopped halfway to her mouth. She looked at the bread in her fingers as if she'd never seen bread before.

"Melie?" Jin set his plate on the ledge.

She scratched her neck. Then her forearm. Both movements quick and distracted, the way you scratch at a mosquito bite without deciding to. She took another bite. She chewed. She scratched her neck again, harder.

"What's wrong?" He was already moving toward her, not running, but the five meters between the kitchen wall and the lower step felt like a distance he couldn't cross fast enough, each stride landing on stone that seemed to stretch.

"Itchy," Melie said. She scratched her forearm and Jin saw the skin — raised, blotchy, a red rash spreading from her wrist to the inside of her elbow in the time it took him to reach her.

He knelt beside her. "Show me."

She held up both arms. The rash was on both now, blooming outward from the insides of her elbows, welts rising under the skin like something pushing up from beneath. Her face was changing — the skin around her mouth puffing, her lower lip thickening, a flush climbing her cheeks that had nothing to do with the evening heat. She scratched at her throat with both hands.

"I feel funny," she said. Her voice was wrong. Thicker, squeezed, a six-year-old's voice forced through a narrowing passage.

Jin grabbed her plate. Bread, lentils, the brown-gold paste smeared across the edge. He looked at what she'd eaten and didn't know what was wrong and the not-knowing opened a hole in his chest that felt exactly like falling because he was her father and he was supposed to know. "What did you eat? Melie, what did you eat just now?"

"The bread. And the—" She coughed. A small cough, nothing, except beneath it was a wheeze Jin had never heard from her, a high thin scraping sound like air pulled through wet cloth. She coughed again. Her eyes were watering.

"Melie." He pulled her into his arms. She was light — she'd always been small for six, small enough that strangers still asked if she was five — and her body was hot against his chest, hotter than the evening warranted, and the rash was spreading down her neck now, red welts rising on skin he'd been washing with his own hands twenty minutes ago. "Breathe. Just breathe for me."

She tried. The wheeze was louder. Her fingers went to her throat, scratching, pulling at the skin as if she could widen the passage by hand.

"Something's wrong with her." He said it to the courtyard, to everyone, to anyone. Adults were turning. Suna had already stood from where she'd been eating with Deshi and the children. Hala was coming out of the kitchen, wiping her hands on her apron, her face shifting from confusion to something faster than confusion. "Something's wrong — she ate something, I don't know what—"

"Let me see." Bett was beside him. She'd been sitting four meters away with Nori, eating from her own plate, and now she was here and he hadn't seen her cross the space. Her hand went to Melie's face — not gently, efficiently. Chin up. Lips checked. Two fingers under the angle of Melie's jaw where the swelling was worst.

"Her throat's closing," Bett said. Her voice dropped, and the words still cut clean through the courtyard. "What did she eat?"

"Bread, lentils, the—"

Bett picked up Melie's plate. She touched the smear of paste with her finger and brought it to her nose. "Cashew," she said.

"She's eaten cashews before. She eats them all the time, she's never—"

"Not this." Bett set the plate down. She was moving fast now, her body stripped of everything that wasn't functional, her hands already reaching behind her as if she expected something to be placed in them. She looked at Melie's swelling face and something in her locked into place — not coldness, the opposite. "Hala. The kit. Go."

Hala ran. Not walked, not hurried — ran, her feet slapping the stone, back into the kitchen area where the Daos kept their things, and the sound of containers being shoved aside carried across the courtyard.

"What's happening to her?" Jin's voice broke on the second word. Melie was wheezing against his chest, each breath a whistle through a gap that was narrowing by the second, and her fingers were clawing at her throat hard enough to leave red marks on skin that was already welted, and her eyes were swollen to slits and he could feel her ribs heaving against his forearms, each inhalation pulling her ribcage wide as her body fought for air that wasn't coming fast enough.

"Anaphylaxis." Bett said the word like it had been worn smooth from use. "Tree-nut allergy. Cashew. It's our family's allergy. Sem has it. My son had it."

The words didn't arrange themselves into sense. His daughter was choking in his arms and this woman he'd spoken to three times in a week was saying something about families and allergies and a son, and Melie was making a sound now — a high, thin, aspirated whistle on each inhale, air forced through a space the width of a straw — and the sound was the most terrifying thing Jin had ever heard because it was the sound of something that could stop.

"Hold her still." Bett took Melie from his arms. She didn't ask. One arm under Melie's back, the other supporting her head to keep the airway as straight as possible, she lifted her in a single clean motion and set her on the stone step, propped against her own bent knee. "Melie, I know it's scary. Don't scratch. Look at me."

Melie looked at her. Her eyes were wet and swollen and terrified, her face a stranger's face, puffed and distorted, and her hands were still going for her throat. Bett caught both small wrists in one hand and held them against Melie's chest. "You're going to breathe. It's tight but you're going to breathe. Look at me."

Hala came back with a tin box — battered, scratched, a dent in the lid that had been hammered mostly flat. Bett opened it one-handed, her other hand still holding Melie's wrists. Inside: a small glass vial and a syringe wrapped in white cloth. The cloth was clean and carefully ironed, the creases sharp, and this was the detail that would stay with Jin afterward — the ironed cloth, the exact folds, the way the battered tin had been kept as if order itself could hold a throat open.

Bett drew the dose with the same economy she brought to everything else. She glanced once at the vial and filled the syringe without pausing. The dosage seemed to live in her fingers, not in her head.

"Epinephrine," she said to Jin. She didn't look at him. Her eyes were on Melie's face, tracking the pull above her collarbones, the grey at her lips, the beat between one whistle and the next. She flicked the barrel, pushed a clear drop through the needle, and set two fingers on Melie's thigh exactly where the drug had to go, as if she'd measured that spot a hundred times. "It'll open her airway. Her heart's going to race. It'll scare her but it's the drug working."

"Do it." He didn't know what epinephrine was. He didn't care what it was. Melie was wheezing and her lips were going grey and her fingernails were digging into Bett's palm where Bett held her wrists and Jin would have let this woman pour fire into his daughter's body if it meant the whistling would stop.

Bett pushed Melie's shirt up and exposed her outer thigh — the skin there still clear of rash, pale and goosebumped in the cooling air. She drove the needle in. No flinch, no tentative pause. She pressed the plunger with the steady force of practice, and then she held the needle in place and counted — silently, her lips moving, her thumb keeping the muscle steady while the drug entered.

She withdrew the needle and set the syringe on the stone. "Now we wait."

The courtyard was quiet. Forty-odd people, some standing and some still sitting with plates in their laps, watching. Suna had her hand on Asha's head, holding her daughter against her hip. Hala stood behind Bett with her arms crossed over her chest, her face tight and furious with worry. The Kondo children had stopped eating. Old Peyam had put down his pipe. Nori Dao was standing exactly where Melie had been sitting a minute ago, holding the plate Melie had been eating from, her face white and absolutely still. She knew what this was. She had grown up knowing.

Melie breathed. The whistle was still there. Bett's palm was flat between Melie's shoulder blades, feeling each breath — checking the vibration, the depth, the resistance, the way you'd feel a pipe for blockage, your hand reading what your ears couldn't measure. Twenty seconds. Thirty. Jin counted with her, or tried to, but the numbers kept slipping because his hands were shaking and his jaw was locked and the sound of that whistle was still there, still narrowing, still the width of a straw—

And then it changed. Loosened. Deepened. The sound of a passage widening by millimeters, the wheeze dropping half a tone, and Bett's hand went still on Melie's back.

"There," Bett said.

Melie took a breath that sounded like breathing. Then another. The swelling around her eyes was still bad — her face still looked distorted, unfamiliar — but the color was coming back into her lips and her breathing was slowing from panicked shallow gasps to something Jin's body recognized before his mind did: she was getting air. She was going to keep getting air.

"Papa," Melie said. Her voice was hoarse and her eyes were still nearly shut. "My heart's going really fast."

"I know. That's the medicine. It's supposed to do that." He was repeating what Bett had told him. He was reaching for Melie, pulling her from Bett's knee into his own arms, and she came — hot and shaking and alive, her heart hammering against his chest through both their shirts, her breathing ragged but open, open, the air going in and coming out. He pressed his face into her damp hair and smelled the harsh soap from the cistern and the faint residual sweetness of the cashew paste and his own sweat, and he held her, and his hands were shaking so badly that he could feel them vibrating against her back like something mechanical.

"She can't eat tree nuts," Bett said. She was still sitting on the step, her hands on her thighs, the syringe on the stone beside her. The ironed cloth had come open and the glass vial caught the last daylight, a small bright point. Her voice was steady but her jaw was set in a way that Jin recognized as effort — steadiness as a choice, not a condition. "Cashews, pistachios, walnuts. Almonds are sometimes safe but I wouldn't test it here. Not without a clinic."

"She's never—" Jin's jaw locked. The consonant caught and held, the old hesitation, and for a moment he couldn't make the word come out. He pressed his mouth against Melie's hair and waited for his throat to release. "She's never been allergic. To anything. She eats everything."

"She does now," Bett said.

Its flatness startled him. It wasn't cruel. It wasn't bewildered. It wasn't wrapped in any of the language the compound had been generating for a week about manifestations and connections and what the naming revealed about families. It was just the fact, set down on the stone beside the syringe and the vial and the ironed cloth: your daughter has this thing now. It is real. It will not go away because it shouldn't be there.

Hala knelt beside Bett and put a hand on her sister's shoulder. Bett didn't lean into the touch. She didn't acknowledge it except by not pulling away. She sat on the stone step with Hala's hand on her shoulder and kept her eyes on the spot on Melie's thigh where the needle had entered, waiting through another full count after the wheeze had gone, as if the danger might turn around and come back. Only then did she reach for the syringe. She recapped it without looking, wrapped the vial in the ironed cloth twice, and pinched the fold flat with her thumbnail before closing the tin. Jin understood, without knowing the details, that none of those movements were new.

"I've got one more dose," Bett said. She nodded at the tin. "After that we'd need to get to a clinic, and the road's not open."

"What if it happens again?"

"Don't let her eat tree nuts."

"I don't know what has tree nuts in it." His voice was rising and he couldn't stop it — the flood, the loss of volume control, the shame of being beaten by a bowl of paste at dinner. "I've never had to know. She ate cashews at home, I gave her cashews, I put them in her — she's eaten them her whole—"

"So come to me before she eats." Bett stood. She brushed the stone dust from her knees — one brisk pass of each hand, no fuss. The syringe went back into the tin, the vial beside it, the cloth folded over both with the same careful creases. She closed the lid and tucked the tin under her arm. "I've been managing this allergy for eleven years. Sem's had it since he was born. I can tell you what's safe and what's not."

Jin stared at her. He was holding Melie against his chest and his daughter's heart was still hammering and this woman was offering to manage what he couldn't manage, to know what he didn't know, and the offer was made without softness, without the gentle tone people used when they felt sorry for you. It was made the way you'd hand someone a tool: here, you need this, take it.

"Why does she have your family's allergy?" He didn't mean it as accusation. He meant it as the question it was — the thing he could not hold inside his body, because his daughter had just had her throat close from a condition she didn't have yesterday, a condition that belonged to strangers, and a woman he barely knew had saved her with a needle and a counted silence and hands that knew exactly where to push the plunger.

Bett looked at him. The guarded surface was back — the face she showed the compound, economical, closed. But the muscles around her eyes were tight in a way that had nothing to do with composure and everything to do with something she was choosing not to say.

"I don't know why," she said. "I just know what it looks like."

She climbed the steps. Hala followed, her hand briefly touching the back of Bett's arm as they went — a sisterly gesture, practiced and unremarkable, the habit of two women who had been looking after each other for a long time. Nori was still holding Melie's plate. Bett took it from her daughter's hands as she passed — gently, the way you take something with an edge from a child — and set it face-down on the ledge where no one else would eat from it.

The courtyard emptied in stages. The crisis was already becoming the thing people would talk about in low voices after the children slept — did you see it, how fast, the Dao woman's kit, how did she know. Suna caught Jin's eye as she passed with Asha on her hip and Rimi trailing behind. She didn't speak. Her gaze moved from Melie to the food ledge to Asha's face and back again, already recalculating — her children, the food, what else might change without warning, whether the world she thought she understood had shifted again beneath all of them.

Jin sat on the step with Melie in his arms. Her heart was still going too fast. He could feel it through her ribs, a hummingbird tremor against his chest, and he kept hearing Bett's voice — it's the drug working, it'll scare her but it's working — and repeating it to himself as if the repetition could reach whatever part of him was still falling through the hole that had opened when he heard that first wheeze. Her breathing was steady now. Regular. The swelling was going down, slowly, the puffiness around her eyes receding enough that she looked like herself again — the gap teeth, the dark hair he'd just washed, the chin that was Reva's chin. He saw it every time. Every single time.

"Papa," she said. Her voice was scratchy and small. "My throat still feels weird."

"It'll get better. The medicine's still working."

"How did she know what to do?"

"Because someone in her family has the same thing you have."

"But I don't have it. I ate cashews at Auntie's house. I ate them loads of times."

"I know."

"So why did my throat do that?"

He didn't have an answer. He kept one hand spread between her shoulder blades and felt each breath scrape in, pause, and come back out.

"I don't know," he said.

Melie pressed her face into his shirt and didn't ask again.

The custodians' bell rang for evening prayers. The compound moved around them. Somewhere above, Bett's voice carried briefly — saying something to Nori, ordinary words, a mother's evening instructions — and then the compound settled into its nightly routine of mats and lamps and the sound of rain beginning again on stone.

Later, when Jin carried Melie to their sleeping space and laid her down and sat beside her with his back against the wall, he found the tin box on the edge of their mat. Bett had left it without telling him. No note, no instructions, no explanation. Just the dented tin with the ironed cloth inside and the single remaining dose of the drug that would keep his daughter breathing if her throat started to close again.

He sat with the tin open in his lap and counted Melie's breaths the way Bett had counted in the courtyard, one hand on the blanket ready to pull it back if she started clawing at her throat again. Every time she shifted in sleep, his fingers found the dent in the lid.

Around him the compound breathed. Rain on stone, mats shifting, a baby crying somewhere — Ada, the Okoye infant, her small voice carrying through the thin walls and then stopping as someone picked her up. The flood's steady rush from the valley below, unchanged, indifferent. Melie's breathing beside him, even and open.

The old catch fired on nothing — no word, no thought he was trying to form, just the body's habit of seizing before speech. He pressed his thumbs against the dented lid of the tin and kept counting until the rain thinned toward morning and sleep still didn't come.


# Chapter 7

The naming bell was not beautiful. Leni had settled this on the third day and nothing since had changed her mind. It was a flat, bossy sound — bronze on bronze with no sustain to it, no ring, just the blunt announcement that someone wanted your attention and didn't care whether you wanted to give it. It reminded her of the substitute teachers at her old school, the ones who rapped the desk with a ruler because they'd already lost the room and had nothing else.

Day nine, and the courtyard stones still held the night's rain. She knew because she'd been counting in her journal, a line drawn under each date at the bottom of the page, and because Tomas had asked that morning. He'd wanted the exact number. She'd said nine and he'd said *that's a long time* with the flat certainty of a ten-year-old stating a fact, and she'd said *yeah* and he'd gone back to throwing stones at the courtyard wall.

She unfolded herself from the steps and went inside.

The main hall had developed a seating geography nobody had assigned but everybody maintained. The Kondos filled the right side — twelve of them, impossible to miss, a family that seemed to travel by census count. Venn sat at the end nearest the open wall where you could see the valley and the brown water below, a position that let him look outward when the naming bored him and inward when it threatened him. Tila sat beside him, her hands in her lap, her posture a careful complement to his — matching him without yielding, the practiced geometry of three decades with a man who took up a great deal of space. The Meras anchored the left, near the kitchen passage. Deshi sat with her back very straight, as if poor posture were a moral failing, and old Peyam beside her with his prayer beads wound around one wrist, his eyes half-closed even before the naming started. Suna was behind them with Kael and Rimi and Asha, organizing children's positions with small efficient gestures, as if braiding hair and steering a crisis used the same part of her hands. The Daos sat in the middle: Hala always with both hands flat on the bench beside her, half-ready to stand, a stance built by forty-four years of getting between her younger sister and trouble. Bett next to her, arms crossed, face closed. The Leos took the back wall. Cade kept Pik between himself and Wynn, a quiet triangle of protection that Leni had noticed from the first day — the way Cade's hand went to Pik's shoulder when anyone official spoke, the way Wynn leaned slightly toward the boy as if proximity were a form of legal claim. Vera, Cade's mother, sat at the end of their bench, a gray-haired woman with steady hands who carried a sewing needle in her front pocket and used it the way other people used worry beads — Leni had watched her mend three separate garments since they'd arrived, always with tiny, precise stitches, always while listening to something she wasn't supposed to comment on.

Jin and Melie sat wherever there was room, which these days meant near the Daos. Since the allergic reaction, Bett checked every plate before Melie touched it. Jin accepted it with stiff courtesy. The thanks sat on his face without making it to his mouth; the words looked too expensive.

The Okoyes sat wherever Bram decided to sit. Today, that was the front.

Leni clocked it immediately. Her father usually chose the middle — close enough to engage, far enough to observe, the teacher's instinct for sight lines. Front-row Bram was argument-Bram. She'd seen the posture at parent-teacher nights, at the town-council meeting he'd dragged her to when she was twelve, at the kitchen table when her parents argued about whether to leave the city. His shoulders came forward and his chin dropped and his hands went into motion, the left circling loosely while the right chopped air for emphasis. She'd once timed the hand-chops at a school-board meeting: three per sentence when he was warming up, five or six when he meant it.

Her mother sat beside him with Ada on her chest. Nessa's face wore the careful blankness that meant she'd tried to talk him out of whatever this was and failed. She was picking lint off Ada's blanket in small, methodical strokes, the kind of useless cleaning people did when their hands needed a job while they waited for the argument they couldn't prevent.

Fasa came in from the custodians' walkway, followed by Olet. She carried the naming ledger — cloth-bound, water-stained along the spine — held against her chest with both hands, the grip of someone who had carried this same object a thousand times and no longer noticed its weight. She took her place at the center of the room and waited.

The conversations thinned. They always did. Fasa had a quality of silence that worked better than volume — she didn't shush anyone, didn't raise a hand, just stood there with her attention aimed at the room like a lens, and people stopped talking because the quiet she carried was heavier than whatever they'd been saying.

"The naming gathers us," Fasa said. "We speak what is true. We hear what is spoken."

Leni could have said it in her sleep by now. The formula was always the same. What changed was what came after.

Her father stood up.

The room noticed. Standing during the opening was new. Fasa paused with the ledger half-open, her eyes going to Bram with the focused attention of someone who had, perhaps, been waiting for this.

"Before we continue," Bram said, "I want the room to consider what we're being asked to do."

His voice was steady — the teacher's voice, calibrated to carry without shouting, clear enough to reach the back wall where the Leos sat. Leni watched his left hand begin its circle.

"Nine days ago, the road flooded and we came here because there was nowhere else to go. The custodians took us in. They've fed us, given us shelter, and I'm grateful for that — genuinely grateful."

The *genuinely* was his setup word. She'd heard it a hundred times. It meant the reversal was two sentences away.

"But the naming isn't hospitality. It's a requirement. Every day we stand in this room and declare our families — our composition, our relationships, our private circumstances — in front of strangers. And each time the questions go deeper. Who your spouse is. Where they are. Whether your children are biologically yours. Whether the family you've described is the family you actually have."

He paused. Jin's jaw locked so hard the hinge jumped once under the skin. For a second he looked caught on the first word, that familiar snag before a hard consonant, then he swallowed it. Across the room Cade shifted on the bench and rested his arm along the wall behind Wynn, a motion so deliberately casual it announced itself.

"Outside this shrine," Bram continued, and his voice picked up the slightly faster cadence Leni recognized as conviction, the argument gathering force before it tipped into anger. "There is a government requiring identity papers. Ethnic registration. Movement permits tied to documented family structure. People are being stopped at checkpoints and made to prove who they are, who they belong to, whether their papers match their faces. That's why most of us are here. We were trying to get away from exactly that system."

His right hand chopped once. Twice.

"The naming is the same impulse in different clothing. It asks the same questions the state asks. It requires the same public declarations. And it carries the same implicit threat — cooperate or face consequences. The only difference is the state uses soldiers and checkpoints, and the shrine uses" — he paused, choosing — "tradition."

The room held its breath. The argument itself wasn't shocking — Leni suspected most of the adults had built some version of it in their own heads. Hearing it said aloud in front of the custodians was something else: Bram's hands moving, his teacher-voice carrying, his glasses catching the grey light from the open wall.

Bram turned to Olet. The head custodian stood where he always stood — slightly apart from Fasa, near the stone pillar, with his hands at his sides and his face arranged in the same expression it always held, which was no expression at all. Olet's face was a policy. It did not change for arguments, for children crying, for the sound of the flood below them. It did not change now.

"I'm asking you to address this directly," Bram said. "Not the formula, not the tradition. The substance. Why should these families be required to declare their private business to you?"

Olet was silent for a moment. Leni had learned to distinguish his silences from pauses people used for effect. His were simply empty. The space where urgency should have been, and wasn't.

"The naming is the shrine's practice," Olet said. "Families who come to the shrine participate in the naming. This has been so for longer than I have served here, and for longer than anyone living remembers."

"That's not an answer," Bram said. "That's a description. I'm asking you to justify — "

"The naming does not require justification. It requires participation."

Leni watched her father's hands stop moving. Just for a second. Then the left one started again, faster.

"You don't hear how that sounds? You genuinely don't hear yourself?"

"I hear you clearly, Bram Okoye. You have spoken. I have answered."

The thing was — and this was what Leni couldn't stop tracking — her father was right. His argument lined up in her head the way geometry proofs did: each box filled, each reason following the last. Same questions, same demand to show your work. Only here the chalkboard was a ledger and getting it wrong could cost you dinner.

But Olet spoke the way the bus driver back home said *last stop*: flat, final, already past discussion. Her father kept reaching for a seam and coming up against solid metal. The whole thing was welded shut.

"If a family refuses to participate," Bram said — and now his voice was quieter, which Leni recognized as danger, the dropping register that meant he was about to ask the question he already knew the answer to — "what happens?"

"They may stay," Olet said.

"And?"

"They may stay."

"That's it? No consequences?"

Olet's pause was longer this time. A fraction of a second, maybe two. Leni noticed because she'd been timing his pauses since the second day — not consciously, at first, but then as a kind of practice, a way to measure what the man didn't say by how long he didn't say it.

"The naming and the common stores are one provision," Olet said. "The shrine offers both together. Families who do not participate in the naming may continue to stay at the shrine. They cannot draw from the common food stores."

The room changed. The noise stayed the same, but the silence turned from attention to arithmetic. Leni watched it happen on face after face: people counting mouths, counting days, counting what they had left in their bags. Suna uncrossed her arms and put one hand on Rimi's shoulder, an inventorying touch from a woman confirming her children were still in reach. Venn looked down the row of Kondos — the quick, assessing scan of a man counting the mouths he was responsible for. Tila's thumb caught on the edge of her wedding ring. Hala's hands pressed harder against the bench. Nessa pulled Ada closer and the baby made a small sound of protest at the tightening.

"You're saying you'll starve us into compliance," Bram said.

"I am saying what the shrine provides and the terms on which it provides it."

"That's coercion. You understand that? You're using food as — "

"The naming is the shrine's practice," Olet said, and the repetition landed with a flatness that was worse than any rebuttal. He had said what he was going to say. He would go on saying it. The room could object as long as it wanted and his answer would not change, because his words had the shape of a wall.

Fasa had not moved. She stood with the ledger against her chest and her eyes on Bram, and for a moment Leni saw something cross her face that she couldn't quite name. It skipped past both sides of the argument and landed somewhere more private — a flinch, almost, as if the mechanism she'd served for twenty years had been opened in front of her and the workings were uglier than she'd remembered. It lasted half a second. Then Fasa's face settled back into the custodian expression — calm, attentive, present — and the moment was gone.

"If there are no further questions," Fasa said, "the naming will continue."

"There are further questions," Bram said.

"They will be heard. The naming hears what is spoken."

Bram looked at Fasa, then at Olet, then at the room. His left hand closed and opened. He pushed his glasses up with one knuckle and pressed his fingers against his eyelids — the gesture he made at home when the internet failed for the third time in a week, a small domestic motion that belonged to the specific exhaustion of fighting something that wouldn't acknowledge the fight.

Nessa reached up and touched his hand. Only that. Her fingers rested against the back of his wrist, and Leni could read the whole conversation in the touch: *sit down, this isn't working, the baby needs to eat, you've said what you came to say.*

He sat down.

&nbsp;

Hala was the first to speak after the custodians left. She waited until the covered walkway's door closed behind them, then said, "Well, shit," in a flat voice that did more work than Bram's entire argument.

Nobody suggested leaving the hall. Moving would have meant the conversation was over, and nobody was ready for that — even if nobody knew what to do with it while it was still happening.

"We don't comply," Bram said. He was leaning forward, elbows on his knees, glasses pushed up into his hair. Without the glasses his face looked younger and more uncertain, which was something Leni did not want to see right now. "All seven families refuse together. If we refuse collectively, they can't enforce it. They're five people. We're forty-three."

"We're forty-three people who can't leave," Suna said from the Mera side of the room. "That's not the same as forty-three people with options."

"If we refuse as a body — "

"We are not a body," Venn said. He didn't raise his voice. He never needed to; the room leaned toward it anyway. "We are seven families. My family is twelve. How long do you propose we hold out?"

He didn't wait for an answer.

"I will tell you how long. Our provisions lasted until the fifth day. Everything we've eaten since then has come from the common stores. Mava is seventy-three. Ori is nine. There are four other children under sixteen in my family alone. If you want to stand on principle, Okoye, stand on it. But not with my family's meals."

"And that's exactly what they're counting on," Bram said. His sentences were getting longer, the subordinate clauses multiplying — Leni could hear the teacher in him building the argument even as the argument was losing, still believing a well-constructed case should matter. "The leverage works precisely because the larger families can't afford to resist. Which means the larger families are being punished for — "

"Don't lecture me about my family's size."

"I'm not — "

"My family is not your argument."

Leni followed the line of the Kondo bench when he said it: Mava's narrow hands folded in her lap, Ori's feet not touching the floor, Arash staring at the boards as if looking away counted as staying out of it. *My family* didn't sound possessive now. It sounded like a man trying to get twelve bodies through a door built for one.

"Our provisions are gone too," Suna said, cutting between them with the neutral efficiency she brought to everything — settling children, distributing blankets, stopping fights before they fully committed. "We have the children's emergency food. Dried fruit, some biscuits. Two days' worth if I stretch it. Maybe three."

"The Parras have nothing," Hala said. She looked toward the back of the hall where Jin stood against the wall with Melie beside him. Jin gave a nod so slight it was more a contraction of muscle than a gesture. Melie was braiding and unbraiding a strand of her own hair with one hand, the other locked in Jin's, her face carrying the particular opacity of a child who knows the adults are talking about something bad and has decided not to ask.

"We've got some things," Hala continued. "Dried fish, some oil. Bett packed for a longer trip than the rest of us." She didn't look at Bett as she said it. Bett sat with her arms folded, face in its public setting: guarded, level, offering nothing she hadn't chosen to offer. "But it's for four. Not forty-three. And it won't last."

Suna's numbers hit harder than her father's principles. Two days. Maybe three. Leni felt her own stomach answer with a sour little pinch, as if hearing about biscuits had taught it a new trick.

Vera Leo's needle paused in the cloth of Pik's shirt. She didn't resume sewing.

"We'll sit tomorrow," Cade said. He set each word down like a foot on rotten wood, checking it before he trusted it. "I'm not pretending we won't. But Pik doesn't answer for us. He says his name if they ask it. That's all."

Wynn kept one hand on the boy's knee. "If they want the part we don't know, they ask us. They don't get it out of him."

"He's seven," Vera said. "They can do their own dirty work."

Cade and Wynn sounded different from Dad and Venn. Dad and Venn were still fighting over principle; the Leos were drawing a fence around one seven-year-old boy, and the fence was made out of grammar: *our son, our answer, ask us*.

Jin spoke from the wall without moving off it. "Same for Melie. If they want answers from my family, they ask me. She doesn't open her mouth."

That was the first real count of the room, Leni thought. Not her father's forty-three against five. This was smaller and meaner: the Leos closing around Pik, Jin bracing around Melie, each family deciding which body took the question.

"Then start there," Suna said. "No child answers. Not Melie, not Pik, not any of mine. Children stay behind the adults. We keep our answers short and we don't hand over more than they ask."

Bram looked at her. "You hear what we're doing."

"Yeah," Suna said. "I'm trying to keep doing it with fed children."

"Draw whatever lines you like," Venn said. "The Kondos will speak for the Kondos. But my family is eating tomorrow. We will not miss a meal because another family wants to make a stand."

Deshi had been watching from the Mera side of the room with her chin slightly raised, waiting until the room had tired itself out enough for her cut to land.

"You thought," Deshi said, and her voice had the dry, scoring quality that made Leni glad she wasn't on the receiving end, "that the custodians who feed us, house us, and control every hour of every day were offering the naming as a gentle invitation? A polite request we could decline?"

The room took it with visible aftershocks. Ori climbed into Tila's side without asking. Bett uncrossed her arms for the first time all morning and rubbed one thumb over the heel of her palm as if checking whether it was still there. Somebody at the back clicked open a ration tin, heard the sound it made, and shut it again. Deshi had done what a red pen did to one of Leni's essays: circled the thing that had been sitting there the whole time and made everybody feel stupid for missing it.

"Then say the whole thing," Bram said. He said it to the room, not to Deshi, and his voice had gone flat in a way that Leni recognized — the register he dropped into when he stopped trying to persuade and started trying to mark something permanent. "Say we're sitting tomorrow because the shrine holds the food. Say the choice ends where the children need dinner."

"Whose record?" Venn asked.

Bram looked at him. For a moment neither man spoke, and Leni saw something pass between them beyond hostility: the grim mutual recognition of two people who understood power from opposite ends. Venn understood power because he held it, inside the borders of his family. Bram understood power because he'd spent his life trying to argue against it, which required understanding the exact shape of the thing you couldn't move.

"Mine," Bram said.

Nessa's hand was on his arm. She didn't say anything. She didn't need to. The conversation was the touch: *you've made your point, you've put it on record, now let it go because Ada is going to need to eat in two hours and there is nothing in our bags.*

"We need to talk about tomorrow," Suna said. She said it the way she said things that needed saying — without emphasis, without ceremony, as if the practicalities were a relief after the principles. "The naming will happen. We should figure out what we're willing to say and what we're not."

"Dao children stay with me," Hala said immediately. "If Bett gets pulled in, they don't follow her."

"Pik sits between us," Vera said. "He doesn't answer twice."

"Melie stays beside me," Jin said.

"The Kondos will speak in our usual order," Venn said. "No one else decides it for us."

The conversation shifted then. Consensus never arrived; logistics took over instead — who sat where, which child stayed behind which adult, how few words a family could survive on. Vera nudged Pik's place one body inward with her sandal. Hala called the Dao children to her knees and kept a hand on each shoulder while Bett stared past them at the empty center of the hall. Suna swapped Rimi and Kael so Asha would be bracketed on both sides. Her father had asked for refusal. The room had answered by rearranging the children.

Leni stopped listening after that. Caring had nothing to do with it. The pattern had already set. Tomorrow was already in the room: benches scraped into defensive lines, children pulled closer, adults testing how small a family could sound without breaking apart.

&nbsp;

The courtyard was cooler. The clouds were low and soaked and the air had that heavy wet-cloth feeling that meant rain was close but hadn't committed. From somewhere behind the kitchen overhang came the sound of the custodians' assistant rinsing pots — the scrape of metal on stone, unhurried, as if nothing had happened, as if nothing had changed. Leni sat on the top step and pulled her knees up and wrapped her arms around them.

Tomas was below her, throwing stones at the wall. He'd arranged them in a line by size — smallest to largest, a ten-year-old's ritual of order in a place that offered very little. The thud of stone hitting stone was steady, almost soothing. He threw with his whole body, the way boys did, more force than accuracy.

"What happened in there?" he asked, without looking up.

"Nothing. Grown-up stuff."

"Dad was arguing."

"Yeah."

"Did he win?"

She watched him throw. Small stone, medium stone, larger stone. Retrieve, reset, repeat. His focus was complete and unconcerned and she envied it with a sharpness that surprised her.

"It wasn't that kind of argument," she said.

Tomas considered this. He threw the next stone and it hit the wall with a crack and bounced into the gutter that ran along the courtyard's edge. He watched it roll, then looked up at her.

"Like when he argued with the landlord?"

"Sort of."

"He didn't win that one either."

"No," Leni said. "He didn't."

Tomas nodded and went back to his stones. The simple economy of a ten-year-old's conclusions: either you won or you didn't, and then you threw the next rock. She wished it were that clean.

Across the courtyard, under the opposite overhang, Cade had crouched in front of Pik. Wynn stood behind the boy with both hands on his shoulders. Vera sat beside them with the torn shirt in her lap, the needle flashing in and out of the hem.

"Again," Cade said softly.

Pik stared at the wet stones between his sandals. "My name is Pik. Cade is my father. Wynn is my father. Vera is my grandmother."

"Slower," Vera said, not looking up. "Like you know it."

Wynn said, "And if they ask more, you let us answer."

Pik nodded without lifting his head.

On the kitchen side, Suna had spread a dish towel on the stone and emptied the Meras' emergency food onto it: biscuits gone damp at the corners, a twist of paper raisins, strips of dried fruit stuck together with sugar. She separated it into careful little rations, stared at how small they were, and swept them back into one. Rimi watched without speaking. When Kael drifted too close, Suna covered the towel with her palm.

Her mother came out a minute later with Ada crying in those short outraged bursts babies made when the world failed them. Nessa sat under the eave, opened her blouse, and got the baby latched. Even then her eyes kept lifting to the kitchen door, to the storeroom beyond it, as if milk itself had started to feel borrowed.

Below the steps, below the courtyard, below the shrine's stone walls, the valley was brown water all the way to the tree line. The road they'd driven in on was invisible — erased, the seven converging paths submerged under a flat expanse of flood that reflected the clouds and gave back nothing. Somewhere under that water was the world they'd come from. Bram's school, their apartment, the street where Leni used to walk to the bus stop in the mornings with her earbuds in, the particular and irretrievable normalcy of a life that hadn't required you to sit in a stone compound and watch your father lose to a man who didn't bother to win.

She thought about her journal. She'd write tonight, same as every night — on her mat, oil lamp turned low so her mother wouldn't tell her to sleep, her pen moving across the page in handwriting that got worse as the dark got deeper. She'd been recording the days with the scrupulous attention she gave things when she didn't know the point yet but didn't trust herself to miss one. The details of the naming, who said what, who sat where, the arguments and their outcomes. But tonight the part she'd write first wasn't Olet's sentence. It was Pik under the overhang, saying his family back to himself until it sounded rehearsed. It was Suna sorting and re-sorting the damp biscuits. It was her mother watching the storeroom while Ada swallowed.

Her father had been right in every way you could write on a board with arrows between the points. It hadn't bought him anything she could touch. Not Kael's stopped hand. Not Pik's careful recitation. Not the way her mother kept looking at the kitchen door while the baby drank. Being right hadn't opened a road or a cupboard. It had just taught everyone how to brace.

The scary part was how far tomorrow had already gotten into their bodies. Into Pik's careful recitation, into Kael backing off when Suna covered the towel, into Nessa watching the storeroom latch while her baby ate. Bram had given the trap a clean name. The rest of them were already learning its shape by feel.

Below them the flood made its constant sound — the steady rush of water over a road that no one could use. Tomas threw his last stone and it hit the wall dead center and fell straight down.

The bell would ring again tomorrow, and they'd answer it.


# Chapter 8

Suna had been watching Tomas Okoye for two days before she understood what she was watching.

It started the morning after the families agreed to keep sitting for the naming, when the work bell pulled them back into the circle. Not agreed — submitted. Bram Okoye had argued his case with a teacher's precision and it hadn't mattered, because the custodians held the food stores and the Kondos had twelve mouths and the calculation was simple and humiliating: you eat, therefore you comply. The compound's mood since then had been the mood of people performing cooperation that fooled no one, and against that backdrop Suna noticed Tomas because noticing things was how she managed, and managing was how she stayed upright.

He was ten, slim, with his mother's round face and his father's long fingers — hands too big for him still, palms and knuckles arriving years ahead of the rest of his body. He played with the younger children more than most ten-year-olds would tolerate, carrying baby Ada on his hip when Nessa needed both hands, showing Kael how to stack pebbles without using the flat ones as a base. His patience with smaller children had no shine to it. He'd already learned how often a baby woke, how many small hands tugged at you in a day, how many times you had to answer the same request without snapping.

The hesitation appeared at the morning meal. Or the morning before. She couldn't pin it, which bothered her, because Suna knew days by what they demanded of her: whether Asha refused lentils, whether Kael drained his cup and came back asking for more water, whether Rimi climbed off her mat rubbing the shoulder she'd slept on wrong. She knew the slap of the cistern bucket against stone, the look of a bread stack that would not make it around the circle twice, the adults who showed up sweaty from labor and the ones who arrived clean with reasons. She knew when a child had skinned both knees and when somebody else's baby had a cough starting. So the fact that she could not place the first catch in Tomas's mouth felt like dropping a bowl and never hearing it break.

So she couldn't say exactly when Tomas started catching on his consonants.

He was asking Kael to pass the water cup, and the word caught behind his teeth. Not a stutter — Suna had grown up with a cousin who stammered on vowels, and she knew that sound; a stammer pulled the whole face into the labor of speaking. This was different. Tomas's jaw locked briefly before the hard consonant, the muscle along his cheek tightening for less than a second, and then the word came out all at once, late and slightly stretched. "Cup," he said at last. A catch and release. A small mechanical failure in a mouth that had been working fine for ten years.

She knew the mechanism. She had been listening to it for ten days from the mouth of Jin Parra.

Jin's hesitation was subtle enough that most people seemed not to register it, or else they folded it into the general impression of a man who spoke as little as possible. Suna had noticed on the second day. She tracked speech with the same restless attention she gave a person's grip on a knife, their hands before communal bread, the small bodily facts that told her how close trouble was. Jin caught on certain consonants, the hard ones, the plosives, the sounds that required the jaw to close and open with force. C, k, p, t. His jaw would lock for a fraction of a second, the muscle along his cheek visible beneath the skin, and then the word would emerge slightly stretched. The first sound always seemed to come through a narrowed gap.

Tomas was catching on the same hard sounds, the lock flickering in the same place along his jaw.

Suna set down the flatbread she'd been pulling apart for Asha and watched. Tomas was talking to Melie now — Jin's daughter, six, who had attached herself to the older boy with the uncritical loyalty of a child who recognized someone kind. He was explaining a rule of the pebble game, and the word *put* sat shut behind his teeth the same way. The catch. The delay. The jaw unlocking like a hinge that had briefly rusted.

Across the morning circle, Nessa Okoye sat with Ada in her lap, bouncing the baby absently on one knee. She was watching her son. Nessa's face had no defense — never had, as far as Suna could tell.
