Fear, hope, confusion, tenderness — everything moved across her features without permission, which made her easy to read and hard to look at, because what Suna saw in her face right now was recognition, quick and consonant by consonant.

Their eyes met. Suna looked away first, but not before she'd registered the question in Nessa's expression: *You see it too?*

She saw it. Asha had already tied the Meras to the Kondos. Melie's throat had already tied the Parras to the Daos. Now Tomas's jaw had pulled the Okoyes onto the same map, and the count in Suna's head jumped to five families before she could stop it. The number didn't feel clarifying. It felt like the floor losing another board.

Suna finished pulling apart the bread and handed the pieces to Asha, who received them with both hands and ate with her mouth open, watching her mother with the unreadable calm of a four-year-old who did not know that her own body had already become evidence.

---

That afternoon the rain came in hard and the children compressed into the main hall. Twenty-one of them, ranging from Ada's one year to Fen Tesh's sixteen, occupying the stone floor in a shifting mass of noise and negotiation while the adults arranged themselves along the walls and tried not to contribute to the volume. Deshi had positioned herself near the cold cooking fire — an act of territorial habit that no longer drew comment from anyone except Rimi, who rolled her eyes with the practiced contempt of a twelve-year-old who had decided that adults were a failing species.

Suna stationed herself where she could see the children's clump without sitting in it. The older ones — Leni, Fen, Raya Dao — maintained their adolescent separateness near the walls, performing the studied indifference of teenagers trapped with younger children they considered beneath their dignity. The younger ones pooled at the center in a shifting economy of games and grievances. Tomas was in the middle, as usual, mediating a dispute between Kael and Sem Dao over a set of smooth stones that had become, through the alchemy of confinement, more valuable than anything the adults could offer.

She watched Tomas talk. Three hesitations in ten minutes, all on hard consonants. Each one stalled the same way: a visible seize at the jaw hinge, then the sentence came late, the first sound stretched thin. "Take it back. I already put it there. Come on, Sem." The children around him didn't seem to notice. A man limped, a woman hummed, a boy started catching on his k's. At eight or six, the world simply admitted such things.

Jin Parra was sitting against the far wall with his knees drawn up, Melie leaning against his side, her thumb in her mouth. He was watching the children with the specific vigilance of a man whose daughter's body had already done something impossible — whose daughter's throat had swelled shut from a food she'd eaten her whole life, claiming an allergy that belonged to another family. His jaw worked slightly as he watched, the unconscious motion of a habit he'd carried since adolescence.

Across the room, Tomas's jaw had started answering to it.

Suna looked at Jin's jaw, then at Tomas's jaw, then at Nessa. Nessa was nursing Ada in the corner with a cloth draped over her shoulder, her free hand resting on her thigh. Her fingers were opening and closing in a slow rhythm that had nothing to do with the baby and everything to do with the boy across the room whose mouth was becoming someone else's.

---

The next morning was the first without rain since the fourth day. The clouds didn't clear — they thinned, turning the light from gray to a diffuse white that made the courtyard stones steam as the moisture lifted. The children spilled outside with the urgency of animals released from a hold, and even Deshi stood at the edge of the covered walkway and lifted her face to the warm air with an expression that, on anyone else, would have been called relief.

Suna was wringing laundry at the cistern. The work was necessary and constant — forty-three people produced a volume of soiled fabric that had become the compound's most tangible communal problem, a problem she addressed with the same methodical attention she brought to everything else because the alternative was to address nothing, to let the vertigo underneath the method have its way. She wrung a shirt of Kael's until her knuckles whitened against the wet fabric, draped it over the stone lip, picked up the next piece.

Kiri Tesh was sitting on the courtyard steps about ten meters away, her legs stretched in front of her, sorting seed pods she'd collected from the edge of the terraced garden. She was a wiry child, all elbows and sharp collarbones, with a habit of biting the inside of her cheek when she concentrated that made her look older than eight. She had her father Miro's dark hair, but she carried herself with a physical self-possession that belonged to neither parent — Miro was cautious in movement, Enn nervous, and Kiri was neither. She arranged her seed pods on the stone step with the focused determination of a scientist organizing specimens, dividing them by size into precise rows.

She was humming.

Suna didn't register the melody at first. It entered as background, the hum of a child busy with a task, part of the compound's ambient soundtrack where someone was always making sound — coughing, arguing, praying, scraping pots. Then the melody turned at a specific interval. A minor third dropping to a major second in a pattern that was not common, not universal, not the kind of thing a child would pick up from exposure to ordinary songs.

Suna gave Kael's shirt one more twist before the turn in the melody reached her. The seam bit into her palm. She let go so abruptly the wet cloth slapped the stone lip and slid back against her wrist, cold and heavy. Water ran off her elbows and darkened the front of her skirt.

The melody was her mother's lullaby.

The one with the descending line that reversed itself in the third phrase and looped back to its own beginning instead of resolving. The one her mother had sung in the old dialect from a mountain valley four hundred kilometers south, where the roads were bad and the villages had been emptying for thirty years. It was built on intervals that leaned away from the scale most people learned. Suna's mother used to say the tune was older than the words.

Her mother had died when Suna was nineteen. The lullaby went with her. It belonged to that voice, those arms, the darkness of her mother's bedroom; anywhere else it curdled into imitation. Suna had never sung it for her own children, not here, not anywhere. She hadn't even thought of it in months, maybe years. Hearing it now from the mouth of an eight-year-old Tesh girl carried the flat impossibility of meeting a dead woman in the road.

Kiri hummed through the third phrase, took the turn cleanly, and kept going. Her pitch wavered only at the edges. The intervals held. So did the rhythm, including the suspended note before the loop, where Suna's mother used to let the breath sit for a beat and then begin again.

Suna braced both hands on the cistern edge and watched an eight-year-old girl hum her dead mother's song while sorting seed pods on the steps.

She should go over. She should ask Kiri where she'd heard the melody. She should go carefully, looking for the ordinary explanation before admitting the extraordinary. Someone in the compound might know the song. She might have hummed it in her sleep. The melody might be more common than she thought, a variant that had traveled farther than she knew.

She didn't move.

She stood at the cistern and listened, and the melody looped twice more before Kiri lost interest and started talking to one of the younger Kondo children who'd wandered over, and the song stopped as easily as it had started. Kiri's mouth moved on. The melody dropped dead in the air, stripped of history and weight for everyone but Suna.

The pattern had reached the Teshes too. A lullaby had crossed a dead woman and four hundred kilometers to land in Kiri's mouth.

Suna picked up the next piece of laundry. Her hands were trembling in a way that had nothing to do with cold water and everything to do with the specific frequency of the third phrase, which was still in her ears like a tone that continued after the source had stopped. She wrung the fabric until the tremor was absorbed into the work, and when Deshi called from across the courtyard to ask whether she planned to wash everything or just the things that mattered, she answered with a steadiness that cost her something she could feel but couldn't name.

"All of it," she said. "I'm doing all of it."

Deshi looked at her for a beat longer than necessary, and Suna understood that her mother-in-law's eye missed very little, and that the effort of seeming fine was itself a kind of signal to anyone watching closely enough.

She did not ask Kiri where she'd heard it. Later, when Enn crossed the sleeping quarters with a stack of blankets against her hip and Kiri trailed behind biting the inside of her cheek, Suna let them pass. Asking would give the song witnesses. Once it belonged to the compound, it would belong to the shrine.

---

That evening, after the children were settled and the compound had reached the uneasy quiet that passed for nighttime peace, Suna found Nessa at the edge of the prayer courtyard.

Nessa was standing with Ada asleep against her chest, the baby's weight pulling her slightly forward. She had wrapped Ada in a length of faded blue-gray cloth — her own shawl, repurposed, the fringe knotted where it had begun to unravel — and in the oil-lamp light her outline blurred into the stone behind her. She held Ada as armor, the baby positioned between herself and whatever was coming. The placement was irrational and absolute.

Suna had never spent time alone with Nessa. Their interactions had been practical — the choreography of women who cooked in the same kitchen and washed at the same cistern and negotiated sleeping arrangements for children who increasingly refused to sleep where they were told. They weren't unfriendly. They simply hadn't needed each other yet.

"Can I sit with you?" Suna asked.

Nessa shifted sideways on the low courtyard wall. "Please."

Suna sat. The stone was still warm from the afternoon's thin sun, and the warmth seeped through her skirt into her thighs. The courtyard smelled of the wet herbs from the terraced garden, a green mineral scent that the rain drew out of the soil and that clung to everything for hours after the water stopped.

They sat in silence for a while. Ada made small sounds in her sleep — the grunting, liquid noises of a baby whose breathing was a performance even unconscious. Moths circled the nearest oil lamp, their shadows huge and flickering against the courtyard stones.

"Tomas," Suna said.

Nessa's hand moved to Ada's back. "You've noticed."

"I've noticed."

"Since two days ago. Maybe three." Nessa looked down at the stones between her feet. "He's never spoken that way in his life."

"It's Jin's pattern."

"I know." Nessa touched two fingers to the hinge of her jaw. "I've been watching Jin, and so have you. Bram says imitation. Or coincidence. Or whatever else lets him finish a meal. Then he lies next to me at night and doesn't believe any of it."

"There's another one," Suna said. "Another manifestation. That's why I came to you."

Nessa's hand stilled on Ada's back. "Who?"

"Kiri Tesh. She was humming a song this morning on the courtyard steps. A lullaby."

"Lots of children hum."

"My mother's lullaby. A song from my mother's village, in a dialect nobody up here speaks. My mother's been dead nineteen years. I haven't sung it since."

The silence that followed pressed. Nessa pulled Ada fractionally closer, and the baby grunted in protest, and Nessa loosened her hold but didn't change the position of her arms.

"How can you be sure it's the same song?" she asked. "Songs travel."

"It doesn't sit in the normal scale," Suna said. "The melody never resolves. It loops back instead of ending. My mother used to say that was the point — a lullaby shouldn't stop, because sleep should feel like going around again, not arriving somewhere. Nobody writes songs like that. It was hers."

"Could you have hummed it without knowing? In your sleep, or while you were working?"

Suna sat with the question. Outside the courtyard, the flood's sound carried up from the valley, that constant rush that had become so woven into the compound's atmosphere that it registered only during strange silences, and then with the force of something that had been listening the whole time.

"I don't think so," she said. "But I can't be certain. Which is almost worse than knowing."

"That makes six," Nessa said quietly. "If you're counting."

Suna was. The count didn't steady her anymore. It only told her how little blank space was left.

"The Leos are quiet. Cade hardly talks to anyone who isn't Wynn or Vera."

"Have you noticed anything with Pik?"

Nessa shook her head. "Give it time." The words landed with the dead flatness of someone naming weather already on its way.

"Nessa."

"What?"

Suna let the sentence sit for a beat before she gave it weight. "When I watched you this morning — watching Tomas — you looked like you'd been waiting."

Nessa's face did what it always did, which was everything. The oil lamp caught it all — the tightening around her eyes, her mouth pressing shut and then opening again under the strain of holding the words back.

"I've been waiting since Melie's throat swelled shut," she said. "Since the allergy. Because when I watched Bett lean over her — knowing exactly what to do, knowing it was the Dao allergy before anyone said it — I thought: it's going to find us too. It's going to reach one of mine."

"And it has."

"And it has." She adjusted Ada's weight, resettling the baby higher against her chest. Ada's face was mashed against Nessa's collarbone, one fist curled against the faded cloth. "But this isn't — what's happening to Tomas isn't the first time something's been taken from me."

Suna waited. Something was already moving toward them. The pause had pressure in it, the next words already taking up space before Nessa said them.

"We had another child," Nessa said. "Before Tomas. A boy. His name was Efe."

The name entered the air between them and stayed.

"He died at three months. A fever. It came fast — by morning he was hot, by afternoon he wouldn't nurse, and by evening his breathing had changed. Bram drove for two hours to find a clinic that was open, and it didn't matter. By the time they got a line in, he was—" She broke off. The sentence hit something inside her and stopped. She breathed. Started again from a different place. "We buried him in the yard behind our house. Bram dug the hole himself because he said he wouldn't let someone else do it. The hole was very small. That's what I remember most. How small the hole was."

Suna didn't touch her. The impulse was there — hand on knee, hand on arm, the practical touch she used with her children when words weren't enough — but something in Nessa's stillness told her that touch right now would crack the structure that was holding the words in their sequence, and Nessa needed the sequence to finish.

"We don't talk about him," Nessa said. "We — Bram and I decided, after. That we wouldn't carry it forward. That Tomas and Leni would grow up without the weight of a dead brother they'd never met. We thought that was the right thing." Her voice was steady and her eyes were wet and the two facts weren't in contradiction — they were the specific product of a grief that had been managed for so long it had developed its own architecture, its own load-bearing walls. "I don't know anymore if it was the right thing."

"Is that why you've been waiting?" Suna asked. "Because if the children are manifesting other families' losses—"

"What's the loss?" Nessa finished. "What's the gap? Melie's allergy connects to the Daos, and the Daos lost a son. Asha connects to the Kondos, and Mava lost a sister. So when Tomas starts speaking like Jin — what's missing? Whose absence?" She looked at Suna with the directness of someone who had decided to be fully present rather than protected. "Ours. Maybe. Or maybe Jin's — his wife."

"His wife died," Suna said, and as she said it she heard the echo of her own constructed story — *my husband went ahead* — and recognized, not for the first time, how a lie took on the texture of fact when nobody pushed against it.

"His wife died," Nessa repeated, and her tone carried either doubt or the sharpened attention of a woman who'd learned to listen past declarations.

The compound sounds filtered through the stillness around them — someone coughing in the sleeping quarters, the creak of the kitchen overhang's timber frame, the low murmur of the custodians' radio bleeding through their closed door, an indistinct voice reporting news from somewhere that wasn't here.

"I can't tell what this is," Suna said. "I don't know if it's the shrine, or the custodians, or something the children are doing to each other without knowing it, or something that would have happened anywhere if you put this many families in a small enough space for long enough. I don't have an explanation."

"Bram cycles through his four explanations like a man shuffling cards. He deals himself a new hand every morning." For a second Nessa's mouth twitched with the old habit of making a joke because the alternative was worse. "I don't have four explanations. I've got zero. I've just got my son's jaw doing something it shouldn't be able to do."

Suna reached across the space between them and put her hand on Nessa's knee, the same steady pressure she used on her children when language stopped helping. Nessa's hand came down and covered hers, and they stayed like that for a while, two women on a warm stone wall with a sleeping baby between them and the flood's sound rising from the valley below like a fact that wouldn't be argued with.

"Efe would've been eleven this year," Nessa said. "Almost the same age as Tomas. They would've fought constantly — brothers do. Bram would've had to mediate, because I cave in about three seconds. Leni would've bossed them both." She was smiling now, a small smile that had nothing behind it but loss and the particular tenderness of imagining a life that hadn't happened. "Instead, Tomas is a patient older brother to a baby sister, and he's the kindest boy I know, and now he talks like a stranger he met ten days ago."

"Do you think we should tell the custodians?" Suna asked.

"About Tomas? They'll find out. They see everything."

"About Efe."

The name again. Suna felt its weight differently the second time. Surprise was gone. The word had mass now.

Nessa was quiet for several breaths. "About the gap, you mean. About what might be pulling Tomas toward Jin's pattern."

"Yes."

"I don't know. I don't know what they'd do with it." She turned Suna's question back. "Would you tell them about the lullaby?"

Suna thought about Fasa's steady gaze during the namings, the custodian's habit of holding silence until other people filled it. She thought about Olet's flat calm, which was either faith or indifference, and which unsettled her precisely because she couldn't tell which.

"Not yet," she said. "Not until I understand what they want from us."

"They want the map," Nessa said. "Fasa keeps saying it — the true kinship map. Like we're hiding it instead of trying to figure out what the hell is happening to our kids."

Nessa stood, lifting Ada higher against her chest, the baby's weight resettling into the familiar position. "I should go in. Bram's probably lying in the dark composing his fifth explanation."

Suna let the joke pass. She stayed on the wall after Nessa went inside, listening to the flood, feeling the stone cool beneath her as the last of the day's heat bled away. Six families. One left. The number no longer looked like order. It looked like approach.

Tomorrow, if Fasa asked again for the true kinship map, Suna would say nothing about the lullaby and nothing about Efe. Not yet. Let the custodians pull what they could from children's mouths and mothers' fear. She would watch the Leos first. She would see whether the pattern reached Pik before she handed the shrine another dead child to fit into its shape.

The melody came back to her, uninvited. Her mother's voice sometimes surfaced between waking and sleep with that same blunt force, not as memory but as presence. The song did not stay where the dead were supposed to stay. It returned whole, looking for a mouth to carry it forward. Kiri's mouth. An eight-year-old in a different family from a different region who sorted seed pods by size and bit the inside of her cheek when she concentrated and hummed songs she had no reason to know.

Suna pressed her lips together and didn't hum.

She sat with the silence and the flood and the cooling stone, feeling Nessa's dead son settle beside the older ache of Sev's absence and the daily fright of Asha's borrowed gestures. She kept her hands flat on the wall until grit marked her palms. If she let those three things hit at once, she would fold in half right there in the courtyard. Not yet. Not here. Not with three children sleeping in the next room and a mother-in-law whose sharp eye missed nothing and another day of shrine work and naming ahead of her tomorrow and a lullaby that had crossed nineteen years and four hundred kilometers to arrive in the throat of a stranger's child.

The oil lamps in the main hall threw soft light across the courtyard. Inside, someone coughed. Ada cried once — sharp, sudden, a single note — and then stopped. The flood went on. By morning the work bell would drag them back to damp coals and assigned places, and she'd carry the lullaby there without saying a word.


# Chapter 9

Under the western overhang, last night's coals had gone gummy with rain. Fasa crouched at the fire pit while the work bell carried thinly across the compound, breaking the crust apart with a stick and feeding dried grass into the damp center until a thin flame caught. Across twenty naming cycles she had learned that the naming told her what people were willing to declare, but the kitchen told her what their hands knew.

The cooking area was a stone ledge on the compound's western side, open to the courtyard and the smell of wet hillside beyond. The air beneath the overhang was layered — ash at the bottom, green mineral rain above, and the persistent wood-smoke that had worked its way into everyone's hair and clothing by the end of the first week. The wet season made everything slow. Even fire needed coaxing.

Suna Mera arrived first among the assigned families, carrying the morning's water in a clay jug balanced on her hip. She set it down without greeting, but the absence of greeting was itself familiar by now — they had reached the stage of proximity where not every arrival required acknowledgment. Deshi followed a few paces behind, her posture evaluative before she'd even looked at the cooking area, the lean angle of her shoulders conveying judgment about things she hadn't yet seen.

"The coals are still damp," Deshi said. She knelt beside the fire pit and held her palm over the heat, testing. "You need harder wood underneath. Softwood burns wet."

Fasa did not point out that she had been building fires at this shrine for two decades. She moved the clay jug to the flat stone where the cutting board lived and began slicing onions from the garden. Twenty years had taught her when to assert authority and when to let a woman who needed control find it. The kitchen was Deshi's territory, even someone else's kitchen.

Hala Dao came next, with Sem behind her carrying a basket of the dried pulse stores Olet had released from the custodians' pantry that morning. Hala was broader than Deshi, sturdier in the way she occupied space, and she entered the kitchen with the settled authority of long practice in lean kitchens. She wore a cotton scarf over her hair that she'd rewashed so many times at the cistern it had faded from blue to the color of overcast sky. She assessed the fire, the jug, the onions, and Deshi's kneeling posture in a single sweep.

"Morning," she said to Fasa. Then, to Deshi: "The wood bin behind the prayer courtyard has harder pieces. Sem, go."

Sem went. He was eleven, dark-eyed, quiet in a way that suggested he was accustomed to running errands without needing them explained. Hala watched him go with the automatic tracking of a mother who counted her children without deciding to.

Deshi looked up from the fire. "I was about to send for the same thing."

"Then we agree," Hala said, and knelt on the opposite side of the fire pit.

Fasa listened to the silence that followed — two women who had just, in three sentences, established that neither would yield the kitchen to the other. She continued slicing onions. The knife was dull; the shrine's tools needed sharpening every few days in the humidity, and no one had done it recently. She could feel the blade dragging rather than cutting, the fibers of the onion separating raggedly under the steel. Her eyes stung. She let them.

The Teshes arrived last. Miro first, a tall man of forty-five who moved with the careful deliberation of someone aware he took up more space than he wanted to and slightly apologetic about it. His hands were large and he used them gently, setting down the flour sack as carefully as if it might crack. Enn followed, her hair wet from the morning rain, wiping her face with the back of her wrist. Her mouth was tight from an argument she'd left unfinished before she arrived.

Gitta came behind them, slower. She carried nothing — Miro had taken her share. She stood at the edge of the overhang where the rain dripped from the stone lip and watched the others settle into the kitchen's geography before choosing her own position. She lowered herself onto the flat stone by the secondary cutting area, folded her hands in her lap, and waited to be needed. Her stillness was different from Suna's — Suna went still to manage crisis, but Gitta went still to disappear, and the discipline of it was practiced enough that Fasa had to remind herself to keep watching.

"We've got lentils, some rice, dried peppers, onions, and turmeric," Suna said, inventorying the stores. "And the flour. That's it from the pantry. We can pull herbs from the garden if someone wants to go down."

"Flatbread," Deshi said. It was not a suggestion. "And a dal. We can stretch the lentils if we cook them long enough."

"Dal needs more spice than turmeric," Hala said.

"Then we use what we have. It'll be bland. People will eat it."

"My father brought cumin seed in his pack," Hala said. "And black pepper. He won't mind."

Deshi paused. The pause was evaluative, and Fasa read it clearly: Deshi was deciding whether to accept help from a woman she hadn't yet ranked in her private hierarchy of domestic competence. Whatever the internal calculus produced, the answer was practical.

"Good. Cumin first, then we'll see."

Enn offered to sort the lentils. She sat beside Gitta and began picking through the dried pulse on a tin plate, discarding stones and shriveled husks with a deftness that had its own kind of authority — her fingers moved quickly, two hands working in complementary arcs, and Fasa could see she had done this thousands of times, that the sorting was not a task she performed but a motion her hands had memorized. Gitta joined her without being asked, and for a few minutes the two women worked in silence, the small clicking of lentils against tin the only sound between them.

Miro took a knife and began cutting onions alongside Fasa. His cuts were slow, methodical, each slice the same width as the last. He did not try to make conversation. Fasa appreciated this. She had noticed that Miro Tesh understood the value of doing a task without narrating it — the habit of people who had spent years alongside a dominant personality and learned that silence was its own contribution. His mother was that personality, though Gitta's dominance expressed itself through quiet rather than volume, through a silence heavy enough to make rooms rearrange around it.

Suna measured rice into the largest pot. She rinsed it three times at the cistern — Fasa heard the water running and then Suna's footsteps on the wet stone, returning — and set the pot over the fire that was now, with Sem's harder wood, burning clean and steady. The rain continued, lighter than yesterday, tapping on the overhang in the irregular rhythm that had become the compound's background music.

The flour came out in a shared bowl. Deshi took half and began mixing it with water, her hands working the dough with an assurance that bordered on aggression — each fold sharp, the heel of her palm coming down hard and exact, flattening the stubborn dry streaks. Hala took the other half. She mixed her dough more slowly, adding water in careful increments, and when she began to fold, she used two hands where Deshi used one, turning the dough over itself in a motion so similar to Deshi's that Fasa's own hands stopped moving on the cutting board.

They were making the same bread.

The technique was not identical — Deshi folded three times to Hala's two, Deshi used less water, Hala oiled her hands and Deshi floured hers — but the fundamental motion was the same. The press-fold-turn, the way they tested thickness by pushing two fingers into the center of the round and reading the give beneath, the rotational shaping that brought the dough from lump to disc with a quick inward turn of the wrist, too ingrained to teach and too ordinary ever to name. Neither woman had noticed. They were each absorbed in the rhythm their hands knew, the unconscious mechanics of a task performed so many times it had ceased to be learned and become simply known.

Suna saw it. She was kneeling by the fire, feeding it a piece of the harder wood, and her gaze moved from Deshi's hands to Hala's and back. She caught Fasa's eye across the cutting board. Neither of them spoke.

Fasa watched for another full minute before Hala glanced sideways and stopped mid-turn. The dough sagged over her oiled knuckles, one edge stretching thinner than the other. She stared at Deshi's hands — at the three-fold motion, at the heel-press, at the two-finger test — and recognition knocked across her face, the off-balance jolt of seeing your own muscle memory in a stranger's body.

"Your fold," she said.

Deshi, feeling the attention, looked up. Then she looked at her own hands, as though seeing them for the first time in years. Then she looked at Hala's dough, at the two-fold rounds forming under Hala's oiled palms.

A silence opened between them that was different from the competitive silence of the morning. This one had texture and weight, and Fasa could feel both women reaching for an explanation that would make the sameness ordinary.

"Where'd you learn?" Deshi asked.

"My mother. And hers before that."

"Same." Deshi turned her dough over, pressed it flat. "Three folds, though. Makes the layers softer."

"Two's enough if your flour is fine-ground."

"It's not fine-ground here."

"No," Hala said. She looked at the coarse communal flour on the stone. "It isn't."

Hala added a third fold. The concession was small and undramatic — one extra turn of the wrist, one adjustment to a lifelong habit — but Fasa heard it for what it was: one woman revising her technique because another woman's point was good. Deshi did not acknowledge the concession, which was its own form of respect — the respect of assuming competence rather than praising it.

They worked side by side. The flatbreads went onto the hot stone over the fire, and the compound filled with the smell of bread cooking — yeasty, charred at the edges where the dough pressed against the stone, the smell that belonged to bread everywhere it met open fire. Fasa breathed it and felt something shift in her chest that she would not have named if asked. Deshi's tighter rounds and Hala's flatter ones blistered the same way. She kept watching.

Gitta's voice interrupted the pleasure. "I can make something with the lentils."

Fasa turned. Gitta had finished sorting and sat with the clean pulse in a clay bowl balanced on her thin knees. Her hands were folded over the bowl's rim. Her tone was mild — the elderly-polite register Fasa had catalogued as Gitta's public mode from the first day.

"What did you have in mind?" Suna asked.

"There is a way to prepare them," Gitta said. "You crush the lentils first — not to powder, but rough, so the edges break. Then you toast the cumin in oil until it smokes. The turmeric goes in at the same time, not after. Then the crushed lentils and water, and you cook it thick. Not thin like a dal. Thick enough that it holds the shape of the spoon when you lift it."

She spoke as if the recipe had already arranged itself in her mouth, each instruction landing in its exact sequence like beads on a string. Fasa had heard her speak this way before — at the first naming, declaring Fen as her grandson without a flicker of hesitation. The same careful placement. The same certainty.

"That sounds like something my father makes," Hala said. "A tadka, but thicker."

"It is not the same," Gitta said.

Then she stopped. Her hands tightened on the bowl's rim.

"Gitta?" Enn said from beside her.

"I have never made this." Gitta's voice stayed level, but the certainty in it split open, and what showed through was a bewilderment so raw that Fasa felt her own hands go still on the cumin she was grinding. "I know every step. I can taste it, the way it should taste. But I have never made it."

The kitchen went quiet in a smaller, more private way, six women and one man recognizing that something strange had happened in a place where strange things had been happening for days. Deshi looked at Gitta. Hala looked at Gitta. Suna's hands stopped moving on the pot she was adjusting over the fire. Miro, who had finished the onions and stood with the knife loose in his hand, turned toward his mother with an expression Fasa could not fully read — concern, yes, and some harder thing beneath it. His mouth stayed half-open for a beat, as if he had expected the next ordinary sentence and had not gotten it.

Gitta recovered. It took three seconds, and Fasa counted them because counting was what she did when something mattered. Gitta's hands loosened on the bowl. Her face smoothed, each line settling back into the arrangement Fasa had come to recognize as Gitta's public architecture.

"Perhaps I watched someone make it, years ago," she said. "These things stay in the hands."

"They do," Fasa said. She said it because the silence needed filling, and because she wanted Gitta to know that her statement had been heard and would not be challenged in this room. Not now. Not yet.

Gitta made the lentil dish as if something inside her were calling the steps. Fasa watched her hands — how she crushed the lentils between her palms rather than with a stone, how she judged the oil by holding her open hand over the pan, how she stirred the thickening mixture: slow, counterclockwise, three turns and a pause. Every movement carried the weight of habit, and that was what made it uncanny: knowledge with that much muscle behind it had to come from somewhere.

Twenty years of the naming, and Fasa had never seen it touch an adult. The children were porous — everyone said so, as though children's permeability to outside influence were a natural law that required no explanation. But Gitta was seventy. Her hands had belonged to the same body for seven decades. Whatever knowledge was moving through them now had not entered through the ordinary channels of learning, watching, doing.

Fasa ground pepper into the dal and kept her face arranged. She would bring this to Olet later as observation, with interpretation held back for now. Gitta Tesh knows a recipe she has never made. The manifestations may not have a lower age limit. They may not have any limit at all.

For now, the food cooked. The flatbreads piled up on a wooden board, each one slightly different — Deshi's tighter and more layered, Hala's flatter with a golden char from the slightly hotter side of the stone, the ones Suna made thicker and less even but perfectly good. The dal thickened. Gitta's lentil dish sat in a smaller vessel near the edge of the fire, its surface dark with toasted spice, filling the overhang with a fragrance that did not belong to any of the three families who had contributed ingredients. The rain had eased to a fine mist, and the kitchen felt, for the first time since they'd all arrived at the shrine, like something other than a site of obligation.

---

The custodians' bell called the compound to eat. Fasa rang it herself — she preferred the bell to her voice for routine summons, saving the lower resonance of her speaking voice for the naming, where it served a different purpose.

They gathered in the main hall, forty-three people and the custodians settling onto the stone floor in the pattern that had become habitual over twelve days. The families still formed clusters, but the edges no longer held: Suna's children drifted to the Mera family's outer ring, Arash sat a body-length from Venn, and the Leos spread their folded blanket in their corner while Pik set his tin cup at its edge like a boundary marker.

But today the food pulled harder than habit. The pots and the flatbread board arrived in the hands that had made them, and when they were set on the flat stones in the middle of the hall, the room leaned in. The smell did it — charred bread, thick lentils, toasted cumin blooming dark in oil. Nessa, Ada on one hip, tore a flatbread open and passed the larger half across Bram's bowl to Tomas without breaking the motion.

Kal came in last with his bowl already in hand and lowered himself beside Peyam as if he had been sitting there every day of his life. Peyam shifted his knee to make space and nudged the pepper pouch toward him.
"You brought it," Peyam said. "You can guard it."
"Guard your own pepper," Kal said.
The pouch stayed between them like a shared joke.

The children's clump had already formed. Kiri Tesh stood behind Rimi Mera with three sections of hair threaded between her fingers and a strip of cloth clenched in her teeth. "Hold still," she said, and when she jerked her chin, Pik Leo held out the ribbon end with solemn concentration. Asha leaned into Melie Parra and stole lentils from Melie's bowl until Melie gave up and shoved the bowl between them. Ori watched over the rim of her own dish, pretending not to smile. Nori Dao kept eating as if someone might remember to take the food back.

Raya, thirteen, and Sem, eleven, lingered at the edge of the group, old enough to feel above it and not old enough to stay away. Fen Tesh stayed near the wall, shoulders rounded over his bowl. Leni watched all of them while she chewed, wary and intent.

People ate. The dal was bland, as Deshi had warned, but the flatbread was good — warm and layered, with enough char at the edges to give it texture — and Gitta's lentil dish was something else entirely. Dense, fragrant, with a complexity of flavor that drew second tastes from people who had been eating without interest for days. Jin Parra lifted his spoon and looked at it, surprised, the way a person registers an unexpected kindness from a direction they weren't watching. Bram Okoye took a second helping and said, quietly, to the room at large, that it was the best thing he'd had since they arrived.

Enn tasted her mother-in-law's dish and turned to Gitta. "Where's this recipe from?"

"I am not sure," Gitta said. She said it with the deliberateness she brought to everything, each word placed rather than spoken, and the care made the uncertainty heavier than a shrug would have been. Enn looked at her a moment longer, waiting for more, and when more didn't come she turned back to her bowl.

Mava Kondo began telling a story. The children had asked for it — or rather, Asha had climbed into Mava's proximity with the imperious confidence of a four-year-old and said the word "tell," and the other children had arranged themselves in a ragged semicircle that constituted a request no one with a story in them could refuse. Mava settled her back against the wall, adjusted her shawl over her shoulders, and spoke.

The story was old. Fasa could hear its age in the rhythm — the repetitions that belonged to oral tradition rather than written composition, the formulaic openings that marked a tale passed through generations of telling. A family of birds lived on one side of a flooding river. Another family of birds lived on the other side. A storm tore the nests of both families loose from their trees and carried them downstream, tumbling and breaking apart, until the tangled remains of both nests caught in the branches of a single tree at the river's bend. The birds could not separate what was left — the twigs and grass and feathers had woven together too tightly — and so they rebuilt around each other, and the new nest was larger and stranger than either family's had been, and it held twice as many eggs.

Mava told it with an unhurried cadence worn smooth by repetition. Her voice dropped on the repetitions and rose on the moments of change. Her dialect surfaced in the telling — old-language phrasings that Fasa recognized but could not place exactly, the vowels rounder and the consonants softer than the common speech they all shared, as though the story itself demanded a different mouth. By the second refrain the children had gone motionless. Even Fen, from his position by the wall, had stopped eating.

The adults listened too, though most of them pretended they were doing something else. Bram's head was tilted, his glasses catching the light from the open side of the hall. Nessa had stopped bouncing Ada. Suna was looking at her own hands on her bowl as though the story were playing there. Venn Kondo sat with his bowl balanced on his knees and his eyes on his aunt, his face loosened into listening. When Mava came to the refrain, his mouth moved with hers on the last words, almost soundless.

Fasa watched the room narrow around Mava's voice. No one reached for more bread. The children stayed down in their ragged semicircle; even Fen held his spoon suspended near his mouth. Forty-three people and the custodians sat in a stone hall above the flood, listening to a story about two nests caught in the same branches. The rain had stopped while Mava was speaking. Fasa had not noticed when.

Through the hall's open side, she could see the courtyard, and on the courtyard steps she could see Peyam and Kal. They had taken their bowls outside and eaten there, and now Peyam's pipe was out — his personal possession, the clay pipe with a bowl darkened by years of use that he kept wrapped in cloth in his pack. Kal sat beside him, his broken-clasped vest open in the humidity, and they passed the pipe between them with the wordless choreography of two men who had learned each other's rhythm without deciding to. Smoke rose from the pipe in a thin line that the mist smeared before it reached the overhang.

Their talk stayed on roads, weather, what this year's monsoon was like compared to past years. Fasa could hear fragments through the open side of the hall. Practical talk, unhurried, the kind of exchange that had no destination and required no conclusion.

"Rains were heavier last year in our district," Kal said. His voice was lower than Peyam's, rougher, with a rasp that might have been tobacco or might have been the grain of how he'd always spoken. "And the year before. The whole lowland flooded out."

"Always heavier in the east."

"Depends where you draw the line for east."

Peyam took the pipe back and considered this. "East is east," he said. "A man knows it when he looks at it."

"That's what a western man would say."

Peyam's silence was amused. The pipe passed. The rain had stopped and the light had gone silver where the clouds thinned over the valley, and the two old men sat in the silver light with smoke between them and nothing urgent to say.

Fasa watched them. Peyam tamped the pipe with his thumb and passed it back. Kal took it without looking away from the valley.

She sat with her empty bowl on her knees and watched the hall. Suna crossed to the children's clump and laid the end of her shawl over Asha and Melie where they had tipped together in sleep. Jin looked up, met Suna's eyes once, and did not reach to reclaim his daughter. At the cistern Wynn Leo took the communal pot from Deshi before she could push herself upright.
"I've got it," he said.
Deshi gave him a look sharp enough to cut dough, then let go.

When Bram held out his bowl with an embarrassed lift of the eyebrows, Gitta scraped the last of the lentils into it without making him ask twice. Arash had moved down beside Mava's circle and let Ori lean against his thigh while the story worked its way toward the final repetition. In the Dao corner Hala bent to hear something Bett said, and Bett's mouth softened around the end of the sentence.

The mist thickened and thinned outside the hall's open side. The valley below stayed drowned — brown water where the road should have been, the lower switchbacks gone under, the flood muttering below the hilltop. Tomorrow afternoon, when she rang the naming bell again, the compound would contract around it as it had around every bell since the flood. Jin's cracked story waited. Venn's resistance waited. Gitta's impossible dish sat cooling in its pot with the spoon against the rim.

But not yet. Now the courtyard was silver. Peyam passed the pipe to Kal. Mava's voice dropped to the last lines of the story. On the floor by the wall, Asha had one foot over Tomas's ankle, Melie's cheek was against Asha's shoulder, and Pik slept against Sem's hip with his tin cup tipped on its side beside him.

She rinsed her bowl at the cistern. Cold water slid over her knuckles and down her wrist. Behind her, the hall settled around small sounds: a child breathing through an open mouth, the wooden spoon tapping once against Gitta's pot, Peyam knocking ash from the pipe onto the stone step. Fasa dried her hands on her clothing and went back inside.


# Chapter 10

At the cistern the next afternoon, Jin was lifting the last kitchen jug when Fasa rang the naming bell. Cold water splashed over the stone lip and across his wrists, and for a count of three he let the sound mean nothing — just another instruction, another hour marked — before the weight of it settled. The naming bell, different from the meal bell only in Fasa's way of striking it: three measured beats, a pause, three more.

He lifted the jug by both handles and walked it to the kitchen overhang, where Enn Tesh was banking the afternoon fire. She looked up at him and then past him, toward the main hall. Her mouth tightened. She had flour on her forearms from the evening dough, and she folded them against her chest as if she could keep the kitchen's warmth from leaking out while she was gone.

"Leave it there," she said, meaning the jug. "I'll pour when we're done."

Jin set it on the stone shelf and wiped his hands on his trousers. The water left dark marks on his thighs. The compound was already contracting around the bell's summons: Venn's voice directing the Kondos, a child's sandal scuffing flagstone, Nessa Okoye pulling Tomas by the hand while she settled baby Ada against her shoulder with the other arm. Thirteen days of this, and the movements had become practiced. People found their places without being told.

Melie was already inside. He could see her from the hall's entrance — cross-legged in the children's cluster near the left wall, her shoulder touching Kiri Tesh's, both of them still holding the chalked stones they'd been playing with in the courtyard. She hadn't noticed him yet. She was whispering something to Kiri, her mouth close to the older girl's ear, and Kiri was smiling.

She was so easy here. That was the thing Jin couldn't stop noticing and couldn't stop fearing. Melie, who had barely spoken for the first two weeks after her mother left when she was four, who had stopped asking for stories at bedtime, who had spent the two years since becoming careful with Jin in a way no six-year-old should be careful with her father — that Melie had dissolved somewhere during the first week at the shrine and been replaced by this one, this child who laughed at Pik's jokes and climbed into Suna Mera's lap without asking and had once, three days ago, called Bett Dao by her first name without any honorific, as though Bett were family.

Jin took his place at the back right of the hall, where the Parras always stood. Not a family section — there were only two of them, so they occupied the narrow gap between the Teshes and the stone wall, a margin that suited Jin's preference for being peripheral. The stone was cool through his shirt where he leaned against it. The oil lamps had been lit despite the daylight still showing through the hall's open side, and the combined glow gave everyone's skin a warm cast that felt dishonest, as if the light were trying to make this kinder than it was.

Fasa entered from the custodians' walkway. She wore the same cotton shawl she always wore for the naming — gray, undecorated, wrapped over her left shoulder, giving her silhouette a formal asymmetry. She stood at the center of the hall's curved front wall, where the stone floor was slightly raised, and waited. She always waited. She didn't call for silence; she just stopped, and the room quieted around her, water settling after a stone dropped through it.

Olet was not present. He attended some namings and not others, and Jin had stopped trying to predict the pattern. Today there were four custodians in the hall — Fasa and three assistants, one of whom Jin knew only as the young man who refilled the oil lamps every evening and had a habit of humming under his breath while he worked, a thin tune that sounded like a prayer but might have been a pop song from before the crisis. He stood behind Fasa now with his hands clasped, his humming stilled, his face settled into the deliberate blankness all the custodians wore during the naming.

"The naming," Fasa said. Her voice carried without effort. "We are here to name."

The formula. Jin knew it: the opening words, then the families in turn, then questions wherever Fasa sensed weakness. The first naming had taken less than an hour. Since the manifestations began, the room listened differently. Every declaration sounded like it might split.

Fasa moved through the first families quickly. Venn named the Kondos as if volume could nail all twelve of them in place; Ori's water fear earned a single question and Habel's eyes went to the floor. Bram named the Okoyes with teacherly clarity and got no questions at all. Then Suna stood with Asha on her hip and named the Meras without a husband, the omission bright as a cut Jin recognized from the inside.

After that the hall kept turning. Gitta's hands were steady. Hala spoke for the Daos while Bett sat beside her, watchful and closed, the woman who knew exactly what Melie's throat had sounded like when it narrowed. Cade Leo named his four. Adults stood when called and sat when released. Children leaned against the walls and watched. The hall had been built so everyone could see everyone else, and when Fasa lifted her gaze again there was nowhere for Jin to hide.

Then Fasa looked at Jin.

The transition was nothing — just her gaze shifting — but his body knew first. His jaw locked. The muscles along the left side of his face tightened before a hard consonant, that fraction of a second when his mouth refused to open, and he had to wait it out, push past it, the word coming through like a door forced against a swollen frame.

"Jin Parra," Fasa said. "You and Melie."

He stood away from the wall. His hands went to his sides. He was aware of the stone behind him, the lip of it pressing against his lower back where he'd been leaning, and he missed it — the wall's support — as soon as he stepped forward.

"Two," he said. "Jin and Melie Parra. Father and daughter."

He waited for the formula to release him. Before, it had — two people, the smallest family, nothing to interrogate because there was nothing to see. But Fasa didn't nod. She didn't look away. She stood very still at the center of the hall, her hands clasped at her waist in the pose she used when the naming was about to ask more than someone wanted to give.

"You declared, at the first naming, that your wife died three months ago."

It wasn't a question. Jin felt the words land on his skin like the cistern water — cold, specific, from a source he couldn't stop. A small sound came from the children's cluster — Melie shifting, or Kiri, or one of the other children — and it drove a spike of focus through his skull.

"Yes," he said.

"The naming has noted a connection between Melie and the Dao family." Fasa's voice was steady, measured. Twenty years of this had sanded the hurry out of it; the questions landed cold on their own. "A response in Melie's body that belongs to the Dao line, not to yours. This is connected to your declaration. Do you understand?"

"I understand what you're saying."

"Then I would like to ask about Melie's mother. Three months ago. When, exactly?"

The room was very quiet. Below the shrine, the flood kept rushing — deeper today because of last night's rain, a sound he could usually fold into the background but that was now pressing against the walls of the hall like something waiting to get in.

He'd prepared for this, but not carefully. He wasn't the kind of man who lined dates up in his head and checked where they split. He had one sentence ready, rubbed smooth in his mouth. He had a sentence.

"Before the first hard rains," he said. "At the end of the dry."

"Before the rains." Fasa's voice was patient. "Which week?"

He couldn't name a week. The dead-wife story — neat enough in a form, impossible under a roomful of eyes — buckled under the need to say something that might pass. "Near the turn," he said. "Just before."

"Before the planting. What illness took her?"

He had said illness to someone — he couldn't remember who, maybe Bett, maybe one of the other mothers who had asked about Melie's mama in the way women asked about absent mothers, gently, assuming the worst. He had said illness and illness was what he had.

"A fever," he said. "She got sick and she didn't get better."

"A fever." Fasa repeated it without inflection. "She was young. Was she treated?"

"There wasn't —" He tried for the next sentence and stalled on the d, mouth open, breath scraping high in his throat. Forty-odd people watched him reach for the word *doctor*, and he hated them for watching, hated himself for giving them something to watch, hated *fever* for sounding like the first thin lie a man grabbed when he needed a woman dead.

"There wasn't a doctor," he said. "Not where we lived."

Fasa paused. The pause was its own instrument — a space she opened and then stood in, letting the room's silence do work that her voice did not. When she spoke again her tone had not changed, but the question carried something new beneath it, a pressure Jin recognized as the moment when a ritual stopped being form and became excavation.

"Where is she buried, Jin?"

"At home. In our village."

"Your home village." Fasa's hands had not moved from her waist. Her face had not moved from its ceremonial composure. But her voice leaned into the next words with the slight emphasis of someone placing a stone on a scale. "And you are traveling away from your home village. With Melie. During the crisis."

She didn't finish it as a question. She didn't need to. The direction was the crack and everyone in the room could see it: a man whose wife was buried at home, traveling away from home, away from the grave, with a six-year-old, during a political crisis that made the roads dangerous and the journey senseless unless there was something at the other end more powerful than what he'd left behind.

Jin's hands were shaking. He put them behind his back, pressing his knuckles against his spine, and the posture looked like a man trying to appear controlled but was really a man trying to hold on to the bones in his own body.

"I'm taking Melie to her mother's family," he said.

"Her mother's family. They're expecting you?"

"Yes."

"They know about their daughter's death?"

"Yes."

"When did you tell them? By letter? By messenger?"

He hadn't told anyone anything because nobody had died. The lie sat on his tongue with no letter behind it, no messenger, no answer tucked anywhere in his bag. Fasa stood there with her hands folded, waiting for him to reach for something that wasn't there.

"I sent word," Jin said. His voice sounded wrong to him — flat, detached, as if it were coming from the wall behind him rather than from his own chest.

"When?"

"Before we left." He swallowed. "A few days after."

"A few days after the death." Fasa repeated it in the same even voice, laying his own words back in front of him. "And they answered?"

Now the lie needed paper: a letter folded in his pocket, a reply with a name on it, some uncle or sister waiting at the end of the road. He had none of it. No letter, no reply, no name, because none of it had happened.

"I don't remember the ex—" The word broke apart in his mouth. His lips worked once, then again, and nothing came out but a thread of air that carried farther than speech. By the time he forced sound through, the silence had already told on him, and a quick intake of breath came from the children's cluster — Melie, he was certain, no longer playing, no longer whispering, listening.

"Is there family on her side?" Fasa asked. "Parents, siblings, anyone who might help us understand the connection between—"

"I've answered your questions."

The words came out louder than he intended. Not a shout — Jin didn't shout — but louder, the volume of a man losing control in public and hearing it too late.

"We have not finished this," Fasa said.

Jin looked past her before he could stop himself. Melie had gone still among the other children, the chalked stones idle in her lap. He dragged his eyes back to Fasa and understood that he was trapped in the exact space between his two impossibilities. He could not tell the truth. He could not keep repairing the lie. There was only one thing left that was his to refuse.

"Melie's right there," he said. "I'm not saying another word about her mother with her listening."

The room was absolutely still. Jin could feel each person in it — their heat, their attention, the weight of their watching — and he could feel, behind all of them, the flood's roar from the valley below, and the oil lamps' tiny sounds, the wicks burning, the soft hiss of flame against liquid. Somewhere to his left, in the Okoye section, Leni was watching him with her arms no longer crossed. Somewhere behind Fasa, the young custodian who hummed had gone very still. Bett Dao, in the Dao section, had her eyes on him — steady, not pitying, not hostile, just present — and Jin couldn't bear that either, the gaze of the woman who had saved his daughter, watching him fail to speak about the woman who had walked away from her.

He could tell the truth. He could say it: *she left. Two years ago, when Melie was four, she walked out of our house on a Tuesday morning while Melie was eating breakfast and she didn't come back. She's alive. She's somewhere. I told Melie heaven because what word do you give a six-year-old for a mother who chose to leave?*

But Melie was sitting fifteen feet away with her chalked stones in her lap, and if Jin said it here, said it in front of all these people and a six-year-old who believed her mother was with the angels, it would be worse than the lie. The lie at least gave Melie a mother she could love without complication. The truth would give her a mother who didn't want her.

"I'm not answering the rest here," Jin said.

Fasa looked at him. Her face held its composure, but something moved behind it — a recognition, maybe, that she was standing at the edge of something she couldn't push past without breaking it. Her mouth opened and then closed. For a moment she looked less like a custodian conducting a rite and more like a woman who had just understood, too late, the cost of what she'd been pressing against.

"The naming will note your refusal," Fasa said.

"I'm done."

Jin stepped out of his place by the wall and walked toward the hall's open side. The space between the families' sections was narrow — he passed the Meras, passed Suna's face turned toward him with an expression he wouldn't let himself read, passed Deshi's sharp eyes and Peyam's closed ones — and then the air changed as he reached the open side of the hall, where the stone floor met the sky and the valley fell away below and the flood was visible, brown and endless, covering the roads.

Behind him, Melie's voice.

"Papa?"

Her voice was small. She wasn't frightened — not yet — but knocked sideways by it, her chalked stones idle in her lap because he had stepped out of his place and kept walking. He was supposed to stand there, say the words, sit down. He was not supposed to leave her in the middle of it.

He didn't stop. Behind him, Suna said something low — to Melie, he thought, or to the room, it didn't matter — and then the sound of the hall receded as he stepped onto the courtyard flagstones and kept walking, past the kitchen overhang where Enn's fire was still smoking, past the cistern where he'd filled the water jugs an hour ago, past the terraced garden with its herbs going leggy in the monsoon heat, to the outer wall of the compound where the stone was rough-cut and body-warm from the afternoon sun and there was nothing beyond it but the hill's slope and the flood below and the gray-green sky.

***

He sat down with his back against the wall. The stone pressed against his spine through his shirt. He put his hands on his knees and looked at them — his hands, the dark water-marks still on his trousers from the cistern — and he tried to think about anything other than Melie's voice saying his name and the sound of it landing in the silence he'd made by leaving.

*Papa?*

He pressed his palms flat against his kneecaps. The fabric was damp. The air smelled like rain and wood smoke and the mineral tang of wet stone, and inside the hall Fasa's voice went on, muted by distance, asking someone else's family their questions, the ritual proceeding as if his refusal were a stone thrown into a river, a disruption the current absorbed without changing course.

Out here, away from the walls, the shame changed from spectacle to body. Inside the hall it had been forty-odd faces turning toward him at once. Outside it came as short breath, a pull between his shoulder blades, the stupid need to keep staring at the damp marks on his trousers instead of the valley. Nobody could see him now. He still couldn't straighten up.

He thought about the first time he'd said it. Not to the naming — before that, three months ago, at the transit office where he'd applied for the travel documents. By then Reva had been gone almost two years, and the truth had hardened into something impossible to explain in the blank space beside *marital status.* He'd looked at the form and the word *married* was there and the word *widowed* was there and neither was true, but *widowed* was closer, because she was gone, because she might as well be dead, because *abandoned* was not a category the form offered and *separated* would have required an address for her and he had no address because she had given him nothing. Not a note, not a reason, not even the courtesy of a fight she could have won.

So he'd checked *widowed.* And the woman at the transit office hadn't looked up, hadn't asked, had stamped his papers and handed them back. That was the first lie. Every one since had been a child of it — quick, easy, each one costing less than the last until the cost had become invisible, until *she died* was as natural in his mouth as *good morning* or *please* or *thank you for letting us stay.*

The lie had worked because nobody asked follow-up questions. A dead wife shut people up. Their faces softened. Their eyes slid away before the next question could form. They could imagine the grave well enough without making him point to it. Until Fasa, until this place with its patient questions, that had been enough.

Now it hadn't.

He thought about the morning she left. He thought about it most days, but here, with the stone warm against his back and the compound's noise distant and the flood rushing below, the memory had the quality of something being pressed through a sieve — only the hardest parts getting through. Two years had not softened any of them.

The shoes. She had put on her shoes. That was the part that kept him, because putting on shoes was nothing, was ordinary, was what you did before you walked to the market or the neighbor's or the well. She had been wearing the brown sandals with the strap that needed fixing, and she had bent to buckle them at the door, and four-year-old Melie had been at the table eating rice with her fingers because Jin hadn't washed the spoons yet, and Jin had been standing at the stove watching the kettle and he hadn't said anything because there was nothing to say about a woman putting on her shoes.

She'd straightened up. She'd looked at Melie — not at Jin, at Melie — and the look was the thing Jin couldn't parse, even now, even after two years of parsing. It wasn't guilt. It wasn't grief. It was something closer to a decision that had already been made, a door that was already closed, and the woman on the other side of it was just going through the physical motions of walking out. She looked at Melie like she'd already lost her.

She said, "I'll be back."

She wasn't.

Melie had finished her rice and asked where mama was and Jin had said *out* and then *she went to the market* and then, later, when the market answer stopped working, *she had to go somewhere far away* and then, later still, when Melie's questions grew more specific and her eyes grew more careful, *heaven, baby, mama went to heaven, she's watching you from there.*

Heaven was a better country than the truth. In heaven, mama loved you. In heaven, mama hadn't chosen the brown sandals with the broken strap and the door and the road over the girl at the table with rice on her fingers.

The naming bell rang — three beats, the closing signal — and Jin let his head fall back against the wall and closed his eyes.

He didn't know how long he sat there. The light shifted. The compound's noise changed register — naming-formal to evening-casual, the bell's authority replaced by the smaller sounds of families returning to their sections, children being collected, the kitchen fire being rebuilt for the evening meal. Hala called for Sem. Venn said something to Tila, too low to make out, in the tone he used when he wanted something done without being seen wanting it.

He didn't hear Melie.

Suna had her. He was sure of it without being told. Suna had three children of her own; she would have put Melie on a mat, checked the bowl for nuts, torn the flatbread small. She would not be explaining anything. She knew, he was starting to think, what it was to put the gentler story in a child's hands and keep the sharper one in your own mouth.

The sky above the valley was turning the color of a bruise — purple-gray, the monsoon sunset that wasn't really a sunset, just the light failing through cloud cover until everything went flat and then dark. Jin watched it happen. Shame moved with all of it — the warmth leaving the wall at his back, the insects starting their evening pulse, the darkness thickening behind his closed eyes — patient enough to outlast him.

The worst part wasn't that they'd caught him. The worst part was that everyone in the hall would now be constructing their own version of what he was hiding, and most of their versions would be worse than the truth. They'd think he'd done something to her. They'd think he was running from a crime, or a debt, or a violence he'd committed and couldn't face. They'd look at Melie and wonder if she was safe with him, and the wondering would be reasonable, because a man who lied about his wife's death was a man whose other claims were now suspect, including the most basic one: that he was a fit father.

The truth was just sad. His wife had left. No kidnapping, no murder, no dramatic departure. She had buckled her sandals and walked through the door and the door had closed and that was it. Two years of managing Melie alone. Two years of the specific daily grief that was not dramatic enough to inspire sympathy and not small enough to dismiss — the grief of being chosen against, of being the thing someone looked at and decided to leave behind.

Footsteps scraped the flagstones. Not a child's step — heavier, deliberate, the walk of a tall man who was choosing to announce himself. Jin opened his eyes.

Bram Okoye stood a few meters away, his glasses catching the last of the failing light. He was wearing a sweater his wife had patched at one elbow — green fabric against brown, the seam slightly puckered where the needle had caught — and the patch was the kind of detail Jin normally wouldn't notice but that, in this moment, with his defenses down to nothing, registered with an absurd clarity: someone had fixed this. Someone had looked at the torn elbow and decided it was worth mending, and the person wearing it had let them.

Bram didn't say anything. He walked to the wall, a body's width from where Jin sat, braced one hand on the stone, and lowered himself carefully until he was down. He leaned his back against the wall. He stretched his legs out. His hands settled in his lap.

Then he reached into his sweater pocket and produced a cigarette — a single cigarette, slightly bent, the paper softened from being carried for weeks — and a match. He struck the match against the wall behind him. The sound was sharp and small in the quiet. He lit the cigarette, took a drag, and held it out to Jin without looking at him.

Jin took it.

The tobacco was stale. It tasted like dust and old paper and the ghost of whatever it had been before the crisis, before the roads, before the shrine. Jin pulled the smoke into his lungs and held it and felt the familiar small violence of it — the burn, the tightening, the body's brief refusal followed by its acceptance — and when he exhaled, the smoke dissolved into the evening air as though it had never existed.

He passed the cigarette back. Bram took it. They smoked in turns, not speaking, the cigarette traveling between them in a rhythm that required no negotiation.

The flood sounded different from out here — not the muffled roar that reached the main hall but something fuller, more textured, water moving through the valley with purpose. Within the rush, individual currents separated: variations in speed and depth that the compound's stone walls had been compressing into a single noise. Somewhere below them, a tree or a piece of road infrastructure was being worked at by the current — a low grinding sound, intermittent, patient.

Bram spoke once. "Nessa's got Ada," he said. "She'll be a while." It was nothing — just a man explaining why he was sitting outside instead of inside, why he had time to waste on a wall with a man he barely knew. But it carried a second weight inside it, quiet as the green patch on his elbow: *I'm not here because I have to be. I chose to sit here.*

Jin didn't answer. He didn't need to. The cigarette went back and forth until it was too short to hold, and Bram pinched it out against the stone and put the stub in his pocket — a teacher's habit, Jin thought, or a father's, someone who had learned to clean up after himself in places that weren't his.

They sat. The light finished failing. The valley went dark below them and the compound's oil lamps came on, their warm glow reaching the courtyard in patches. Inside, the evening meal began: the clatter of wooden bowls, children being told to sit, Deshi's voice rising above the others with a complaint about the flatbread that was also, in its way, a proof that the world was continuing, that the daily things were still happening regardless of what had broken in the naming hall.

Melie would be eating. Suna would have checked the food — for cashew, for tree nuts, for anything that might trigger the allergy that wasn't supposed to be Melie's but was. Bett would be watching too. Two women watching his daughter eat because he was out here on a wall, unable to go in, unable to face the room he'd walked out of and the forty-odd people in it who now knew, if not what he was hiding, at least that he was hiding something.

"Thank you," Jin said.

He wasn't sure what he was thanking Bram for — the cigarette, the silence, the decision to sit down in the first place — but the words came out before he could examine them, and they were true in a way that caught him off guard, because gratitude had not been available to him for months and its sudden presence felt like warmth returning to something he'd thought was numb.

Bram looked at him for the first time. In the near-dark, Jin couldn't read his expression clearly, but Bram tipped his head a fraction, the small classroom lean of someone taking an answer seriously. The thanks had landed.

"Yeah," Bram said.

They sat together against the wall as the dark settled in and the compound moved through its evening and the flood kept on below them, patient, constant, the sound of water waiting for something that hadn't happened yet. After a while the custodians' closing bell rang — the signal for the oil lamps to be covered, for the families to return to their sleeping sections, for the day to end — and Bram stood, brushed the stone dust from his trousers, and went inside without ceremony, without looking back. He had sat down. He had stayed. That was all.

Jin stayed.

He stayed until the lamps went out and the compound fell into the breathing quiet of sleep and the only light was the cloud-filtered moon on the flood below and the only sound was the water and the insects and, from somewhere inside the stone walls, a child coughing once in her sleep and then settling.

He would have to go inside. He would have to find Melie and carry her to their mat and lie beside her and listen to her breathe and figure out what he was going to do about the fact that his story was now cracked open in front of everyone and he still couldn't tell the truth — not yet, not here, not with her face turned up to his and the word *papa* in her mouth like a name that meant everything he was afraid he wasn't.

He stood. His back ached where the stone had pressed. His shirt was damp with dew or mist or the monsoon air's perpetual wetness, and his knees were stiff, and the compound was dark, and the only direction available to him was in.

He went inside, into the cloth-partitioned dark and the close breathing of the other families, where somewhere beyond the nearest partition a sleeper's breath caught once and let go.


# Chapter 11

The thing that kept her awake wasn't the rain or the mosquitoes or the stone under the mat, though all three were doing their part. It was Tomas. He'd developed a new sound in his sleep — a small catch before each exhale, a micro-stutter that had nothing to do with dreaming. It sounded like Jin Parra's voice. It sounded like the hesitation Jin had been fighting all afternoon at the naming, that brief lock of the jaw at the front of certain words, except now it lived in her ten-year-old brother's breathing and nobody was talking about it.

Leni lay on her back and listened to it. She counted the catches. Fourteen in the last minute, or maybe twelve — she kept losing count because Ada would grunt and shift against her mother's chest and Leni's tally would reset. Her mother slept curled around Ada on the mat nearest the wall, her breathing slow and deliberate even in unconsciousness, like she was rationing air. Her father was on the far side, the bulk of his back to the room, his glasses folded on the stone by his head.

She sat up. The sleeping quarters were divided by cloth partitions that did nothing for sound and even less for smell — two weeks of bodies and cooking smoke had settled into the fabric like stain. Through the nearest partition she could hear one of the Kondo children coughing, a dry persistent sound that someone would tsk about in the morning. Beyond that, the steady backdrop of the flood — the valley water grinding against its own banks, a noise she'd stopped noticing for days at a time and then suddenly heard again, like a refrigerator cycling on.

She didn't decide to get up. She just did. Her feet met the stone — cool, almost cold, the kind of cold that traveled up through the soles and settled in the knees. She picked her way between mats, stepping over the edge of the Mera family's section where Kael had flung his arm across the gap between families. The main hall was a dark room of breathing. Oil lamps turned low at the exits cast the kind of light that made corners deep and faces unreadable. She counted shapes as she moved — Gitta Tesh was a small curve on her mat near the wall, unnervingly still for a seventy-year-old. Fen slept two mats over from his grandmother, long enough that his feet extended past the mat onto bare stone. He'd kicked his cover off.

She stepped into the covered walkway between the sleeping quarters and the prayer courtyard. It was open on one side, and the rain came in at an angle, misting her left arm and shoulder. She pulled her sleeve down over her hand. The air smelled green and brown at the same time — vegetation growing so fast it smelled alive, mud churning somewhere below. She could see the courtyard ahead, the stone paving slick and dark, and she nearly turned back because the courtyard was usually empty at this hour and empty things at night had a specific weight she didn't want to carry alone.

But it wasn't empty.

Two figures sat on the low wall at the courtyard's far edge, where the paving met the stone lip that overlooked the terraced garden and, beyond that, nothing — the valley, the flood, the dark. They sat with the deliberate closeness of people who had chosen each other and seen no reason to pretend otherwise.

Leni stopped walking. She stood in the shadow of the covered walkway, her wet shoulder against the stone pillar, and she looked.

She recognized Arash Kondo by the set of his shoulders, which were his father's shoulders made leaner, less squared, as if someone had taken Venn's frame and relaxed the hinges. He sat with his elbows on his knees, his head turned toward the other figure, and something about the angle of his neck — tilted, open, listening — was so unlike anything she'd seen in the Kondo family that it took her a moment to be sure it was him. The Kondos held their heads a certain way. Level. Declarative. Arash's head was tilted like a question.

The other figure was Bett Dao. Her hair was down. Leni had never seen it down — during the day Bett wore it pulled back and fastened with a strip of cloth, tight against her head, practical as everything about her. Now it fell past her shoulders, damp from the rain or maybe just the humidity that clung to everything, and it softened her. Her face seemed looser somehow. Less held. She sat with one knee drawn up, her arms wrapped around her shin, and she was talking.
