"The naming is not procedure," Olet said. He had not moved from the wall. "It is how the room hears what is already true."

At Bram's side, Tomas said, "M-mama?" The catch on the first consonant was Jin Parra's, not the boy's. Nessa folded over it. The sound that came out of her was small and wrecked, but Ada woke against her chest and began to scream. Ori flinched so hard Habel had to lock both arms around her. Nori buried her face in Bett's shoulder. Voices started at once from three corners of the hall, and the fragile quiet of the day tore.

"We can't keep saying the old lines," Suna said. She had Asha in her lap and one palm over the child's ear, but her eyes were on Fasa. "Not after this. Either the naming changes or it admits it has fallen behind the room."

"It changes when you tell the truth in full," Fasa said, louder now. "One family at a time. One voice at a time."

Miro Tesh half rose from the corner. "You keep asking for full truth and one day you'll get more than this place can hold."

Enn grabbed his sleeve. Gitta's hand came down over his wrist to force him back, and even from across the hall Venn could see that her fingers were shaking.

Bram started again. Hala cut across him. Cade had not sat down. Vera was still holding Pik's wrists. The voices piled over one another until Fasa's ritual cadence snapped and she shouted, "Silence."

The word struck the stone hard enough to make even Ada stop for a beat. The oil lamps jumped in their glass chimneys. Venn sat in the middle of his family and listened to the order break apart — voices crossing, children crying, nobody waiting to be called on anymore. Habit had carried them this far. Habit was gone now.

He looked at Arash. His son was at the far end of the Kondo group, and at some point during the chaos he had turned his body slightly toward the middle of the room, away from the Kondo wall. The change was slight; a casual watcher might have missed it. Venn saw the gap anyway: his son had eased out of the family line and angled himself toward whatever came next.

"Arash."

His son looked at him.

"Come sit with us. With your family."

Arash held his gaze for a long moment. Then his eyes slid past the people in the hall and settled on the rain falling in the courtyard beyond the open side, and he did not move back.

Fasa lifted both hands and spoke before Bram or Hala could begin again. "We are not finishing the naming tonight," she said. Her voice still carried the shape of ritual, but the strain was in it now for everyone to hear. "Go back to your families. Tomorrow evening you will return, and you will consider what this day requires you to say."

The families dispersed, not in their usual patterns and not with the grudging orderliness that seventeen evenings of practice had established. The Daos went quickly — Hala steering Bett and the children toward their sleeping area with the purposeful stride of a woman removing her people from a situation. The Meras separated: Deshi and Peyam one direction, Suna and the children another, and Venn noticed that neither group looked back at the other. The Okoyes stayed where they were, Nessa's tears drying on her face, Bram holding Ada, his face gone flat with the knowledge that he had argued precisely and accomplished nothing. The Leos moved as a unit, Cade and Wynn bookending Pik, Vera behind them, all of them walking with the careful gait of people trying not to be noticed.

The Teshes moved last. Miro helped Gitta to her feet, and she rose slowly, and Venn saw — because he was watching everything tonight with the desperate attentiveness of a man whose world was cracking — that the shaking had not stopped.

Age had never made Gitta Tesh shake like this. She was seventy, but her hands had been steady throughout their confinement — steady when she declared Fen her grandson on the second day, steady through every naming and every communal meal, steady when she'd produced a recipe she couldn't explain knowing and served it without her voice wavering. Her hands were shaking now in a fast, dry vibration that ran from knuckle to wrist, and she clasped them tighter in her lap to stop it and they kept shaking.

Nobody else seemed to notice. The room was too full of its own grief. But Venn noticed, because he had spent his life reading families and he knew the signs of a person nearing the end of endurance. He had seen it in Suna Mera's eyes during the second naming. He had seen it in Jin Parra's face when Fasa's questions broke his story. He saw it now in Gitta Tesh's hands, and some plainer part of him, stripped for a moment of patriarchal claim and reduced to a man who had watched too many things break in seventeen days, understood that whatever Gitta was carrying, it was heavier than a recipe, and close to breaking her open.

He stood. The hall was changing shape around him, families drawing back toward their mats and corners. Tila had Ori by the hand, leading her toward the Kondo sleeping mats along the inner wall, keeping her to the sheltered side of the hall where there was no view of the courtyard and its standing water. Habel followed. The other Kondo children trailed after, subdued, wide-eyed.

Arash was not among them.

Venn stood in the hall and looked at the empty space where his son had been sitting, and then at the doorway through which the Daos had disappeared, and he did not go looking. He did not need to. He knew what Arash was moving toward with the same certainty he knew his own name — not because he had proof but because the evidence had been accumulating for days, in Arash's morning absences and evening distances and the careful blankness he showed when Venn looked at him, and the confrontation with Bett had not caused it but had made it impossible to keep pretending he didn't see.

The family he administered — the twelve Kondos traveling to a wedding that now seemed as distant and as abstract as the capital — still occupied one side of the hall. What was coming apart was the idea beneath it: that the Kondos began and ended with the Kondos, that the border he had enforced for twenty-two years was real stone instead of a line drawn in dust.

Sometime in the slow dispersal, without anyone announcing it, the sleeping mats shifted into new borders. The Kondos tightened along the inner wall. Arash made his place near the south wall with his back turned. Bett Dao sat upright against a far column while Hala kept one hand on her knee.

He stayed where he was in the main hall of the transit shrine until the oil lamps guttered low and the room took on the false stillness of people lying down without sleep. The rain fell. The flood, indifferent and immovable below them in the valley, made its low unending sound. From the Kondo mats along the inner wall, Ori whimpered, and the sound carried through the thin stone walls the way all sounds carried in this place — as if the shrine had been designed to make sure nothing stayed private, nothing stayed contained, nothing remained the property of any single family for long.


# Chapter 14

Melie's hand found the hem of Jin's shirt in her sleep and stayed there. He sat with his back against the west wall of the main hall, her body curled against his thigh, her breathing damp on his forearm, the stone's cold coming through his shirt. Around them, in the dimness before the oil lamps were relit, the compound was still a room of people who had given up pretending to rest — knees drawn up, blankets twisted, the particular stillness of bodies that had stopped trying to sleep and were instead waiting for morning to give them permission to move.

The previous night's rupture had dragged the sleeping mats into new borders. The Kondos were pulled tight around their sleeping mats in a knot that excluded Arash, who slept or did not sleep near the south wall with his back to his father. Bett Dao sat upright against the far column with her legs stretched out in front of her, Hala's hand on her knee, though both of them were awake. The Okoyes were a cluster around baby Ada. Suna Mera had her three children arranged close to the kitchen doorway. The Leos kept to their corner, Vera's arm around Pik.

Jin watched them all. He had become a watcher over these eighteen days, and watching was the only thing he was good at here. Good at watching, bad at everything else — at talking, at declaring, at holding his own story together in front of the whole hall after they had heard it crack.

Melie stirred. Her fingers tightened in his shirt. She did this in her sleep now, as though she needed proof he was still there. He didn't move.

When the custodians' bell sounded — one strike, flat and dull — no one seemed surprised. It was too early for the naming. It didn't matter. Fasa appeared in the doorway from the covered walkway, her hair wrapped in cloth, her face carrying the exhaustion she usually kept out of her public bearing. She didn't announce anything. She stood and waited for the room to organize itself into the semicircle.

It took longer than usual. People got up heavily. Venn Kondo stood, and for the first time since Jin had been at the shrine, the man did not summon his family into formation. The Kondos gathered around him by habit, but the shape was looser than before. Tila touched Venn's back — a small, steadying gesture that Jin noticed because Venn usually planted himself hard enough to steady other people.

Jin lifted Melie. She woke partially, her face pressed into his neck, her breath sour with sleep. He carried her to the edge of the semicircle and sat on the stone floor with her in his lap. She was six and heavy, and the weight of her against his chest was the most grounding thing left in his life.

Fasa stood in the open space between the families and the custodians' bench. Olet sat behind her, his face as unreadable as it had been on the first day. The three assistant custodians flanked him. Beyond them, through the hall's open wall, the valley lay under gray light, the brown flood still holding the roads below.

"The naming will proceed," Fasa said. Her voice was quieter than usual. "After last night, I know some of you would rather it didn't. The naming does not ask what you would rather. It asks what is true."

She paused. The pause was long enough to feel deliberate — the liturgist's pause, Jin thought, the one she used before she called a family's name.

But it was Gitta Tesh who spoke.

"I would like to speak."

The voice was small. It came from the Tesh section of the semicircle, where Miro stood with his hands at his sides and Enn sat on the floor with Kiri pressed against her. Gitta was on a low stool someone had brought for her days ago, the stool that stayed in the naming circle because her knees made the stone floor impossible. She sat very straight. Her hands were in her lap, one folded over the other. They were not shaking.

Jin noticed that. After the previous night, when her hands had trembled so visibly that Suna had watched them from across the room, the stillness was worse.

Fasa looked at Gitta for a long moment. Something passed between them. Fasa's mouth set; Gitta did not look away. What was coming had already gathered too much weight to stop. Fasa stepped to the side.

"Speak," she said.

The silence that followed was the silence Jin would remember later, when everything else about the day had blurred at the edges. He would remember the pressure before it: forty-eight people holding their breath, the flood below, an oil lamp guttering near the east wall, Melie shifting in his lap with her fingers still wrapped in his shirt.

Gitta did not stand. She spoke from the stool, her eyes directed at a point on the floor a few feet in front of her. She spoke slowly, and each word arrived with the same weight as the one before it, so that the sentences had no peaks and no valleys, just the level surface of a woman who had been rehearsing this for sixteen years and now no longer needed to rehearse.

"My name is Gitta Tesh. My son is Miro. His wife is Enn. Their daughter is Kiri." She paused. "And Fen."

Fen was standing behind Miro. Jin could see him — tall, dark-haired, still as something carved. His face was open, unbraced, untouched yet by what was about to hit it.

"I have called Fen my grandson," Gitta said. "I still do. But he is not Miro's son, and he is not the child of any relative of ours."

The room did not react at first. The words were out in the air like something that had been dropped, and no one had decided yet whether to pick them up.

Miro turned to look at his mother. Jin searched for the right word and found none. Miro's face emptied. He stared at her as if the room had shifted under a fact he had always stood on without looking down.

"Sixteen years ago," Gitta said, "I traveled this road. Not this road exactly. A road south of here, in the foothills. It was during the trouble then — the registration sweeps, the papers. You remember." This last part was addressed to no one and to everyone. Some of the older adults — Deshi, Mava, Peyam — shifted slightly. They remembered.

"I was walking to my sister's village. Alone. Miro was at home with Enn. They had been married a year and had no children yet." She paused. Her hands stayed flat in her lap. "I found them on the road. A man. A woman. A girl, maybe eight or nine. They had been dead for — I do not know how long. Days, maybe. Maybe less. The heat was bad. There were flies."

Jin felt Melie's hand tighten on his shirt. She was awake, watching Gitta with the unfocused attention of a child who knows something serious is happening but cannot locate its source. Jin put his hand over hers.

"The infant was alive." Gitta's voice did not change. "He was lying beside the woman's body. He was sunburned and dehydrated and he had stopped crying — he was past crying. He was just making a sound. A small sound, like a hinge." She stopped. Her mouth worked once, quickly, and then she continued. "I picked him up. I carried him to my sister's village. I told my sister he was the child of a woman I knew from the market, a woman who had died in the sweeps and had no other family. My sister helped me feed him. Goat milk at first, then rice water."

She looked up. For the first time since she had begun speaking, she looked directly at someone. She looked at Fen.

"When he was strong enough, I brought him home to Miro and Enn. I told them the same story — a woman from the market, no family, she had asked me to take him. They believed me because I do not lie." Here her mouth pulled tight, the muscles in her cheeks bunching once around the ugliness of what she had just said. "I do not lie, and so they believed the lie."

"I named him Fen. That is not the name his mother gave him. I do not know what name his mother gave him. I do not know his mother's name, or his father's name, or the name of the girl who died beside them. I did not search their belongings. I did not look for papers. I took the child and I left them there, on the road, because the child was alive and they were not, and I could carry one and not four."

The hall broke into small sounds — Nessa Okoye's sharp intake of breath, the scrape of someone's shoe on stone, baby Ada beginning to fuss and being shushed quickly by Bram. Over all of it sat Fen's silence. He made no move, no sound. The skin at his throat worked once, then held.

Jin watched Fen's face. He couldn't stop. The boy's mouth hung slightly open. His eyes stayed on Gitta as though every wall in the room had suddenly shown its seams. Fury hadn't reached him. Grief hadn't either. He was still in the first drop, feet feeling for ground that was gone.

Miro stood up.

That movement broke something in the room. Miro stood before his mind caught up, a broad forty-five-year-old man with thick workman's forearms, his trust in the visible world still plain on his face. His hands went to his sides, his mouth opened, and then he sat down again, hard, as though his legs had quit. Enn's hand went over her mouth. She stared at Gitta as if smoke were already in her lungs.

Kiri, eight years old, still pressed against her mother, looked from face to face with the fierce, hunting concentration of a child who knows the adults have broken something and needs to identify the pieces.

"Why," Miro said. The word came out flat. It was the only word his body could produce.

"I have told you why," Gitta said. "He was alive. They were dead."

"Why didn't you —" Miro's voice cracked. He swallowed. Started again. "Why didn't you tell us. Why didn't you tell me. He's — I've — for sixteen years —"

"Yes."

"He's my son." Miro's voice was raw, and Jin recognized the sound of it — the sound of a man trying to hold onto something that had already changed shape. "He's my son. I raised him. I'm his father."

"You are his father," Gitta said. "That is also true."

Enn made a sound. It was not a word. Her hand was still over her mouth, and the sound came through her fingers — a low keen that she cut off by pressing her palm harder against her lips. Her other arm pulled Kiri closer, as though proximity could protect the child from what was happening. Kiri's face was white.

Jin looked at Fen.

The boy had not moved. He stood in the same position — behind where his father had been standing, now behind where his father sat — with his arms at his sides and his face gone very still. The difference mattered: blankness retreated behind a wall, but this kind of stillness stayed present for every particle of what was happening and still could not find a single movement equal to it.

Jin knew that face. Three months ago he had worn it in his kitchen with Reva's note in his hand and Melie asking him to come look at the horse she had drawn. He had walked to the table and told her it was a good horse because there had been nothing else his body knew how to do.

Fen was falling now. Jin could see it. Nobody else seemed to — they were watching Gitta, or watching Miro, or looking at their own hands because the spectacle of a family coming apart was too naked to witness directly. But Jin watched Fen because he knew what that stillness cost, and he knew the boy could not hold it much longer.

"I am not asking forgiveness," Gitta said.

Fasa, who had been standing to the side with her hands clasped in front of her, lifted her head.

"I stood up because this hall is where hidden things are said," Gitta continued. "I took a living child from a dead family. I lied to my son and his wife. I gave the child a name that is not the name his parents chose for him. I do not know his real name, and there is no one alive who does. I have carried this for sixteen years. I did not come here to be pardoned." She opened her hands — a gesture that included the naming circle, the hall, the custodians, the assembled families. "I came here to say it aloud."

The room held. Jin could feel it holding — the collective suspension of all forty-eight people as they decided, one by one, what to do with what they had heard. Some of them were looking at Fen. Most were looking at Gitta. Venn Kondo was staring at the floor. Bett Dao's face was turned toward the open wall, toward the valley and the flood. Suna Mera had her arms around Asha and her eyes were wet. Her mouth stayed set hard. Jin knew that face from his own mornings at the cistern: recognition had reached her before pity.

Fen moved.

He moved like someone underwater — deliberate, slow, each motion costing something. He stepped backward, away from Miro, away from the semicircle. His foot caught the edge of a sleeping mat and he didn't stumble, didn't look down, just adjusted and kept moving. He crossed the hall toward the open wall. He did not run. He walked through the gap in the stone columns and stepped into the gray morning light and kept walking, down the slope toward the terraced garden, and the room watched him go, and no one followed because no one knew what to follow him toward.

Miro's face was in his hands. Enn had pulled Kiri into her lap, and Kiri was crying with the borrowed grief of the room, loud and helpless because the adults around her had come apart.

Gitta sat on her stool with her hands open in her lap, fingers still slightly curled as though the weight were there. Her shoulders had not dropped. Shock held them up.

Fasa looked at Olet. Olet's face stayed flat. He let an extra beat pass before he spoke.

"The naming has heard Gitta Tesh," Olet said. "The naming does not close until the naming is complete."

"The naming can wait," Fasa said, and her voice held a sharpness Jin had not heard from her before. She looked at the room. "We will pause."

---

The hours after the confession did not blur. Tea still had to be boiled. Children still had to piss and wash and ask questions nobody could answer.

Jin carried Melie to the cistern and splashed water over her face and his own. The water was cold enough to sting. Across the yard, Miro crossed the upper terrace with a tin cup in his hand and called Fen's name toward the garden. The name went out bare across the stone. No answer came back.

Enn stood in the hall opening with Kiri braced against her leg. Gitta was on her stool a few feet behind them, both hands around her prayer beads. When she started to rise, Kiri saw and clutched a fistful of Enn's skirt. Enn shifted without seeming to, just enough to keep the child on the far side of her body. Gitta sat again.

Melie sat on the stone edge of the cistern and swung her legs.

"Papa. Is that boy going to be OK?"

Jin looked at her grimy face, the crease sleep had left on one cheek.

"I don't know," he said.

"His grandma was crying."

"She wasn't crying."

"Inside crying, then."

His hand stopped on the cistern's edge. The stone was wet and rough under his palm, gritty with mineral deposits. Melie was watching him with the blunt certainty he was learning to dread. She knew things. She was six, and she knew things about him he had spent three months believing he was hiding.

"Let's get breakfast," he said.

In the kitchen overhang, Hala Dao was making porridge. She moved with deliberate, slamming force, each action a little harder than it needed to be — the wooden spoon striking the pot's rim, the lid replaced with a crack, the water jug set down with a thump. Hala's way of handling the morning was to cook at it until it gave up. Her daughter Raya sat on the step peeling a tangerine in one long ribbon, staring at the pith in her lap instead of anybody's face.

Bram Okoye came through with Ada on his hip. His glasses were smeared and there was dried milk on his shoulder.

"Seen Fen?" he asked.

Jin shook his head.

"Miro's gone down the terraces three times already," Bram said. "Enn won't leave the hall."

All morning the Tesh corner kept rearranging itself around the missing boy. Miro filled a bowl and carried it to Fen's mat before remembering the mat was empty. Later he took tea to the hall opening, stood there with the steam in his face, and came back with the cup still full. Enn spoke only to Kiri. When porridge ran down the child's chin, Gitta reached automatically with the corner of a cloth. Kiri jerked away hard enough to knock her spoon against the bowl. Enn wiped her clean instead. Gitta folded the cloth and laid it on her own knee.

They sat under the kitchen's stone overhang and ate. The morning was humid and gray, and the flood filled the valley with its usual low rush. The rain was still holding off. The clouds sat heavy over the mountain, waiting.

Near midday, with Melie asleep in the crook of his arm, Jin went to the prayer courtyard for air. From there he could see the lower wall beyond the last herb terrace. Fen was sitting against it with his knees drawn up, staring past the flooded switchback as if the valley might give him back the morning before Gitta spoke. Miro stood over him with a bowl in one hand and both shoulders bent forward, stripped of every easy father's posture. A little way up the path, Enn had come down after him and stopped.

"Fen," Miro said. The boy's shoulders locked at the name. "Come inside. Just eat something."

Fen did not turn.

Miro crouched and set the bowl on the wall beside him. "You haven't had anything."

Still nothing. The flood kept up its low rush below them.

Miro put out a hand toward the back of Fen's neck, the old steering touch of a father guiding a child through doorways and crowded rooms. Fen pulled away before the hand landed, quick and clean. That made it worse. Miro's fingers hung in the air for a second, then closed into his palm.

"I'm still your father," Miro said.

Fen's voice came out rough and flat. "I know."

Enn took one more step and stopped again. "Kiri's scared," she said. "She keeps asking where you went."

Fen dragged both hands over his face. When he spoke again, he was looking at the bowl instead of either of them. "That's her name for me."

No one answered at first. Jin felt the sentence travel all the way up the terraces.

"It's yours too," Miro said.

Fen shook his head once. "I don't know what anything is right now."

Enn made a sound like she meant to go to him. She did not move. Miro looked back at her, then at the boy, then down at the cooling bowl between them.

"All right," he said finally, and there was no authority left in it. "We'll leave you a while."

He said Fen's name once more before he turned away, softly this time, almost to himself. Fen did not answer. Miro picked up the untouched bowl and went back up the terraces with Enn behind him, neither of them close enough to touch the other.

Evening came slowly. The rain started in the late afternoon — heavy, pounding, the kind that turned the courtyard into a shallow lake and made the flood below surge audibly, a sound like a living thing clearing its throat. The families drew inward. The lamps were lit. Fasa did not call another naming.

Jin sat with Melie in the main hall. She was drawing on a scrap of paper Leni Okoye had given her, pressing hard with a stub of pencil, making and remaking the same four-legged shape until the paper went soft under the eraser.

The hall was full and quiet. People talked in low voices. The Teshes occupied their corner like a family under quarantine. Miro and Enn sat shoulder to shoulder with Kiri between them. Gitta kept her stool three feet away. Each time Kiri slumped with sleep, Enn or Miro steadied her first. Once Gitta lifted a hand as if to tuck the blanket higher around the child. Miro had already done it.

He had spent the afternoon carrying things across that gap — a dry shirt for Fen when the rain started, a cup of tea he left cooling at the doorway, a towel folded and unfolded in his lap until the cloth held the shape of his fists. None of it crossed. Enn answered Gitta only when she had to, and never turned her whole body toward her. The distance was three feet. It ran the length of the road.

Gitta sat with the beads in her lap and did not move. Her face gave away nothing. She kept both hands on the beads and her shoulders square, withholding even the smallest reach toward comfort.

Fen did not come back for hours.

When he appeared in the doorway in the small hours, the rain was still heavy. His clothes were soaked through and his hair was plastered to his forehead, and he stood there dripping on the stone threshold, and the room went quiet the way it had gone quiet that morning, in a wave: conversation dying, faces turning, bodies going still.

Fen looked at the room. His eyes were swollen. His jaw was set in the strained, painful way of somebody holding his face together by force. His shirt clung to his chest. He was shivering, though whether from cold or from the day's damage was impossible to say.

He did not look at Miro or Enn. He looked at Gitta.

She looked back at him. She did not stand. Her hands stayed clasped over the beads in her lap. She did not reach for him or speak to him or cry or open her arms or make any of the gestures the moment seemed to demand.

Fen crossed the hall. His wet shoes left dark prints on the stone. He reached Gitta's stool. He stood there for a moment that seemed to pull the whole room tight around it, and then he sat down.

He sat on the floor beside her stool, so close that the space between them was smaller than his hand. He did not lean against her or reach for her. He sat with his head bowed, water running from his hair to the stone between his feet, and his breathing was ragged and audible. Gitta did not move.

Miro made a sound so small Jin almost missed it. Enn's hand closed around Kiri's ankle and stayed there. Kiri watched from between her parents' knees with the raw, sleepless face of a child learning that even names could come loose. At the doorway, Fasa stood watching without moving. She stayed there until the hall had gone still around them, then turned away toward the custodians' quarters. The hall kept its distance. No one crossed to them, and no one said Fen's name.

Gitta had carried her lie for sixteen years. Jin had carried his for three months. Watching Fen sit in the spill of lamplight beside the woman who had taken him from beside the dead family on the road and called him hers, Jin felt the smaller lie under his breastbone cramp hard enough to steal a breath.

Across the hall, Gitta kept both hands on the beads in her lap. Fen sat with his head lowered, rainwater still falling from his cuffs. Miro and Enn watched from three feet away and did not move. Melie's fingers bunched tighter in Jin's shirt. The rain battered the open wall. No one in the hall closed the space between them.


# Chapter 15

In the custodians' quarters, the radio rested in its leather case on the low stone table, and the lamp was turned down to a thread of light that barely pushed back the dark corners of the room. Fasa had not slept since Fen crossed the hall and sat down beside Gitta's stool.

Through the wall she could hear the compound settling — someone shifting on a mat, the rustle of a blanket drawn up against the night's chill, and farther out, the flood's steady rush from the valley floor, and the rain, lighter than it had been in weeks, falling on the stone overhang in irregular clusters.

She kept seeing it.

She had stood in the doorway of the main hall as the boy came back in the small hours, his face drawn tight, and sat down beside the old woman without a word. Gitta had not moved. She did not reach for him, speak, or touch his arm. She had sat with the absolute stillness that Fasa had come to recognize as the woman's deepest register — not the stillness of someone trying to be still but the stillness of someone who had spent sixteen years holding herself in place. Fasa had seen many things in twenty years of the naming: refusals and tears and fury and a man who had thrown a clay cup against the wall and split his own lip on the shards. She had never seen anything as quiet as what passed between those two bodies. The boy chose the woman who had stolen his story by sitting close enough that their shoulders nearly touched, and the woman accepted that closeness without reaching for more.

Fasa had gone back to her quarters after that. She had boiled water in the iron pot and poured tea into the chipped ceramic bowl she had used for twenty years, the one with the hairline crack along the rim that leaked if the tea sat too long, and she had held the warm bowl in both hands and thought.

Now the tea was cold and a thin line of it had seeped from the crack and traveled along the stone table's surface, finding the slight depression where the stone had worn with use. The lamp was low, and the pattern was there, or close enough to frighten her.

She had been reaching for it since the second naming, since Deshi Mera crossed the hall to Mava Kondo as if some older obligation had tugged her there. The manifestations had looked separate only while Fasa forced them into the shrine's old language. Asha rubbing her ear in sleep. Melie's throat swelling shut around the Dao family's allergy. Ori clawing away from a skin of rainwater as if someone else's drowning had entered her bones. Tomas borrowing Jin Parra's damaged consonants from a hurt Fasa still could not fully see. Fen sitting down beside the woman who had taken him from a road full of dead strangers and made him kin by insistence.

For twenty years the shrine had given her a tree: ancestor, branch, hidden root, blood answering blood. She had trusted that shape because it was old and because ritual language liked a clean structure. She could still feel the tree in her mind, and she could feel it failing. A dead sister. A drowned son. An older child held so far inside silence that only the outline showed. A boy claimed out of catastrophe and sealed inside another name. And perhaps, under Jin's ruined story and under Suna's merciless competence, the blunt fact of leaving.

Fasa set the bowl down on the stone table. The cold tea had reached the table's edge and hung there, a bead of dark liquid suspended before it fell.

If she trusted what the nineteen days had shown her, the naming had been asking the wrong question. Not who belongs to whom. Who is missing. What name had been shut out of the room so completely that a child had begun carrying it in the body.

Fasa pressed her palms flat on the table. The stone was cool and slightly gritty — fine mineral dust that the rain washed down from the hillside above the shrine, accumulating on every surface, finding its way into food and fabric and the creases of hands. She pressed until the grit bit into her skin.

She tried to find the ritual language for this. The tripled cadence of the naming's formal speech, the balanced clauses, the liturgical structure that she used every day and that had always, until now, organized her understanding of what the shrine did and why it mattered. She reached for it the way she reached for prayer — automatically, as a structure to hold the weight of what she was feeling.

It did not come. The words she found were plain and flat and insufficient, and no amount of reaching produced the ceremonial architecture she needed. The children kept carrying other families' missing people. Even in her own mind the sentence sounded insane. Worse, it gave her no instruction for how to ask such things without making the naming crueler than it already was.

From the main hall, through the wall, she heard old Peyam Mera begin his morning prayers — the low, rhythmic murmur in his own language, a language he spoke to no one at the shrine except whatever he prayed to. He prayed every morning before anyone else was awake. Fasa had learned to recognize the sound as a marker of the hour: when Peyam prayed, dawn was close.

---

She found Olet on the covered walkway between the custodians' quarters and the main hall, standing where the stone overhang sheltered the passage from the rain. He stood with his hands clasped behind his back, watching the gray morning through the gaps between the overhang's pillars. The rain was thin — not the dense sheets of the previous weeks but a fine, almost hesitant falling, as though the monsoon were losing conviction.

He did not turn when she approached. He had the ability, after decades at the shrine, to identify who was behind him by the sound of their step. She had seen him do this with travelers — naming a returning figure by footfall alone after a single night's stay. His accuracy was uncanny, and he never commented on it.

"You have been awake all night," he said.

"Yes."

"You have been thinking about the Tesh woman's confession."

"About all of them. All the connections."

She stood beside him. The walkway was narrow — two people abreast, their shoulders close to the stone pillars on either side. Moss had grown in the seam where the walkway's base met the overhang's foundation, a bright green line that Fasa had scraped away many times and that always returned within days. Olet had told her, years ago, to stop scraping it. The moss belonged there, he had said. It found the cracks where the water collected and made itself at home. She had thought at the time that he was talking about the moss.

"I've found the pattern," she said.

He waited. This was Olet's response to most statements — not a prompt, not a question, not an encouraging tilt of the head. Simply the space in which to continue, offered without pressure or impatience.

"What is happening." It was not quite a question. Olet's sentences often occupied the territory between question and statement, the rising inflection absent, the silence afterward neither expectant nor closed.

"They're carrying the missing," she said. She could have built toward it, the way she built the naming's questions — from the general to the specific, from the easily answered to the devastating. She didn't. "Asha carried Mava's sister. Ori carried Ren's terror. Fen made visible what Gitta built out of theft and refusal. Tomas is speaking from a wound that belongs elsewhere."

Olet looked at her. His face was the same as it always was — attentive, unhurried, the deep lines around his mouth and eyes offering nothing she could read. She had given up trying to read him around her sixth year at the shrine. His consistency was not a mask. It was simply how he was made, and no amount of crisis or revelation had ever, in her experience, altered the surface.

Rainwater threaded through the courtyard drainage channel below them. Fasa watched it carry bits of leaf and grit toward the far grate. "If I call them back into the hall," she said, "the room fills up. Mava's sister. Ren. The unnamed baby Gitta renamed Fen. A wife who may still be alive. A husband who may have walked out and never looked back."

The rain tapped on the overhang above them, and water ran in the courtyard drainage channel.

Olet did not speak for a long time. The rain fell. A moth that had survived the night circled the walkway's lamp, its wings throwing a quick, frantic shadow against the stone pillar each time it passed the flame.

"Then you know what must be named," he said.

"They don't enter a room the same way," she said. "A dead child comes in one way. A stolen child another. A parent who walked out drags shame in with them. Shame sits differently in a room than grief does."

"Yes."

"Then let them tell us privately," she said.

"No."

The word landed with neither force nor softness.

"Private truth does not complete a public naming," Olet said. "The children have already spoken in front of everyone."

"It's too much." She heard the edge in her own voice, a rawness that the ceremonial composure of twenty years could not smooth. "Gitta Tesh opened herself in front of forty-two people. Jin Parra walked out rather than let his daughter hear what sat behind his lie. Bett Dao had to say Ren because Venn Kondo shouted her into it. And now you want me to ask again, knowing I may make a child hear that her mother isn't dead at all, only gone by choice."

Olet's face did not change. He looked at her with the same even attention he gave to the flood's daily level, the radio's broken dispatches, a child crying in the small hours.

"The naming does not ask for comfort," he said. "It asks for what is true. You have always understood this."

"I've never seen it ask for this much."

"You have never had this many families carrying this many losses in one place."

"That isn't the point."

He paused. The silence between them was not empty — it was the silence Olet used when he was not conceding but allowing the other person to hear what they had said.

"I'm the one who asks," Fasa said. Her voice had dropped — not in volume but in register, lower, quieter, stripped of the cadence she wore in the naming circle like a garment she put on every evening and took off every night. "I'm the one who may have to tell a six-year-old to stand still and listen while her father says your mother is alive. I'm the one who may have to hold the room quiet while another child learns her father never went ahead anywhere. Once I put those words in the air, they don't belong to the family anymore."

"Their children's bodies have already spoken," Olet said. "The secrets are not private. They have not been private since the first manifestation."

She knew he was right. She could feel the truth of it in the ache between her shoulder blades where she had held herself bent forward all night, hunched over the table, tracing the pattern that was now as clear to her as the stone under her feet. The manifestations had already breached every privacy the families had tried to maintain. Ori's screaming had named Bett's drowned child more loudly than Bett would ever have chosen. Tomas's jaw-lock had pointed at Jin's hidden damage before Jin knew anyone was looking. The children's bodies had no discretion. They carried the losses openly, without shame, without the protective stories their parents had built to contain them.

"The naming reaches completion when the truth is spoken," Olet said. "Not when the connections are observed."

"And if they refuse."

"Some will refuse. The naming waits."

"The road is going to open. The rain's already lighter."

"Yes."

"And if the families leave before they've spoken it — before they've named the losses —"

"The road opens when the naming is done." He said it the way he said everything, as a condition of the world, not an argument. "It has always been this way."

Fasa did not argue. She had heard him make this claim before, when he had told Suna and Bram that the flood's duration exceeded what the monsoon alone should explain. She had not fully believed him then. She was not sure she believed him now. But she had lived at this shrine for twenty years, and in twenty years the road had always opened when the naming was complete, and she did not have a competing explanation for the timing.

She straightened. Her tunic was damp where her back had pressed against the pillar during their conversation, the moisture seeping through the worn fabric and settling cold against her skin.

"If I do it the old way, it becomes another tribunal," she said.

"Then do not do it the old way."

"I can change the order," she said.

"Yes."

"Not the truth."

"No."

Olet looked at her for a moment longer. Then he turned back to the rain. The conversation was finished — not closed, not concluded with any formal gesture, simply complete. He returned to what he had been doing before she arrived, and the silence that followed was neither pointed nor uncomfortable. It was the silence of a room after the last person who needed to speak has spoken.

Fasa walked through the courtyard to the cistern.

The cistern was at the compound's far edge, built against the hillside where a spring seeped through a fissure in the rock. The basin was carved stone, wide and shallow, the water clear and very cold even in the humid warmth of monsoon season. She had washed at this cistern every morning for twenty years. She knew its sounds — the thin continuous trickle from the spring's source, the faint hollow echo when the water level was low, the deeper tone when the rains had filled the basin to its rim. Today it was full. The surface trembled faintly with the vibration of the spring's flow, and the reflected sky — gray, low, heavy with the last of the season — shivered and re-formed above the dark stone bottom.

She cupped water in both hands and brought it to her face.

The cold was immediate. She held her hands against her cheeks and let the water run down her wrists, along her forearms, dripping from her elbows onto the basin's rim. Her eyes were closed. Behind her closed eyes the pattern returned as voices. Your mother is alive. She left. Efe was said aloud at last. Ren came back into the room. Your father did not go ahead; he went. Each sentence was plain enough for a child to understand and sharp enough to split a household open. The next naming could not simply lurch from one wound to the next and call that truth.

She opened her eyes. The water's surface gave her back her own face, broken by the spring's tremor, wavering. She looked older than she expected. She always looked older than she expected — the angular face, the roughened hands gripping the basin's stone lip, the deep lines beside her mouth that had appeared in her thirties and only deepened since. She had been at this shrine longer than she had been anywhere. Longer than the house where she was a child, longer than the two years of training before Olet had taken her on, longer than any other portion of her life. She had chosen this — the naming, the shrine, the daily rhythm of prayer and maintenance and the care of strangers — because she believed the naming told the truth.

Jin had chosen a grave because a grave ended the questions. Reva alive would start them all over again: what kind of woman leaves a child, what kind of man gets left, what in that house had curdled badly enough to send her away. Suna's secret would draw the same inventory. She could hear it already: a chair leg scraping stone, someone taking breath through their teeth, Deshi going rigid and saying nothing. A dead child lowered a room into pity. A husband or wife who had simply gone made people listen for fault. It made children hear that they had been left on purpose. She could see Melie's face if the truth was pulled out of Jin in public, the way a sentence like your mother is alive and she left us would split the world in two before breakfast. She could see Rimi going still in the way children did when they understood an adult had lied for a long time and there was no way to put the old story back.

She could not make the naming gentle. She could stop making it lazy. When the naming came again, she would change the order. No child would stand first. No manifestation would be held up for the room to interpret while the adults hid behind it. She would call the adults into the circle and ask for the missing names before she asked for kin: who died, who left, who was taken, who had been turned into family by lie or by necessity.

She splashed more water on her face. The cold ran down her throat and soaked the collar of her tunic. She breathed. The air smelled of wet stone and the green, heavy scent of the hillside after rain, and faintly, from the kitchen, the first smoke of the morning fire — someone had already started the breakfast preparation, and the wood smoke carried across the courtyard and mixed with the clean mineral smell of the spring's water.

She was afraid.

She had been uncertain before. She had questioned the naming's methods — its clumsy insistence on categories that did not fit the families before her, its aggressive pacing, the food-access rule she had always found coercive. She had argued with Olet about these things and lost every time. He simply stood in his certainty until her words wore out. But she had never been afraid of the naming itself. She had believed it was, at its core, an act of recognition.

Now she was afraid of the shape recognition took when it came with an audience. She saw a thumb pressed into a bruise until the body answered. Perhaps that pressure was necessary. She still could not tell the difference between necessity and harm, and that uncertainty frightened her more than the pattern itself.

She dried her face on her sleeve. The fabric was rough — worn thin from years of washing at this same basin with the same coarse soap — and it left a faint rawness on her cheek that she could feel as she straightened and turned back toward the compound.

The families would be waking soon. The children would tumble out of sleep with their appetites and their noise and their bodies that carried, without knowing it, the map of every loss the adults had tried to bury. The morning would begin. Fasa would eat. She would help with the fire. She would be the woman who led the naming — composed, purposeful, her voice carrying the weight of ritual authority that she had earned over twenty years and that now, for the first time, she was not sure she deserved.

In twenty years her faith in the naming had never broken. It had been tested and it had bent under the pressure of reluctant families and impossible manifestations, but it had not broken. What held it now was the choice she had watched between Fen and Gitta in the small hours of the morning — to stay, even when staying cost more than she had known she would be asked to pay.

The breakfast smoke thickened. The rain continued, thin enough now that the compound's other sounds could rise through it again, as if it too were deciding whether to go on.


# Chapter 16

Deshi was still saying something sharp to Rimi about folding mats properly when Suna carried the last stack of bowls to the basin.

Nineteen days in the compound had worn the evening cleanup into her body — scrape, stack, carry, rinse, return. She tipped the final bowl, watched water sheet off the rim, and realized she could hear Ada Okoye crying from two rooms away. Peyam and Kal were still near the kitchen over the dregs of their shared pipe. Voices kept going. The rain was no longer underneath any of it.

She stood with her hands in the basin and listened into the gap it had left. The compound was full of its usual evening sounds, but behind all of them — where the monsoon had been for nineteen days — nothing. A silence so large it had shape.

She dried her hands on her skirt and walked into the prayer courtyard.

The stones were wet but the sky was not falling on them. She looked up and saw a break in the clouds to the west, a strip of dark sky that was actually dark — not the heavy grey-brown of monsoon cover but the real dark of open sky at night. She couldn't see stars yet. But the clouds were thinning, and the air against her face had changed. Lighter. Drier. Like someone had lifted a lid off the compound that she hadn't realized was there.

She sat on the low wall that edged the courtyard and let her hands rest on the wet stone. Cold through her palms. She'd forgotten what cold stone felt like without fresh water running over it.

From here she could hear the flood below, but it sounded different tonight — lower, steadier, more like a river and less like a catastrophe. Or maybe that was hope doing the hearing. She didn't trust hope. Hope was the voice that said he went ahead, he's waiting for you in Gelden, just get there and everything will make sense again. Hope was a three-month-old lie she'd told so many times it had almost calcified into fact.

She pressed her palms flat against the stone and let the cold climb into her wrists, her forearms. Nineteen days. She'd managed the Mera family through nineteen days in this place — Deshi's complaints, Kael's restlessness, Asha's unsettling new gestures, Peyam's quiet decline into exhaustion, Rimi's too-watchful silence. She'd managed the sleeping rotation and the meal rotation and the latrine schedule and the naming and every moment of every day when someone needed something decided. She'd managed all of it with a gap at the center of her family that she'd papered over with competence.

Her husband had not gone ahead. Her husband had left.

She knew this. She'd known it when she stood at the first naming and told Fasa that he was traveling separately, that he'd gone ahead to arrange housing at their destination. She'd known it when Deshi asked, on day three, when he might arrive. She'd known it when Nessa asked, gently, where he was.

She'd known it when she woke at two in the morning on a mat in a stone room with her children around her and felt the specific vertigo of a woman repeating the same answer to herself even after it has gone false, because if she stops the children will hear the silence where their father used to be.

Three months. He'd been gone for three months before the flood. He'd left on a Tuesday morning. She remembered that because Rimi had a school exam and he'd promised to walk her. He didn't walk her. He didn't leave a note. He took his second pair of shoes and his work bag and the framed photo of his mother, and that was how she knew it wasn't a work trip. Nobody takes their mother's photograph to work.

She sat in the courtyard and pressed her hands against the stone and let herself think about it properly for the first time in nineteen days. Not the organizational question of how to manage without him, which she'd been managing since before he left — he'd been absent in practice for months before he was absent in fact. The other question. The one under the management. The one that made her hands still and her breathing shallow whenever she came close to it.

Why wasn't she enough.

The thought arrived whole, like a piece of glass surfacing in wash water. Clean-edged and specific. Not why did he leave, which had explanations — his restlessness, his debts, his mother's death, his younger face in the window of another woman's house that one afternoon when Suna was carrying Asha home from the market and didn't let herself look twice. Not even what did I do wrong, which was a question that assumed she'd had the power to prevent it. But the barer thing: why wasn't what she was — capable, present, running their lives, raising his children — why wasn't that enough to make someone stay.

She heard the footsteps before she saw who was making them. Someone coming through the narrow passage from the main hall, walking carefully — the stones were slick — and then stopping at the edge of the courtyard as if uncertain whether to enter.

Jin Parra stood there with his arms folded against the evening chill, his thin frame almost lost in a borrowed jacket that was too wide across the shoulders. He looked at her and she saw the hesitation she'd learned to recognize — the jaw working before speech, the fractional pause that had become as familiar to everyone in the compound as the bell. He was holding a tin cup with both hands, the way Melie held things, as if each object deserved his full grip.

"I can — " He stopped. His teeth closed on the consonant. He looked down at the cup, then at her, then at the wet stones between them. "I can go."

"You don't need to," she said.

He didn't sit down. He stood at the edge of the courtyard, two body-lengths from the wall where Suna sat, and he looked up at the sky with an expression she couldn't read. Or maybe she could read it but didn't want to name it, because naming other people's feelings was a way of managing them and she was tired of managing.

"It stopped," he said.

"I know."

"Melie asked if that meant we could leave." His voice caught on the l. "I didn't know what to tell her."

His hands tightened on the tin cup. His thumbnails were bitten short, the skin around them raw — a habit she'd noticed in the first week but hadn't thought about again until now. He was shivering slightly. The evening wasn't cold enough for shivering. Something else was doing it.

"Sit down," she said. It came out the way she said things to her children: not a request, not a command, just the next practical step.

He sat on the wall, leaving the space of a person between them. He set the cup on the stone between his feet — empty, she saw now, or nearly. He'd brought it as something to hold.

For a while neither of them spoke. The compound behind them was settling. She could hear Deshi's voice from the sleeping section, the particular rhythm of her evening instructions — fold this, move that, where did Asha put her sandals. From the other direction, Bram's voice, low and indistinct, probably talking to Nessa. A moth circled the oil lamp in the passage they'd both come through, its shadow large and erratic against the stone wall.

"I watched Gitta," Jin said.

Suna waited.

"I watched her stand up and tell them about Fen. Sixteen years." He rubbed his thumb against the rim of the cup. "She didn't ask anyone to forgive her. She didn't explain why. She just said what happened."

"I know," Suna said. "I was there."

"And then Fen came back." His voice went quiet. Not quieter — she'd never heard Jin speak above a conversational volume — but the quality of the quiet changed, became something he was working to maintain. "He came back and sat next to her and didn't say anything and that was — I haven't been able to stop thinking about that."

"About Fen coming back?"

"About what she must have felt. All those years. Every time someone said he looks like his grandfather or he has your husband's hands. Sixteen years of people telling her a lie she invented and having to just — " He made a sound that wasn't a word. His jaw locked and released. "I told them Melie's mother was dead."

The sentence landed in the courtyard between them. Suna's body registered it before her mind did — her hands pressed flat against the stone, fingers spreading, and the cold of the wet surface shot up through her wrists and held her there. She didn't move. She didn't speak. She could feel the stone's grit under her fingertips, each grain of it distinct.

"She's not dead," Jin said. The words came faster now, as if he'd pulled a plug and the pressure behind it was doing the work. "She's alive. She's alive and she left. She left when Melie was four. Just — left. Packed a bag while I was at work and Melie was at the neighbor's and she was gone when I got home. She left a note on the table next to Melie's juice cup, just a note, not even a long one, just I can't do this anymore and I'm sorry and don't look for me."

Suna didn't speak. The wall took the force of his grip and passed it into the stone under her palms, a faint shudder running through the cold. His hands had abandoned the cup for the wall's edge; the skin around his thumbnails had gone white.

"I told Melie she died. I told her mama got sick and died. Because what was I supposed to — she was four. How do you tell a four-year-old that her mother looked at her and at me and at the house and the dishes in the sink and the juice cup on the table and decided — that she didn't — " His voice broke. Not dramatically, not with a sob. It broke the way a stick breaks, a clean snap, and then he was breathing hard through his nose and staring at the wet stone between his feet.

The moth's shadow moved on the wall behind them. The flood murmured below. Somewhere in the compound a child laughed — one of the Kondo girls, maybe Ori — and the sound was so ordinary and so distant that it felt like it belonged to a different place entirely.

"And then we came here," Jin said. His voice had steadied, but it was thin, scraped out. "And they started asking. And I said the same thing. She died. She got sick and she died. And every day they asked more questions and every day the lie got — I could feel it getting thinner, like cloth you've washed too many times, and then Fasa, in the naming, she asked when and where and how and I couldn't — " He swallowed. "The dates didn't work. What I'd been saying didn't hold because I'm not — I'm not good at this. I can't maintain it. Gitta did it for sixteen years and I can't do three months."

Suna sat very still beside him. Her hands were flat on the stone and the cold was in her wrists and the silence where the rain used to be was enormous around them. Beside her, his breath snagged and leveled and snagged again, and she felt something rise in her chest that she hadn't felt in nineteen days. Or three months. Or longer.

She recognized it. She'd been pushing it down every morning when she made the children's beds and organized the day and answered Deshi's questions and deflected Nessa's kindness and stood at the naming and spoke her family's name and composition with a voice so steady it convinced everyone including herself.

"He didn't go ahead," Suna said.

Jin turned to look at her. His face was wet and he wasn't wiping it.

"My husband." She heard her own voice, steady, factual, the voice she used for household logistics and naming declarations. "He didn't go ahead to arrange things. He left. Three months before the flood. He left."

She waited for the vertigo. It came — a dropping sensation behind her ribs, the feeling of saying aloud something she'd kept inside so long it had grown into her, become load-bearing, part of the structure she used to hold herself upright. But the vertigo was smaller than she'd expected. Or maybe she'd been falling for three months and just hadn't looked down.

"He took his mother's photograph and his second pair of shoes and he left on a Tuesday morning. Rimi had a school exam. He'd promised to walk her."

Jin was looking at her. He'd stopped gripping the wall. His hands were in his lap, open, empty.

"I told Deshi he went ahead. I told the children. I told Nessa. I told the naming." She heard the list forming, the cataloguing that was her own particular refuge — numbering the people she'd deceived as if they were tasks she could complete and check off and put behind her. "I don't know if Deshi believes it. I think she knows. I think she knew before I did. She doesn't ask about him. She asks about everything — where are Asha's sandals, why is the rice burned, did I comb Kael's hair this morning — but she doesn't ask about him. That's how I know she knows."

"Suna."

"Don't," she said, and the word was sharper than she intended. "Don't say you're sorry. I can't do anything with that right now."

He nodded. They sat in the silence together.

The clouds had thinned further. Above the western edge of the compound wall, three stars were visible — faint, tentative, as if they weren't sure the sky would hold. Suna couldn't remember the last time she'd seen stars. Nineteen days of cloud cover, and before that she hadn't been looking up. Before that she'd been packing what the children could carry and selling what they couldn't and telling Rimi they were going on an adventure and telling Kael his father would be there when they arrived and telling Asha nothing because Asha was four and absorbed whatever atmosphere surrounded her, which lately had been the atmosphere of a household quietly dissolving.

"How old is Melie?" she asked, and the question surprised her as it left her mouth. She knew how old Melie was. She'd watched her play with Asha and Kael every day for nearly three weeks.

"Six," Jin said. "She was four when — " He didn't finish.

"Asha was nearly four when he left." Suna looked at the three stars. "She'll remember pieces. His sandals by the door. The sound of him clearing his throat in the mornings. Not him, not the way Kael will. Rimi remembers everything."

"Does Rimi know?"

"I don't know." The admission cost her something — the first time she'd said it to anyone. "She hasn't asked. She's twelve. She's old enough to know that not asking is its own kind of answer."

Another star appeared. Then two more. The sky was clearing from the west, the monsoon cover pulling apart, and the exposed sky behind it was deep and scattered with light. Suna stared at it. The beauty felt almost aggressive after nineteen days of grey — intrusive, like a guest arriving too early and too cheerfully to a house still in mourning.

"I kept thinking about Gitta too," she said. "Sixteen years. Mine is three months, and it already sits in my mouth all day. I wake up and before I think about breakfast or Deshi's mood or the naming, I'm lining up the same answers. He went ahead to Gelden. He's arranging things. He'll be there when we arrive. By morning my jaw hurts from holding it in place. And I'm tired."

The last word left her throat scraped raw. Beside her, Jin kept looking at the stones between his feet.

Jin reached for his empty cup and held it. The metal whispered under his thumb as he turned it, slow and steady, the sound carrying in the quiet between them.

"Melie knows something's wrong," he said. "She doesn't ask about her mother anymore. She used to ask. Every day, for months. When's mama coming back, where did mama go, is mama in heaven. I said yes, heaven, and she stopped asking. But she didn't stop knowing." He pressed his thumb against the cup's rim. "Now when I hand her a bowl, she looks at my face before she looks at the food. At night she hooks her fist in my shirt and won't let go even asleep. The allergy thing — when Bett saved her — she wasn't watching Bett. She was watching me. My face scared her worse than her throat did."

"You're keeping her safe," Suna said.

"I'm lying to her every day."

Suna had nothing to offer that. She sat with it. The stone wall under her was losing its chill, warming toward the dry roughness she remembered from daylight hours, from afternoons when the rain paused and the stone absorbed the sun's heat and held it.

From the main hall, the custodians' bell rang once — not the insistent triple peal of a naming summons but the single tone that marked the end of communal hours. Settle in. Lamps out within the hour. The sound was so familiar now that Suna's body responded before her mind did: she straightened, began the mental list. Check that Asha is on her mat, that Kael's sandals are by the door, that Peyam has water for the night, that —

She stopped the list. Let it go. The children were with Deshi. They would be managed. She could sit here for a few more minutes without the Mera family collapsing.

"Gitta didn't ask anyone's permission," Jin said. "She didn't wait for Fasa to drag it out of her. She didn't let the naming break her open. She chose."

"She chose," Suna agreed.

More stars came through by the minute. The break in the clouds had spread across half the western sky, and the stars no longer looked tentative; they burned with the blunt, ordinary confidence of things that had been there all along, hidden only by weather. The sheen was draining off the flagstones.

"I'm going to tell the naming he left," she said.

She hadn't known she was going to say it until she said it. Or maybe she had. Maybe she'd known since Gitta stood up. Maybe she'd known since Nessa told her about Efe and Suna realized that everyone in this compound was carrying something dead they wouldn't name.

"This isn't for them," she said. "Fasa can make of it what she wants. Olet can keep saying what he likes about the road." She looked at the stars, not at Jin. "It's for the next time Kael asks when his father gets here. It's for Deshi's face when I say his name in that room. I can't keep putting Sev one town ahead of us when I still see the shelf where his mother's photograph used to be and the doorway with his second pair of shoes gone."

Jin didn't say anything for a long time. The hush in the courtyard kept widening; even the flood sounded farther away, and above them the gap in the clouds kept opening.

"What will you say?" he asked.

"That he left. That he's not waiting for us. That the Mera family is five people, not six." The wall's rough edge bit into her palm. Jin turned toward her, the cup forgotten between his shoes. "And Deshi will close her eyes and Rimi will look at me and Kael will ask where papa went and Asha won't understand and it'll be in the room where everybody can hear it, not just inside my own head."

"And then everyone will know."

"Everyone already suspects. I'm the worst liar in this compound. I just — I thought being competent was the same thing as being convincing." Air left her in a short hard burst. Her shoulders hitched once. "It's not."

Jin set the cup on the stone. He looked at her and his face in the starlight was the face of a man who had just spoken more in twenty minutes than he had in nineteen days. The exhaustion of it was visible — in his posture, in the way his hands lay open and spent on his knees, in the dark eyes that were no longer guarding anything.

"I can't," he said. "Not yet. I can't tell her. Not in the naming, not with everyone watching. She's six."

"I know."

"If I tell her that her mother chose to leave — if I take away died and give her left — she'll start hunting through the day for the reason. She'll ask if it was because I left her at the neighbor's. She'll ask if she cried too much, or asked too much, or held on too hard. She'll make it hers. She still sleeps with her hand twisted in my shirt. If I give her left, she'll think she let go."

"I know," Suna said again. "I have three of them."

He tipped his face to the stars. Beside her, his breath came out in one long unsteady stream, then caught hard on the next inhale.

"Tell her before the naming," Suna said.

He looked at her.

"Tell her yourself. Before they make you. Because they will — Fasa, or the naming, or the road opening, or Melie growing old enough to put it together on her own. Sit with her on her mat. Put water in her hand if she needs something to hold. Say it plain. Her mother left. Then stay there and answer whatever comes next. Don't drag her into that room with it still half-wrapped. Don't make the naming do your work for you."

His jaw worked. The catch, the lock, the release. He kept looking at the stone between his shoes.

"I don't know how," he said.

"You just told me. You found the words for me. You can find them for her."

"That's different. You're not six."

"She's not four anymore either. You said it yourself — she's stopped asking. That doesn't mean she believes you. It means the answer you've given her hurts. It'll hurt worse if the naming rips it open in front of everybody."

He put his face in his hands. His shoulders curved in, the jacket bunching around his neck, and he sat like that — bent, folded, his breathing loud in the quiet courtyard. Suna sat beside him and did not touch him and did not speak and did not manage. The naming room kept rising anyway: Deshi with her eyes closed, Rimi looking straight at her, Kael lifting his chin to ask where papa went.

The last dampness had gone out of the courtyard. Heat breathed up from the flagstones, and the sky above was thick with stars she hadn't seen in nineteen days. The flood below had dropped into the background, stripped for a moment of catastrophe and reduced to water moving in the dark.

"If the road opens," Jin said from behind his hands.

"When it opens."

"When it opens." He lowered his hands. His face was blotched, his eyes red, and he looked his age — twenty-nine, which was too young for this, though Suna wasn't sure what age would be old enough. "Where are you going?"

She hadn't thought about it. She'd been so focused on the daily management of being here that she'd stopped thinking about a destination, a city she'd been traveling toward, housing she'd been promised by a cousin who might or might not still be expecting them. Her husband was supposed to be there. He wasn't there. She didn't know where he was.

"Forward," she said. "The same direction. Just without pretending Sev will be in Gelden when we get there."

They sat together without touching, both of them facing the open square of sky. They were two parents on a stone wall in a shrine courtyard on a hillside above a flood, each with a child asleep one wall away and tomorrow's naming already pressing at the edge of the dark.

Suna listened to the place the rain had vacated. The silence carried no message. It was only the clean gap left when water stopped striking stone. Weather had simply stopped.

But the stars were real, and the air was drying, and somewhere in the sleeping quarters her children were on their mats with Deshi folding blankets over them, and tomorrow she would stand in the naming and say it aloud and the room would hear it and Deshi would close her eyes and Rimi would look straight at her and Kael would ask his question in that clear carrying voice of his.

She could force the words out. She couldn't stop what came after.

"Jin," she said.

He looked at her.

"It's not your fault either."

He opened his mouth and closed it. The jaw-lock held, then released into nothing. He looked at her. She didn't nod or look away.

Then he picked up his empty cup and stood. He was unsteady for a moment — the wall was low and his legs had stiffened — and she almost reached out to steady him but didn't. He found his balance.

"Thank you," he said. The hesitation on the th, the slight elongation, and then the rest coming out clean. "For listening. And for — " He stopped. Shook his head. "Thank you."

"Go check on Melie."

He nodded and walked back through the passage, his footsteps careful on the drying stone, the borrowed jacket too wide across his shoulders. The sound of him faded past the lamp and into the sleeping quarters.

Suna sat alone in the courtyard. She tilted her head back and looked at the sky, which was deep and dark and alive with more light than she could count. The compound was almost silent now. One oil lamp still burned in the passage. The moth was gone. She could hear, very faintly, the sound of someone humming — Mava, maybe, one of her old songs, the melody drifting from the Kondo sleeping section. Or maybe it was Kiri Tesh, humming the lullaby that had come from somewhere none of them could trace. She couldn't tell from here. It was just a thread of sound in the quiet.

She stood up from the wall, and it left its warmth along the backs of her thighs. In nineteen days the compound had printed itself into her body — rough stone, narrow passages, the give of a mat on the floor — a place she hadn't chosen and couldn't leave and had somehow come to know by feel.

Tomorrow she would stand in the naming room and say that Sev left on a Tuesday with his work bag, his second pair of shoes, and his mother's photograph. Deshi would close her eyes. Rimi would look straight at her. Kael would ask where papa went in the voice that carried farther than it should. Tonight she would check on her children and lie on her mat listening to Deshi breathe on the other side of the partition, listening for Kael to turn over, for Rimi to stay awake longer than she admitted, and wait for morning without rehearsing the old answer.

She walked back through the passage toward the sleeping quarters. The lamp flame bent as she passed. Behind her, the prayer courtyard lay open to the sky — stone and starlight and the low murmur of the flood below. Ahead, behind the thin partition, her family was sleeping through the last night she would answer Kael as if Sev were waiting in Gelden.


# Chapter 17

The quiet woke her. Not the usual rain noise — not the drip from the overhang or the soft hiss of water against stone — but an absence clean enough to make Leni sit up. She went to the sleeping quarter's narrow window and found the stone sill dry under her hand, warm with actual sun-stored heat where for weeks there had been only the damp cool of perpetual wet. When she turned, her father's mat was already empty, folded at the corner.

Ada was asleep on Nessa's chest, breathing in the tiny percussive rhythm that had become the Okoye section's metronome. Tomas was curled on his side, one arm thrown over his face. Nothing unusual. But the air was different — thinner somehow, or clearer, and the compound sounded like someone had lifted a blanket off the world.

The rain had stopped.

She'd known this was coming. Last night the dripping from the overhang had slowed and then quit, and she'd lain there listening to the new quiet push into the spaces where water sounds had lived for nineteen days. But sleep had softened the strangeness, and now the absence hit her freshly. She could hear a bird. She hadn't heard a bird in almost three weeks. The note was small and repetitive and impossibly clear, sharp enough to make the whole compound sound newly uncovered.

Through the window she could see the sky — not the low grey compress of the monsoon but actual sky, a pale washed blue already warm at the edges where the sun was climbing.

Her father was in the courtyard. She could see him through the doorway, standing near the cistern with his hands in his pockets and his head tilted back, looking upward with an expression she couldn't read. He looked thinner than when they'd arrived. They all did. Nearly three weeks of custodian rations and communal cooking had sharpened every adult's face into something closer to the bone. But on her father it read as drawn rather than lean; whatever he'd lost looked taken from the inside first.

She bit a hangnail and went to find breakfast.

The kitchen was already busy. Deshi was stirring something in the big pot with the kind of aggressive competence that dared anyone to offer help. Hala was laying out flatbread on the stone shelf, and two of the Kondo women — Tila and Habel — were sorting the last of the dried lentils, picking out small stones with practiced fingers. Tila wore a cotton headscarf she'd rewashed so many times it had gone transparent at the temples, and she hummed something tuneless and private as she worked. The cooking smoke was the same — that pervasive wood-char smell that had seeped into every garment and every mat — but without the rain's mineral undertone, it smelled sharper, almost acrid.

Leni took a piece of flatbread and leaned against the stone wall and chewed and watched. She was good at watching. She'd always been good at it, but nearly three weeks in the compound had turned the habit into a survival reflex; she clocked lies now by who kept their hands busy and who stared too hard at the floor. She saw things. She couldn't stop.

Like: Suna was sitting cross-legged near the kitchen edge, braiding Asha's hair, and her hands were steady but her face wasn't. She had the over-braced look of somebody who'd made up her mind in the dark — or last night, after the evening meal, when she and Jin had been sitting together in the prayer courtyard for what must have been an hour. Leni had seen them from the sleeping quarters' doorway, two shapes on the stone bench with their shoulders almost but not quite touching, speaking in voices too low to carry. She hadn't tried to listen. She'd been doing too much of that already.

The bell rang.

Not the naming bell — a shorter, sharper pattern that Olet used for general assembly. The sound cut through the new quiet with a metallic authority that made Leni's teeth ache. Around the kitchen, hands paused. Deshi's spoon hovered over the pot. Habel looked up from her lentils.

They gathered in the main hall, all of them, the way they always did when the bell demanded it — families clustering together by reflex, the children herded to the front, the custodians standing near the hall's open side where the valley spread below. And Leni saw it then, what the clear sky had been trying to tell her all morning: the water was lower. Not dramatically, not a sudden biblical recession, but the brown surface that had swallowed the road and the valley floor for nearly three weeks had pulled back. She could see the first switchback. Mud-slicked and littered with debris, but visible — actual road surface, grey under the new sun.

Olet stood with his hands clasped at his waist, unhurried as always. The news sat on him no heavier than a change in the meal rotation. Fasa was behind him, and Leni thought she looked tired. Not sleepy — depleted. Whatever private reckoning the custodians had been conducting in their quarters, it had left Fasa wrung out.

"The radio received a broadcast this morning," Olet said. His voice was the same: level, measured, the institutional calm that had unsettled Leni since the first day. "Road crews are clearing the route from the lowlands. The pass should be open within two days."

The room inhaled. Not a gasp — the dry, collective pull of breath people make when hope barges in before they're ready to trust it, reshaping posture and expression as it went. Leni saw her mother pull Ada closer. She saw Venn straighten, his broad shoulders squaring, his chin lifting with the specific relief of a man who had been enduring something beneath him. She saw Mava's hand go to her ear, the old circular rub, and then drop.
